And it is as if you take a cannula of bronze, or iron, or of a vulture's feather, and place it on the corn or whitlow. Then pour a small quantity of hot water into the cannula; hold your hand and rotate it with the pressure of your hand for a short time, so that the edge of the cannula is impressed on the root of the corn, and the hot water finds its way to the depth, to the root of the corn. And let the sick person wait a while on the soaking from the hot water for an hour.
Then dismiss him, for the corn is rooted out with its roots.
Do this to them one by one, until you have dealt with all of them on the body.
Then treat them after they are rooted out, with that which generates flesh from ointments.
Concerning the method of cauterization in Tremor.
When tremor occurs in someone from cold in a nerve, or from a quartan fever, or otherwise, then it is necessary that you cauterize it with four cauteries, or five, above the vertebrae of the back, and between each vertebra with a cautery, and on his chest with a cautery, and on his stomach with a cautery.
For tremor subsides with the cautery of olive oil, and the cure of the cold sickness hastens.
Concerning the method of cauterizing Albathir.
Chapter LVII.
Sometimes foul things, that is, pustules, are expelled in the body from corrupted gross matter.
Therefore, when they appear, it is necessary first that every pustule on the head be cauterized with a fine cautery made of myrtle wood, whose tip is heated with fire. Or with the root of long aristolochia. Or with a lenticular cautery.
And sometimes they are cauterized at the beginning of their expulsion in this way, and they do not increase; and the superfluity causing them is broken, and they heal from them.
However, it is necessary that this be done after evacuation with phlebotomy.
Concerning the method of cauterizing in the flow of blood from an artery in a wound.
Often there is a flow of blood from an artery that has been cut in a wound, which happens externally, or in an incision of an abscess, or a cauterization of a limb, and similar things: and it is difficult to stop it.
Therefore, when this happens to someone, then quickly apply your index finger to the opening of the artery, and press it firmly until the blood is stopped under your finger, and nothing escapes from it.
Then put small olive-shaped cauteries, and many large ones, in the fire: and blow on them until they become very hot.
Then take one of them, small or large, according to the wound and the place where the artery is broken, and place the cautery on the vein, after you quickly remove your finger, and hold the cautery until the blood is stopped.
If it escapes when you lift your finger from the artery's opening, and you extinguish the cautery, then quickly take another cautery from the cauteries prepared in the fire.
Therefore do not cease to do this with one after another until the blood is stopped.
And be careful not to burn the nerve that is there, and that another harm comes upon the sick person.
And know that when blood flows from the artery, it is not possible to stop it, especially when the artery is large, unless by one of four methods. Or with a cautery, as we said. Or with extraction, when it is not extracted. For when it is extracted, its extremities contract, and the blood is cut off. Or it is tied with a strong ligature. Or medicines of a nature to cut off the blood are placed upon it, and it is bound with a suitable constricting pad.
However, whoever administers its excision with a ligature, and constriction with a cloth, or application of burning substances, and similar things, does not truly benefit it, except rarely.
If, however, this happens to someone, and a physician is not present for them, nor medicine: then they should begin with the position of the index finger over the opening of the vein itself, as we have narrated, and its constriction strongly, until the blood is obstructed from the upper part of the wound, and over the artery. And the finger is not to be removed, from above it with very cold water, constantly, until the blood is congealed and thickened, and is cut off.
And meanwhile let him consider what it needs from cauterization, or from medicine.
Concerning incision, perforation, and phlebotomy in wounds, and similar matters.
We have already stated in the First Chapter, every ailment in which it is fitting to use cauterization with fire and burning medicine, and the occasions for it, its causes, and its instruments, and the forms of cauteries.
And I placed there chapters from head to foot.
And I shall proceed in this chapter by the same path, so that the seeker may have his question alleviated.
And before I begin that, it is necessary for you, my children, to know that in this chapter there is the terror which is in the first chapter on cauterization.
And because of that, it is necessary that there be greater caution in it, since in the operation which is in this chapter, often evacuation occurs with blood, with which life stands at the opening of a vein, or the incision over an abscess, or the opening of an exit, or the healing of a wound, or the extraction of an arrow, or the incision over a stone, and similar to these: from all of which trembling and fear are associated, and death occurs in many of them.
And I warn you, lest that which causes hesitation fall upon you, for sometimes the ways of men with the ways of illnesses befall you in this art. For among them is he who is angry with his illness, and death is slight to him; because of the intensity he finds in his illness and its length of malice. And with illness is fear that signifies death.
And among them is he who gives his tithe and enriches you with it, hoping for health, and his illness is harmful.
Therefore, you should not agree with him who comes to you from among them, whose method is such. And let your caution be more intense than your greed and your rapacity. And do not presume upon anything of it unless after certain knowledge, which verifies to you that to which praiseworthy succession has arrived.
And use in the treatment of all your sick people, prognosis and indication of that to which health befittingly belongs.
For in that is help for you concerning the acquisition of fame and glory, and of memory and praise.
May God, O children, inspire you with his understanding, and may he not remove from you direction and inspiration; for it is in his hand.
And I have indeed arranged this Chapter by distinguishing it into chapters, just as preceded in the Chapter on Cautery, from the neck to the feet: so that your seeking of it may be alleviated, and what you need.
Concerning the treatment of Water which gathers in the heads of children.
Chapter 1.
Know that this ailment frequently happens to children at birth, or when the midwife tightens the child's head without ease.
And sometimes it also occurs from a hidden cause that is not known.
And I myself have not seen this illness in any but children, and death has approached all whom I saw afflicted by it; therefore, it seemed to me to omit treatment for it.
And I myself have seen one child among them whose head was filled with water, and the head grew larger each day, until it could not sit upon itself, due to the magnitude of its head, and the moisture increased until he died.
And this moisture, indeed, either collects between the skin and the bone, or collects under the bone above the surface.
And if the moisture is between the skin and the bone, and the abscess is small, then one should make a single fissure in the middle of the head, in width, and the fissure's length should be approximately two digits, until the moisture flows out.
And this is the form of a lancet.
If, therefore, the moisture is more increased, and the abscess is larger, then place two fissures, according to this form.
And if the moisture is under the bone, and the sign of it is that you see the sutures of the head open on all sides, and water is pressed out when you press it with your hand towards the interior, and that is not hidden from you, then you should make three fissures in the middle of the head, according to this method.
And after the incision, extract all the moisture. Then bind the fissures with cloths and bandages. Then apply a poultice of wine and oil to it until the fifth day.
Then loosen the bandage, and treat the wound with splints and ointments.
And do not allow the head to be bound with equality. And feed the infirm person with all dry foods of little moisture, until the limb is strengthened and healed.
And another method of incision is to consider where the size of the abscess and the collection of water appear.
Because sometimes there is more in the occiput, or in the forehead, or in the right, or in the left.
Therefore, ascend with the incision where the abscess and collection of water appear to you: and cut it according to what is possible for you.
And observe, lest you cut an artery, and a flow of blood occur, and the infirm person die from that flow with the evacuation of moisture.
Concerning the incision of arteries which are behind the ears.
Chapter II.
When cataracts occur to someone in the eyes or the chest, and it becomes chronic, and the treatment of medicines does not benefit: then a more ultimate treatment is the incision of these two arteries.
Therefore, when you wish to incise them, it is necessary that the infirm person shave his head. Then rub the area with a rough cloth, so that the arteries appear. Then let the infirm person bind his neck with any cloth of his. Then consider where the vein beats. And the places of both are the places which are depressed behind the ears: and it is rarely hidden, except in some men.
Then mark with ink above each: and incise each with a scalpel on both sides, the incision reaching the bone: and let it be done across the width of the head.
And if you wish, insert the scalpel under the artery and cut it upward with the incision.
And the length of the incision be like two joined fingers.
For when a vein is cut, blood comes out with a pulsating flow, leaping to the interior with frequent jumps.
If however the artery does not appear to the senses, then it is necessary to measure from the ear a space of three fingers' breadth. Then mark with ink, and split until the mouth.
And that which it is necessary to discharge from the blood is VI ounces, and perhaps you discharge from it more or less. All of it according to the quantity that appears to you from the weakness of the infirm and the filling of his artery.
Then consider the wound. Then if anything remains at the mouth from the sifac, then cut it, lest a hot abscess occur: then bind the wound with linen bandages.
Then treat it with ointment until it is healed.
Concerning the extraction of arteries, which are in the temples.
Chapter III.
When a man suffers from chronic hemicrania, or acute catarrhs because of acrid humours, and heat in the temporal muscles, or chronic, severe, and similar ailments: and is treated with the methods of medical care, and it does not help: then we are experienced in these illnesses in the extraction of arteries from the temples, or their cauterization, as we have described.
And the method of operation in extracting them is that the infirm person should shave the hair that is on the temples, and direct himself toward the artery appearing in the temple. For it indeed shows itself to you by its pulse, and is rarely hidden except in a few men, or by the intensity of cold.
If it is hidden from you, then let the infirm person tie his head with any cloth of his: then rub the place with a rough cloth. Or steam the place with hot water, until it appears to you by manifest appearance. Then take the spatula, which I formed a little while ago. Then scrape the skin with it easily, until you reach the artery. Then insert a hook into it, and pull it upwards until you pull it out of the skin, and make it escape from the tunics that are under it from every side.
If however the artery is fine: then wrap it with the end of the hook. Then cut a part from it according to the amount by which its ends will be elongated and contracted, and so that no flow of blood occurs. For if it is not cut, and if it is not severed, the blood does not ascend completely.
Then evacuate six ounces of blood, up to three.
If the artery is large, then it is necessary to tie it in two places with a strong double thread, of silk or of al-hohod cords, so that putrefaction does not hasten to it before the wound's consolidation, and a flow of blood occurs.
Then cut away the excess that is between the two ligatures. Do that at that hour, or after another hour.
And if you wish, cauterize it with a cautery up to the bone, with a knife cautery, until its extremities are cut, and it will stand in place of the same operation.
And it is a better operation, unless the sick person has a fever, or is of a hot temperament. Because cauterization is from among those things which help to end moistures. Therefore it is a better aid.
And it is necessary after the extraction of the arteries, that the place be filled with old cotton: and pads made properly should be placed on it.
And after the removal, treat with dry medicines that cause flesh to grow, and with bandages, until it is healed.
If, however, during the operation, a flow of blood from an artery occurs, then hasten to cauterize it, or to fill the place with zegi; and bind your hand until the blood stops. If none of those is present for you, then place your finger on the spot until the blood congeals. And embrocate the place with water of great cold, until the sharpness subsides, and bind it as necessary.
And since it is easier and less laborious to extract an artery, cauterize the vein with this cautery that has two blades; and the sharpness of the two blades should be similar to an almond. However, they are of much less sharpness than two blades. For if they are sharp, like two blades, the cold affects both quickly, and do not cut the flesh with speed. Therefore, since there is a slight thickness in them, the heat of the fire is held within them, and the flesh is cut quickly; with this, then, the work is better than any other work, and lighter, and easier.
And this is the form of cautery.
And let the space between the two blades be the thickness of a finger, after you mark two places with ink; and place both heated strongly, until they reach the bone, and cut the vein in two places, so that the distance between its extremities is extended. For they are not completely consolidated with this cautery.
Chapter IV.
Concerning the treatment of acute, persistent lacrimal discharge from the eyes.
When the tears are constant, and their flow is from the veins that are on the outer skull; and no medicinal treatment with medicines avails in them; and you see the face of the sick person already red, and he feels in his forehead a crawling sensation, like the crawling of ants; and his eyes are thinned, moist, so that their hairs are already corroded with corrosion; and his eyelids are inflamed from the acuteness of the tears; then the ancients needed this operation in their treatment of them, and they named that treatment, "with the Knife.
And it is, that you order the sick person to shave the hairs that are on his forehead. Then make three equally spaced incisions along the length of the forehead; and let the length of each incision be about two fingers; and let one incision be equidistant to the length of the nose in the middle of the forehead, and the second a little to the side of the temples' movement, and the third on the other side; and be cautious not to cut the arteries that are on either side; and keep your hand away from the line of the two jawbones, and let the distance of each incision be the amount of three joined fingers; and let you have a piece of prepared sponge, or many dried cloths, with which you dry the blood.
Then introduce a spatula with two sharp ends, whose form is as follows. From the incision that is on the side of the temple, up to the second incision; and scrape with it the entire skin that is between the two incisions, as if it were over a bone. Then do the same from the middle incision to the second incision. Then withdraw this spatula, and introduce into the first incision another instrument, called a sharp scalpel, with one side smooth, not sharp, on the other side. And this is its form.
And place its sharp side towards the flesh attached with the skin, and its smooth side towards the bone; and push it until it reaches the middle fissure; and cut with it all the vessels that descend from the head to the eyes; except that the incision continues up to that which appears of the skin.
Then do that in the middle fissure up to the other fissure.
And after an equal quantity of blood has run out, express the places from the clots of coagulated blood.
Then place in every fissure a linen cloth from old cotton, and place over them cushions soaked in wine, or oil, or vinegar. And see that a hot abscess does not occur.
And on the third day, loosen the bandage, and administer much infusion with lukewarm water. Then treat it with basilicon ointment, and the other remedies with which wounds are treated, until it is healed.
Chapter V.
Concerning the treatment of acute tears and catarrhs to the eyes from the interior of the head.
When acute, sharp, persistent catarrhs befall someone, and you see their eyes thinned, small, and their sight already weakened, and the eyelids ulcerated, and hairs fall from them, and there is a sharp, harmful pain deep within the head, and sneezing follows: then understand from these occurrences that these matters, and catarrhs, do not arise except from many veins.
And the better treatment for them is this. And it is that you should instruct the sick person to shave his forehead. Then make one incision in the middle of the forehead, or a little further along the width, and begin the incision from the left temple to the right temple.  Let the incision reach to the bone, and move your hand away from the temporal muscles, which move during chewing, until the bone is exposed, and you will dry all the blood with a sponge.
Then separate the two lips of the incision with old cloth, or with flax linen strips. Then bind them from above with pads, and soak the pads with wine and oil, so that a hot abscess does not occur.
And when you have removed it, and see that the hot abscess is already diminishing, then it is necessary to rub the bone, until flesh begins to grow on it.
Then treat it with a drying regimen that causes flesh to grow, such as if you take two parts of wheat flour and four parts of colophony, and prepare a plaster from them, and administer it for the growth of flesh in these wounds.
Chapter VI.
Concerning the treatment of that which falls into the Ear.
Everything that falls into the ear is one of four types: either a mineral stone, or something similar to a stone, such as iron and glass; or it is from a plant, such as chickpeas and beans; or it is a liquid substance, such as water, vinegar, and the like; or some animal.
Therefore, when a stone, or a kind of stone from among those which do not adhere nor grow in the ear, falls into the ear, expose the ear to the sun.  If you then see the stone, then instill into the ear some violet oil, or sesame oil. Then administer its extraction, and make them sneeze with the condiment, and close the nostrils at the advent of the sneeze, after you place around the ear pieces of cloth or wool, and extend the ear upward.
For often it exits with this treatment.
If, however, it does not exit, then administer its extraction with a gesti, that is, with slender pincers; whose shape is this.
If, therefore, it exits with the gesti, it is good.
And if not, then administer its extraction with a slender, single-pronged hook.
If, therefore, it does not exit through that, then make a tube of bronze, and insert the end of the tube into the ear hole deeply, and fill that which is around the tube with softened wax mixed with oil, so that there is no way for wind except through the tube; then extract it with your suction with strong pulling.
For many times it exits with that which we have narrated. If, however, it does not exit in this manner, then take a small amount of helkalimbat, or of viscus, that is, prepared glue, with which birds are caught, and place it at the end of the radius after you have wrapped a suitable piece of cloth around it; then insert it into the ear hole with ease, after you have dried the ear from moisture.
If, therefore, it does not exit with all that we have narrated, then hurry to incision, before a hot abscess or spasm occurs.
And the method of the incision is, that a weak phlebotomist first in the cephalic vein: and extract from it blood according to the measure of his strength. Then make the sick person sit between your hands, and turn his ear upwards, and make a small incision at the root of the ear near his fat in the depressed area: and let the incision be crescent-shaped, until you reach the stone; then remove it with whatever instruments are possible for you.
Then close the incision with your skill quickly, and treat it until it is healed.
If the thing falling into the ear is one of the grains that grow and swell, then administer its extraction.
If however it does not yield to you for exit, then take a thin, slender spatula, according to this form.
And administer with it the excision of that kind of grain that falls into the ear, and do not do it when you think that the grain is already moistened by the vapor of the ear, until you put it in many fragments; then extract them with hooks, or with a fine forceps, or with suction, as we said, for its extraction is alleviated.
However, for water that enters the ear, it is necessary for the weak person to use sneezing with a condiment at first; and already he has filled his ear with some old cotton; and he is lying on that ear in which there is water. If therefore it emerges through it, it is good.
And if not, then take many stones, according to the length of a finger, thin, smooth, and heat them with fire for a short time, and let the weak person insert one of them into the hole of his ear; and let him jump on one of his feet from that side, and strike with another stone the stone that is in the ear. Therefore, he shall not cease to do that with stone and stone until all the water emerges.
And now water emerges thus, so that you take one from papyrus, or from a reed, and insert one end of it into the ear; and you set fire to the other end with fire, until most of it is burned. Then again a different reed, and do that many times, until all the water emerges; and extract it with a cannula, according to that which was previously stated concerning the stone.
In the extraction of an animal that has entered it, if it is of small body, such as a flea, and similar to it, treat it as I said in the division.
But if it is of large body, which appears to the senses: then administer its extraction with forceps and hooks; and its removal is easier than all those which are held in the ear.
In the extraction of a worm generated in the ear, when you treat it with what we said in the division, and in the treatise on distillations, and your treatment does not help: then it is necessary that you examine the ear in the sun. If therefore anything of the worms appears there, then extract it with forceps, or with thin hooks.
If, however, nothing appears there, then take a cannula, which has this shape, narrow at the bottom, wide at the top: and insert the narrow end into the ear according to the amount that the patient tolerates; then suck with it a strong suction. Do that many times, until all the worms come out.
If, however, they do not yield to you for exit, then coat the circumference of the cannula with wax, as I said in the section on stones.
If they do not come out with what we have said, then administer the distillations which the ancients have already tested for killing worms: and you will find it in the treatise on distillations.
And let your pouring of oils and medicines into the ear be with this instrument, whose form is depicted here. As you see, make it from silver, or bronze, narrowed below, in which there is a small opening, wider above.
Let the part that presses, which is in the concavity of the cannula, be made from bronze properly. And if you wish, take a rod, and wrap cotton around its end with a proper wrapping; then pour oil, or juice, or whatever medicine you wish, into the cannula; and let the cannula be in the ear; then insert the rod with the cotton on top, and express it with your hand by a moderate pressure, until the oil is driven into the concavity of the hearing, and the patient feels it inside; and that which is poured into the ear, let it be warmed a little with fire; and beware that the distilled substance in it is not too cold; for medicines do not tolerate that.
Chapter VII.
Concerning the treatment of sudden ear blockage.
Sometimes infants emerge from their mothers' wombs unperforated.
And sometimes blockage of the ear canal occurs in some people from a wound or a growth of flesh in it.
And this blockage sometimes occurs deep within the ear opening, and is not visible; and sometimes it occurs near the outer part of the opening, and is visible.
Therefore, that which occurs deep within the ear, and which the sight does not encompass, is, in many cases, difficult to cure.
And in that which is visible, you should place the patient's ear in the sun, and look at it; then, if you see the blockage appearing, open it with a fine spatula, whose shape is as follows, that is, that one end has a small width, one part of which is pointed; and the rest of the spatula has two smooth sides, so that it does not harm the ear.
If, therefore, the blockage is from flesh grown in the ear canal, then grasp it with a fine hook, and cut it out with gentle force, until all the flesh is removed.
If the blockage is deep within the ear, then take a thin, smooth reed, and heat it slightly with fire; then insert it into the ear canal.
If, therefore, you feel the blockage with the patient's difficulty hearing, try to open it gently, and be careful not to injure the nerves of the ear.
Then place a bandage on the ear, according to the size of the opening you have made, wrapped in Egyptian ointment; do this for several days, until you are sure of the wound's healing.
Or take a bandage, and soak it in rosewater, and sprinkle powdered zegi on it, and use it.
If you see that the ear has already become abscessed, then it is necessary to remove the bandage, and apply another bandage wrapped in a cloth made with rose oil, until the hot abscess subsides; then treat it until it is healed.
If, however, bleeding occurs, then immerse a sponge or cloths in cold water, and place them on the ear; and use the remaining treatment, which stops bleeding, as described in the treatise on distillations.
Concerning the treatment of Altheli of the eyelids.
Chapter VIII.
These Altheli that occur in the eyelids are sometimes moist and sometimes dry.
Therefore, you should grasp them with tweezers or with a hook, and cut them with a spatula from their roots. Then place powdered zegi over the area.
If blood flows from them, then cauterize them with a fine lenticular cautery.
And to cauterize them is better after incision. For they often return when something remains from their roots. And when they are cauterized with fire, those roots are burned, and they do not return.
Concerning the treatment of granulations of the eyelids of the eye.
Chapter IX.
Often something occurs in the eyelids that resembles a granulation in its strength and hardness, and because of it is called a granulation: and it is a collection of thick moisture on the upper eyelid and the lower eyelid.
Therefore the operation on them is, that you should consider then, if it is a granulation proceeding from that which appears on the eyelid, and moves in every direction, and its removal is easy.
Therefore make a fissure over it along its width. Then scrape it from every side with ease until it is removed. Then suspend it with a hook, and cut it off.
However, if its removal is not prepared for you unless after you penetrate the eyelid with a small incision, then that harms the patient in some way.
If the fissure is large, then bring it together with stitches and treat it until it heals.
And if it is small, no harm will come to you from it. For ointment restores it and causes it to flesh over.
And if the granulation is sloping inwards towards the inner surface of the ear, then invert the eyelid, and suspend the granulation with a hook; moreover, you need an incision, and press it from every side.
If the eyelid is elongated by incision, that will not harm it in any way.
Then wash the eye after the incision of the granulation, with salt water; and treat the place with that which causes fleshing, until the sick person is healed.
Concerning the treatment of the eyelashes.
Chapter X.
Axirnath is a fat that is produced in the tissues of the upper eyelid; and it mostly occurs in children.  It weighs down their eyes, and they have catarrhs in the mornings, and cannot look at the sunlight, because tears rush to them, and on account of this you see them always lying on their faces, and sleeping on them, and their eyelids under their eyebrows are wet, which now the swelling has covered.  And indeed the prominent Axirnath is apparent to the eye, and when you press the area with your fingers, you feel Axirnath between them.
And the method of its treatment is that the sick person should place his head in your bosom.
Then take a linen cloth, and prepare a bandage on it, and make a circle from that bandage according to the amount that Axirnath occupies on all sides. Then place it over it, and press with your fingers on all sides, so that the Axirnath is gathered in the middle of the circle.
Then find in the middle of that moisture with a lancet, incisions along its width, and let the incision not be larger than that which is made in phlebotomy in the depths.
Or it is necessary to cut through the entire skin, until you reach the Axirnath, for in many ways Axirnath proceeds from the incision according to the shape of a white fat lump.
Therefore, pull it out with the cloth, which you have inserted between your fingers on the outside; and you will turn your hand to the right and left, until it is dispersed; and beware, lest you add to the incision, so that you do not reach the eye with the lancet and injure it.
If, however, Axirnath does not appear to you at the beginning of the incision, you should add a little to the incision with ease, until Axirnath proceeds. Then extract it, as we have said.
Then immerse the cloths in vinegar and water, and place them on the area, and bind it with bandages.
And some men grind salt and place it in the hollow of the incision, so that what remained of the moisture liquefies.
Then treat it until it is healed.
If however a hot abscess occurs in the place, then treat it with soothing plasters until it is healed.
Concerning the methods for Ataxmir of the eye, that is, when the eyelid of the eye is softened, whence it happens that it wrinkles improperly.
Chapter XI.
When additional hairs grow in the eyelids, beyond the natural hair course, and grow old, then they harm the eye, and cause conditions of illness, such as constant tears, and softening of the eyelids, and cloudiness, until it becomes the cause of the eye's destruction.
And Ataxmir of the eye is indeed cured according to four methods. Or by cautery with fire. Or with a sharp medicine, according to what has been stated in the chapter on cautery. Or Ataxmir is cured by incision and suture. Or by tubes, according to what I will say.
It is necessary that you place the head of the sick person in your bosom. Then turn the eyelid of the eye with your left hand.
If therefore it is turned, it is good. And if not: insert a needle into it, in which there is thread from the lower part of the eyelid, and make the needle penetrate with the thread from above, and let it be near the hairs themselves, that is, near the edge of the eyelid where the hairs grow; and pull the thread upwards with the eyelid, and turn it with a rod.
Then find in the inner part of the eyelid under the added hairs with a spatula sliding from the larger tear duct to the smaller tear duct.
Then pull out the thread, and place a small cushion of cotton, or cloth, under the eyelid; then mark the eyelid with ink, that it may be like the shape of a myrtle leaf.
However, it is necessary that it be shaped according to the size and elevation of the eyelid, since it varies in humans.
For among them is one who needs a large amount of eyelid to be cut, according to the amount softened by the eyelid.
Then, with a spatula, find the two lines you marked, and begin from the larger tear duct to the smaller tear duct, and let the fissure be close to the natural hairs, as is the thickness of a ray.
Then insert the hook into one of the two corners of the skin. Then scrape it all off. Then bring together the two edges with a needle and fine wool thread, and wipe away the blood, and make whatever excess thread remains adhere to the two eyebrows, with certain adhesive substances if you wish to do so; and if not, do not worry about it.
Then leave the suture and threads until three or four days; afterwards, treat it.
And if you wish, leave the wound without a suture and treat it with that which dries and contracts it. For the eyelid is raised by the sealing and bringing together of the wound; but it will be better.
The ancients taught this method of treatment.
However, there is care for the sick person in it, and it is from good operation, and there is no fear in it.
And another method is in Ataxmir also: and it is, that you mark on the eyelid a figure that is like the figure of a myrtle leaf, as we have recounted.
Then raise the eyelid with three separate hooks, or grouped together, according to this following form, then cut what is superfluous with small scissors, according to this form, with equal incision.
If however it is not possible for you to hold the hook, and it is not equal: then take a needle in which is a thread, and insert it into the middle of the figure; and insert another thread near the larger tear duct, and a second thread near the smaller tear duct; and gather the threads between your fingers with equality.
Then raise your hand with equal elevation, and raise the skin upon which the mark is made, as we have recounted.
Then bring together the two lips of the wound with sutures, and care for it until it heals.
If indeed a boil occurs at your incision, or your fissure: soothe that boil with cauterium, and similar things, from soothing ointments.
And sometimes it happens to the eyelid as well, that its hairs turn.
Therefore, it is necessary that you use what we said in the incision: but the suture is more artificial in both. Concerning the treatment of Ataxmir with canes.
And it is, that you turn the eyelid, and split the fissure which is within, according to what I have described. Then make two canes, or two thin pieces of wood, whose length is according to the length of the eyelid, and their width is less than the width of the spatula; and you have already incised at the ends of each on both sides where you hold the threads.
Then carefully apply each one to the excess of the eyelid, and bind the two canes firmly together on both sides, and leave it for a few days. For the tight skin will die, and darken, and putrefy, until it falls off by itself.
If it is delayed, then cut it with scissors. Then care for it until it is healed.
When it is consolidated, the eyelid is raised: and the hairs do not prick.
And this is the form of two canes, which you will make according to this figure, and quantity in length and breadth.
Chapter XII.
Concerning the raising of prickling hairs in the eye with a needle.
The operation in that is, that you should take a slender needle, and introduce into it a fine, smooth silk thread. Then tie its ends together, and make very small knots on each side: and let the length of the threads be one palm.
Then place another fine thread underneath it in Alinxotati, briefly, and tie its ends if you wish.
Then let the sick person place their head in your lap, and be near the sun so that the operation may be revealed; because it is a subtle or minute operation.
Then introduce the needle with alinxotati into the root of the eyelid, and make it penetrate the eyelid quickly, until you draw it out above the natural hair.
Then pull the hair upward through the alinxotati: and introduce that hair into the alinxotati: if it is one, or two, or three, not more.
Then pull your hand with it, until the hair emerges with the alinxotati into the eyelid with the natural hair.
However, if the alinxotati is pulled out, and the hair does not emerge with it: you shall pull the alinxotati downward, with the thread that you have placed within it, until the alinxotati emerges from the upper opening, and you see it with the eye's vision; for then you shall pull the alinxotati and the thread: and remove both, and close the eye: and leave it closed for two days or three, until the hair adheres to its place, and flesh grows over it.
If the hair is short, then either associate a long hair from the natural hairs with it, and raise it with it; or leave it until it lengthens after a time, and then raise it.
Chapter XIII.
When this Alxatrati happens to the eye, it is called Leporina.
And this Alxatrati is either natural or accidental.
Accidental, therefore, it arises from the consolidation of a wound, or incision, or cautery, and things similar to these.
And the method of treatment in it is that you find that consolidation, and that you separate its edges, and place on that which is between them a linen bandage, and tie it, until it is healed.
And it is not appropriate to use in its treatment things which dry and constrict: for if you do that, the Alxatrati returns worse than it was: rather, use in it things which soften, such as fenugreek; and embrocate with water in which marshmallow, flaxseed, have been cooked; and with the unguent dyaquilon, which has been liquefied with some of the oils; and wrap it with a bandage, and treat it with it.
And the treasure of your treatment is that you strive with all your ingenuity so that it does not consolidate according to the form which it was in the beginning.
And this operation, which we have described, is only a certain rectification of the Alxatrati, so that the shape of the place does not return to what it was entirely.
Concerning the treatment of Alxatrati which occurs in the lower eyelid, and is a wrinkling or contraction upward to the right and left.
Chapter XIV.
This Alxatrati, which occurs below, is the one truly called Alxatrati. And it is natural, and it is accidental.
And the accidental one results from a wound, or a fissure, or a cautery, and similar things.
And the method of operation in it is that you should take a needle, on which there is a double thread, and you should dip it into the flesh, and make it penetrate from the left tear duct to the right tear duct, until the thread reaches the two extremities of the flesh.
Then extend the flesh upwards with the needle, and cut it with a wide spatula. If therefore the shape of the eyelid returns as it should, it is good.
And if not, take a radius, and place it in the fissure, and turn the eyelid with it: and make two fissures on the inner side of the eyelid, and let the extremities of the two fissures be from the two angles of the incision, until they meet and form an angle from them: until when they are joined together the shape of them becomes this shape, and it is the Greek letter lambda.
Then remove that flesh, by the quantity that is its sharp lower side, from that which follows the eyelid. Then join the separated parts: sew it with a wool thread.
Then treat them with what we said, concerning softening medicines, and bandages, until they heal.
And if a laceration happens from a fissure, and suture, or cautery: then it is necessary that you make a flat fissure under the hairs of the eyelids, even according to what has preceded. Then separate between the two lips with a bandage, according to what we said.
And the summary of the treatment for Alxatra, when it is above or below, is that the operation should proceed according to the form of the Alxatra.
For indeed it has many varieties of form: and the skillful craftsman prepares an invention in whatever way possible for it, until he causes the figure to return to its natural form, or nearly so, and the infirm person himself, according to what suits him from the operation and instruments in many arrangements.
Chapter XV.
Concerning the adhesion of the eyelid to the conjunctiva or cornea.
Sometimes this consolidation happens in many people, so that the upper eyelid consolidates with the white of the eye and prevents it from its natural functions.
Therefore, you should insert the end of a probe under the eyelid and lift it upwards; or extend it with a hook.  Then cut the adhesion with a fine spatula, which should not be sharp, but somewhat blunt, so that the patient is not disturbed during the procedure, and the spatula is removed without injuring the eye with its sharpness. Your incision should be as if you were gently scraping away the adhesion until the eyelid returns to its natural form, and the entire adhesion is healed.
Then pour salty water into the eye, washing it with the water; or distill it into it from a sieve made from dissolved zinc with water.
Then separate the eyelid from the eye with linen strips; and place on the eye wool soaked in egg white; and after three days use a sieve, and with consolidants, until healed.
Concerning the incision of the toenail and the fleshy prominence of the tear ducts.
Chapter XVI.
Know that the toenail is according to two ways. Or it is nervous: and it is similar to hard, fine sinew. Or it is not nervous, similar to frozen white moisture: which when it touches iron, or you try to grasp it with a hook, it is severed, and the hook does not fix in it.
And both types do not begin, unless from the greater tear duct, until it gradually goes, until it covers the sight, and hinders light, and the movement of the eye.
And the method of operation in its incision is: that the weak person should place his head in your lap. Then open his eyes, and raise the eyelid with your hand. Then gather the nail with a hook of little duplication, and extend it upward. Then take a needle, and insert into it a hair from the hair of a horse, or an ox, or a cow: and let the thread be strong, and double the tip of the needle a little, and fix it in the middle of the nail: and pierce it with the needle, and tie the nail with the thread, and extend it upward: and scrape with the hair the side of the nail that follows the pupil, as if you were sawing it with the hair until its end.
Then cut the remainder at the root of the tear duct with a delicate spatula, or with small scissors: and leave the natural tear tissue, so that a continuous flow of tears does not occur from its incision.
And sometimes the nail is extended with a hook alone, or with a thread, as we said. Then it is scraped with a gentle spatula, whose form is this.
And beware that the spatula does not touch the corneal membrane, and a rupture occurs in it, and the pupil is pierced.
Then, after the incision, place some crushed salt, or a poultice made from ziniar, on the eye, and leave it until the next day. Then treat it with what is necessary, until it is healed.
If the nail is not nervous, and you cannot insert a needle into it, nor is a hook fixed in it, then this kind of nail is treated many times with remedies, and is healed as I have described in the division.
If you wish to cut it, then open the sick person's eye, and take a fine, gentle, weakly sharpened spatula, according to this form. And scrape the nail from above with precision. If you see that it becomes thinner and breaks, and the scraping impresses it, then distill from your hour glass into the eye a sief made from ziniar, or from red sief, or crushed salt: and bandage the eye until the next day.
Then repeat the operation on it, until the whole of it is removed. Unless there is a hot abscess in the eye; then leave it, and treat the abscess until it heals.
Then repeat the operation on it with scraping, until it heals.
However, if a fleshy prominence of the tear duct is causing great harm to the eye, suspend it with a hook and cut away part of it; and do not make the incision too deep, so that the tear ducts are not damaged.
Then instill into the tear duct a red, or ziniari-made, liquid, until it heals.
Concerning the incision of Alguardenic, and flesh grown in the eye.
Chapter XVII.
Sometimes in some people's eyes, red flesh grows, either compounded or superimposed, until it covers the pupil, or approaches it, or presses against the eyelids, and perhaps even turns the eyelids outward; and it resembles a rose hip.
And the treatment in it is also that the sick person should place his head in your lap. Then open his eyes, and collect all that flesh with suitable hooks: and hold it with one forceps, or a similar instrument.
Then cut into the flesh first, until all of it is finished with the scalpel. And be careful of the eye, so that you do not injure it during the operation; and let your incision, with which you cut it, be either with a spatula, such as we described in the incision of a nail, or with small shears, with which asebel is collected, according to the form it will take after this chapter.
When therefore your incision is complete, and the entire alguardenici is removed, then fill the eye with crushed salt; or distil into it a red powder, and similar medicines that corrode; and place over the eye, externally, a cloth with egg white; so that it is safeguarded from a hot abscess.
If anything of the alguardenici remains, and hot blood dominates you, and you fear a hot abscess, then leave the eye, and treat it with whatever soothes the abscess.
Then repeat the operation on it until it is healed.
And similarly do with the added flesh that occurs in the eye of this sort.
However, it is necessary that you avoid treating any weak eye by a natural or accidental route, by operating with iron, and do not insert your hand into any of these operations until you see that the member is tolerant of that operation.
Concerning the collection of Sebel from the eye.
Chapter XVIII.
Sebel are red veins that are woven over the eye, and hinder the eye's operation of seeing; and they weaken the eye with the length of days.
Therefore, it is necessary for you first to consider if the eye in which the Sebel is present is strong, and there is no illness in it besides the Sebel; then collect its Sebel.
And it is that you should instruct the sick person to place their head in your bosom.
Then suspend those veins with one hook, or two according to your prudence: and let the hook be a fine double hook, according to this form, or let there be two hooks graded on a single body, according to this form.
Then collect those veins with fine scissors with delicacy, and wipe away the blood hour by hour, until you see those veins removed from the eye and they dissolve; and beware lest you injure the eye with the tips of the scissors.
And let your operation be in the midday sun, and be very firm in your operation, so that you do not strike anything but those veins.
And when you have finished, then distill into the eye a green or red syr, so that with its sharpness it corrodes whatever remains of the scab.
However, if it is not possible for you to collect it all in that hour, then plaster the eye with that which soothes the eye's pain, and leave it for a few days until its pain subsides, and is secured from a hot abscess; then repeat on it according to the same method, until it is healed.
And this is the form of the scissors.
Concerning the reduction of a fractured nose.
Chapter XIX.
Physicians call a fistula, which you treat with a cautery or with a burning sharp medicine, according to its preceding description.
And if it does not heal, then there is no remedy for it, unless you lance the abscess near its origin, and extract all the moisture and pus from it, until the bone is exposed.
When therefore the bone is exposed, and you see corruption or blackness in it, then scrape it with an instrument, whose shape is this.
And it is called a rough head.
Let it be made of tempered iron, and let its head be round like an azir, that is, pointed with the filing of a file, or with the fine point of an aliskilfer: and place it on the diseased place of the bone.
Then turn it between your fingers, and press your hand a little while, until you know that the corruption has been scraped away: and do this several times.
Then restore the place with drying, astringent medicines.
If therefore the place is consolidated, and flesh grows on it, and the course of pus is cut off; and it remains for forty days, and is not renewed, and does not become an abscess, and no accident happens to it: then know that it is now healed.
And if not, then there is no cure for it, unless to reduce the fistula to the nostril opening, according to this form.
And it is, that you expose the bone with an instrument or with a sharp medicine; and when the bone has been exposed, then take a drill according to this form, let its iron tip be triangular; and let its wood be a tapering pyramid to the tip, as you see.
Then place it on the bone itself; and let it be near the tear duct; and move your hand away from the eye a little. Then revolve your hand with the drill, until it penetrates the bone, and the sick person feels it: through this, that he finds air escaping from it, when he holds his hand over his nose.
Therefore, then restore the place with astringent drying medicines, as I have taught you.
When therefore flesh grows, and the place hardens, then the matter which was flowing outwards returns to the nose; it is therefore lighter for the sick person.
Concerning the reduction of the prominence of the Eye.
Chapter XX.
When the eye protrudes completely, and no harm or diminution occurs in the vision, then you should release the patient, then bleed him, afterwards apply cupping to the neck without scarification, but with suction.
Then apply a liniment to the eye made from acacia, aloe, olibanum, and sarcocolla. Then cover the eye from above with a plaster with many small bandages.
And the way of the small bandages is: that you take soft cloths, many, and each cloth is folded over four times; make many of them; then place them one over another, according to the amount the eye needs; then bind them with a strong bandage.
And repeat the cupping with suction without scarification.
Then remove the cupping, and leave the eye bound day and night.
However, if the bandage softens again, then it is necessary to bind it tightly; then remove the bandage after a day and a night, as we said.
If therefore you see the eye now returned to the white tunic, it is good; and if not, then apply the plaster again, and the bandages, and the binding, and the cupping, until it is healed.
Concerning the incision of the white tunic.
Chapter XXI.
When a rupture occurs in the white tunic, then it protrudes and comes out beyond the eyelids like a grain of the white tunic, and it disfigures the appearance of the person on account of it, and the wish is to incise it; then the procedure for it is, as I describe: and it is, that you introduce a needle into the root of the white tunic from the lower to the upper part.
Then introduce another needle, on which there is a double thread from the tear duct side, and have it penetrate; and leave the first needle according to its position.
Then cut the place of the double thread, and tie with it the part of the white tunic in the circumference of the needle; and tighten it greatly.
Then withdraw the needle, and place on the eye wool soaked in the white of an egg; and leave it until the thread falls with the white tunic.
Then care for the eye with that which strengthens it, until the wound is healed.
And sometimes the white tunic is tightened from every side with a circle of cloth, until the eye adds great prominence.
Then pierce it with a fine spatula, which reaches the depth of the eye, and let the white moisture run, and let the eye descend, and immediately enter [the wound]; then tighten it until it is healed.
Concerning the treatment of Alcomnat.
Chapter XXII.
This ailment, called Alcomnat, is nothing but pus that collects in the eye, similar to descending water, and it is not the eye itself.
And the method of operation in it is that you make the sick person sit on a prepared seat, or upright.
Then take his head with your hands by both sides, and move it until you see the pus reach the lower parts of his eyes. Then let it be fixed, and do not let it descend, and let the light be cleared: and let the sick person see things as he saw them before: and if it does not descend further, know that it is water.
If, however, the descent of the pus is not prepared with what we have said, then make the sick person sit between your hands. Then take a fine spatula, and with it split the corneal membrane slightly at the junction of the conjunctiva with the cornea, in the crown, until pus exits.
When it therefore exits, then instill into the eye warm water in which you have mixed honey; or water in which you have already boiled fenugreek and honey; then treat with the remaining treatment, until he is healed.
Concerning the treatment of water that descends in the eye, or cataract.
Chapter XXIII.
We have already discussed the types of water in division, and what is suitable for piercing, the speaker explaining and setting forth; then take that same thing according to health.
Then, therefore, it is necessary that you make the infirm person sit between your hands, square, opposite the light, near the sun; and bind his healthy eye, and tightly restrain it; then, with your right hand, if the eye in which there is water is the left eye, or with your left hand, if the eye is the right, press the eyelid of his eye.
Then take an almond with your right hand, if the eye is the left; or with your left hand, if the eye is the right.
Then place the tip of the almond near the crown, through the thickness of a ray, on the apparent white of the eye, the smaller tear; then push the almond with force, and you will rotate your hand with it, until it penetrates the white of the eye; and you will feel that the almond has now reached the empty space.
And it is necessary that the measure of the almond's journey to the depth be the measure of the space that is from the pupil to the end of the blackness; and it is the crown of the eye. For you will see them in the pupil itself, by means of the vision of the eye, because of the clarity of the cornea.
Then place the almond to the place where the water is. Then press it below, one time after another.
If therefore the water descends immediately, then the sick person sees that upon which he opens his sight immediately, and the almond in his eye.
Then rest a little. If therefore the water descends, then remove it a second time, except that the almond emerges.
When therefore it is fixed, and does not ascend, then extract the almond with ease, and you turn your hand gradually.
Then dissolve some of the clear gem salt in water, and wash the inside of the eye with it. Then place over the eye a whole piece of lint, or wool soaked in rose oil and egg white, and bandage the sound eye with it.
We, and those of our time, place crushed cumin with egg white on it.
If the almond does not obey you in entering the eye because of its hardness, since it is from humans whose eye is very hard, then you should take a spatula, which is called Alberid, of which this is the form.
Therefore, scrape with it only the conjunctiva itself, and do not place your hand in the opening; and it is only to make a subtle path to the almond's place.
Then introduce the almond, according to what we said.
And at the completion of your work, prepare a properly made bedchamber for the sick person, in which he shall sleep on his back in a dark room; and prohibit all movements and coughing; and place his food that which eases his nature; and let him not move his head to the right or left at all.
And let the bandage be according to its arrangement until the third day.
Then loosen it in that dark room, and test his sight; and make him see things. Then replace the bandage until the seventh day.
And it should not be done at the hour of treatment, or immediately after the incision; rather, it should be deferred; because water ascends rapidly through forceful looking.
However, if a hot abscess occurs, then it is necessary that you loosen the eye before the seventh day, and treat it with that which calms the abscess until it is calmed; then free its sight, and place over its face an oral thing by which its sight is governed for a few days, while he remains in that dark room.
Then let him emerge from the house gradually, and converse in his houses.
And know that in the zaltea a pupil is not excused from sight sometimes; then indeed let him dare on the operation.
And indeed there has come to us one of those who are from Alayrach, who said that a perforated magdan was made in Alayrach, from which water is drawn.
But indeed I have not seen anyone in your land who has done that; nor have I read it in any of the books of the ancients; and it is possible that it is new.
And these are the shapes of the species from the magda, so that he who is ignorant of them may stand upon them.
Let them be made properly of bronze; and let their extremities be triangular with the same subtlety, and the extremity sharp.
Chapter Twenty-Four.
Concerning the treatment of flesh grown in the nose.
Sometimes diverse fleshy growths arise in the nose, some resembling a scorpion with many legs; and from this there is cancerous, stony, dark-coloured flesh; and from this there is soft flesh, not dark-coloured.
In those fleshy growths that are soft, not fraudulent nor cancerous, it is necessary to make the patient sit between your hands facing the ground, and open their nostrils, and insert a hook into the flesh. Then pull the growths outward, and cut what you have grasped with a fine, sharp spatula on one side, until you know that all the flesh has been removed.
If anything remains from it whose removal is not possible, then scrape it away with one of the fine instruments easily, until nothing is left of it. If, however, blood overwhelms you, or a hot abscess occurs, then apply what is appropriate; or if it is from malignant abscesses, then hurry to cauterize it, until the blood is separated and all the flesh is removed.
Then vinegar and water, or wine, should be applied to the nose after the incision. If, however, the nose opens, and moisture flows from it to the palate, then know that it is now healthy.
If, however, the moisture does not penetrate as it should, then know that inside it there is flesh grown on the upper bones of the nostril, to which the instrument did not reach with the incision.
Then it is necessary to introduce a linen thread of some thickness, with many knots tied in it, and place a space of a finger's width, or less, between each knot.  And let the sick person push one end of the thread into the nose with a rod, or with whatever is possible for them, after having made it similar to a probe.  And let them pull out their odor, until it reaches the abscess and emerges over their throat.
And often boys in schools make something similar to this atomi; and it is an easy matter for him who wishes.
Then place the two ends of the thread, one end which emerges from the nose, and the other which remains in the nose. Then cut away the flesh with the knots that are on the thread. Do that until you know that the flesh is now with the knots of the thread.
Then pull out the thread; and apply to the nose after cleansing the blood a compress soaked in Egyptian ointment: do this for three days, or more, until the ointment eats away all that remains of the flesh.
Then place a lead cannula at the end of the thing in the nose for several days, until it is healed.
If however it needs a treatment that dries it, use that.
To make a nasal douche, with which oils and other medicines are distilled into the nose, make it of silver or bronze: it should be like a small lamp, open like a seashell; and its passageway similar.
And if you wish, make a closed cannula, like a tube.
And the seashell instrument for nasal douching should be full, uncovered; which has a handle at its rear, with which you hold it when you heat oil in it, or whatever you wish from juices and flowing substances.
Chapter XXV.
Often a boil is born at the tip of the nose, and it grows larger and increases in size with the days, until its appearance becomes unsightly.
And because of that, it is necessary to cauterize it at the beginning of its appearance, and to eradicate its entire being. Then place over the area either a cautery or a burning medicine that acts in its place.
If its incision is delayed until it grows larger, then consider, if it is stony, hard, dark in color, and with little sensation: then do not touch it with iron, for it is a cancerous abscess.
For I have often seen that one who incised a man's abscess, and great harm came from it to the one who had it.
And if the abscess is soft to the touch, not dark in color, and you see that its incision is possible throughout its entirety, then evacuate the pus, and incise it without fear, without apprehension; and treat the area with something that dries and contracts it until it is healed.
Chapter XXVI.
Concerning the suture of the nose, and lip, and ear.
Know that when a separation of continuity occurs in one of these cartilages, little help is given by surgery to it, except in certain individuals.
Therefore, when something from that happens to someone, you should consider if it is a recent wound with its own blood; and then bring together the edges of the wound with a suture; afterwards, care for it until it heals.
And if the edges of the separation of continuity are already separated, and each part is healthy: then it is necessary that you scrape away every part from its visible skin until it bleeds. Then bring together the two edges with a suture, and tighten them, and powder over it the blood of the dragon and ground olibanum; and place on top of the powders a piece of a diafinicon plaster, and some other of the consolidating plasters; and leave it bound for two or three days. Then loosen it and change the medicine, and leave it until the threads come loose by themselves. Then treat it with ointment until it heals.
The method of suturing is, that you bring together the separation of continuity, either with needles, as we have described in the suturing of the belly; or bring together the separation with thread, as I have made you know.
Chapter XXVII.
Concerning the extraction of lip nodules.
Many people frequently suffer hard abscesses resembling plant seeds on the inner parts of their lips: and some of them are smaller, and some larger.
Therefore, it is necessary to turn back the lip, and to cut above every nodule on it, and to lift it with a hook, and to cut it from every side.
Then fill the place after the incision with pounded zegi, until the blood ceases.
Then it should be washed with vinegar, and the place where there is astringency should be treated with it, until the wounds are healed.
Chapter XXVIII.
Concerning the incision of flesh growing on the gum.
Often flesh grows on the gum, which the ancients call a  bulging.
Therefore, you should suspend it with a hook, or hold it with forceps, and cut it at its root, and let the pus or blood run.
Then place on the area ground zegi, or other astringent, drying powders.
If that flesh returns after treatment, for it often does, then cut it a second time, and cauterize it; for it will not return after cauterization.
Chapter XXIX.
Concerning the extraction of teeth with iron.
Sometimes rough, foul, and sometimes blackened, and yellowish or greenish, hard crusts collect on the surfaces of the teeth from the inner and outer sides, and between the gums, until that corruption reaches the gum, and the teeth become unsightly through it.
Therefore, you should have the sick person sit between your hands, and his head in your lap, and scrape the molar and tooth, on which the crusts, and matter like sand, appear, until nothing remains of them; and similarly, deal with the blackness, greenness, and yellowness, and the rest, until they are cleansed.
If therefore what is in them is removed from the beginning of scraping, it is good; and if not, then repeat the scraping on them on another, second, and third day, until finally your desire is achieved.
And know that a tooth requires scrapers of diverse shapes and many forms, according to what is prepared for your operation; for the scraper with which the tooth is scraped from the lower part is different from the scraper with which it is scraped between the teeth, according to other shapes.
And these shapes of scrapers be prepared by you, which you see.
Chapter 30.
Of the extraction of toothache.
You ought to treat the tooth from its pain with all skill, and delay in its eradication. For there is nothing to replace its function when it is eradicated, since it is a noble substance; until when there is no excuse for its eradication, then it is necessary when the sick person is forced to eradicate it, that it may be strengthened, and that the aching tooth be certified by you.
For often the pain deceives the sick person, and he thinks that it is in the tooth itself and eradicates it; then the pain does not recede until the sick person's tooth is eradicated.
For we have already seen that from the operation of blood-letters.
Therefore, when the aching tooth itself is certified by you, then it is necessary that you make incisions around the tooth with a lancet, in which there should be something of virtue, until the gum is loosened on every side; then move it with your fingers, or with fine forceps at first slowly, until you dislodge it.
Then place large forceps in a good position on it, and place the patient's head between your knees; which you have now firmly held, so that it does not move; then pull the tooth straight, so that you do not break it.
If therefore it does not come out, then take some of those instruments, and insert it underneath it from every side with ease, and try its movement, as you did at first.
If however the tooth is perforated or decayed, then it is necessary that you fill that hole with cloth; and bind it with the end of a fine reed, so that it is not broken at the time of your constriction upon it with forceps.
And it is necessary that you meticulously perform the incisions around the gum from every side very much.
And maintain your strength, so that you do not break it, and a part of it remains, and returns upon the sick person with greater harm than its initial pain.
And beware that you do not do that which ignorant phlebotomists do in their haste, and their boldness in eradicating it, other than that which we have described.
For they often cause great ailments in people, which are more difficult to break, and all or some of its roots remain; or that they extract it with some of the jaw bones, as we have often witnessed.
Then it should be washed after its removal with wine, or with vinegar and salt.
If a flow of blood occurs from the places (for it often does occur), then apply some of the zegi, and fill the place with it; and if not, cauterize it if the zegi does not help you.
The shapes of the fine forceps, with which the tooth is first moved, should have long extremities, a short handle, and be thick so that they do not double when pressed on the tooth.  They should be like you see handles that are thick, so that when you tighten them, they do not yield or double; they should have short extremities, and be made of good iron, or of properly made steel, with straight extremities; and on their extremities there should be teeth, some of which enter into others, and tighten with a suitable, strong constriction.
And sometimes the extremities are made like the shape of the beak of a bird called a stork, and they are of strong constriction.
Chapter XXXI.
Concerning the removal of teeth, and the extraction of broken jawbones.
When, during the removal of a tooth, something remains, then it is necessary to apply a poultice of butter to the area for one or two days, until the area is softened.  Then introduce to it algasti or forceps, whose extremities resemble the beak of a bird called a stork. However, this is the shape of the forceps.
Their extremities should be made like a file inside, or like an aliskilfegi.
If, therefore, it does not yield to you for removal with these forceps, then it is necessary that you carefully expose the root and remove all the flesh, and insert the instrument called a small lever, whose form is this.
Let it be short, somewhat thick, and not soaked, so that it does not break.
If therefore the root emerges through it, it is good; and if not, it is necessary to assist with other instruments whose forms are these.
The form of the first is triangular with a certain thickness at the extremity.
The form of the extremity is triangular and slender.
And with other instruments and tools which have preceded in the consideration of teeth.
There is also assistance with this instrument, which resembles a large hook, of this form: triangular extremity twisted, with a certain thickness so it will not break; and let it not be soaked.
And know that there are many instruments for teeth; and similarly, other instruments are not counted, and a skillful craftsman, subtle in his art, finds for himself the instrument according to which the operation and the illnesses themselves indicate to him.
Because there are illnesses for which the ancients did not say there is an instrument, owing to the diversity of their kinds.
If however a bone of the jaw, or of other bones of the mouth, breaks or rots, then search upon it in its place with what is suitable to it from among the instruments and forceps that I have spoken of in the extraction of roots, and assist with a forceps, whose form is this.
Let it have a certain gradual thickness, so that the bone is grasped or held with it, and does not escape and go, until the bone comes out; and restore the place with suitable medicines for it.
If however there is a small rot in the bone, then scrape it from its rot and care for it until it is healed.
Chapter XXXII.
Of the sawing of teeth that protrude above others.
When teeth protrude not according to their natural course, an unsightly form results; and especially when this occurs in women and girls, namely those who are for sale.
Therefore it is necessary that you consider. For if a tooth is born behind another tooth, and it is not positioned properly, or its sawing or filing is not possible, then remove it; and if it is joined to another tooth, then cut it with this instrument, of which this is the form.
It is similar to a small beak, and is made of tempered iron, with a very sharp tip.
And let your incision be such that you cut it over many days, and because of the tooth's hardness, and so that another tooth is not moved.
If, however, it is prominent, exposed to its filing: then file it with a file of tempered iron, whose form is this.
Let it be entirely of tempered iron, and its handle of very small points, like the file with which needles are made; with which the tooth is filed gradually over many days with ease, so that the tooth is not moved and falls out; then smooth it, and finally shave it with some razors.
If it is a tooth from which a part has broken off, and it injures the tongue, then it is necessary to file it also, until the roughness of that fracture is removed and it is made even; and flattened, so that it does not injure the tongue, nor impair speech.
Chapter XXXIII.
Of the retention of teeth with gold threads.
When the anterior teeth suffer concussion and displacement from a blow or fall, and the patient cannot bite on it without it falling out, and you have treated them with astringent remedies, and the treatment was not successful, then a device for them is to be tied with gold thread, because silver turns green and putrefies after days, but gold never changes.
And let the thread be medium in fineness and thickness according to the amount that it can hold between the teeth.
And the manner of retention is, to take a thread, and to insert double its length between two sound teeth; then to weave the two ends of the thread between the loosened teeth, whether one or more, until you reach with the weave to a sound tooth on the other side. Then repeat the weaving to the point from which you began; and tie your hand with care and wisdom, until it is not removed at all; and let your tightening of the thread be at the roots of the teeth, so that it does not slip out.
Then cut the superfluous ends of the thread with scissors; and gather each, and turn them with forceps; and hide them between the sound tooth and the displaced tooth, so that it does not harm the tongue; then let it be so tightened that it remains.
If however it is loosened, or cut, tighten them with another thread, and it will stand with it in this way for the entire time.
And this is the method for teeth, and the figure for retention of two sound teeth and two displaced teeth, as you see.
And sometimes it happens that one or two teeth return to their places after their fall, and are held as we have described, and remain; and only a skillful, prudent, and experienced craftsman can do this.
And sometimes a piece of bone is fitted; and it is made into the shape of a tooth, and placed in the gap, whereby the tooth is strengthened and tightened, as we said; and it remains, and is preserved with it for a long time.
Chapter Thirty-Four.
Sometimes this ligament that occurs under the tongue is either natural, with which a person is born, or accidental, from a wound that has already healed.
And the operation on it is that you open the patient's mouth at his head in your presence, and raise his tongue; then cut that nervous ligament across, until the tongue is released from its restraint.
If there is hardness and knotting in it, and it is from the healing of a wound, then insert a hook into it and split it across until you cut the ligament, and the knotting is released.
And beware, lest there be a fissure deep in the flesh, and you cut an artery there, and a flow of blood occur.
Then wash the area of the incision with rosewater, or vinegar, or cold water.
Then place a linen bandage under the tongue, which the patient should hold all night, so that it does not heal a second time.
If a flow of blood occurs, then apply crushed zegi to the area. If, however, blood overcomes you, then cauterize the area with a freckled cautery, which is suitable for it; then treat it with the rest of the treatment until it heals.
Chapter XXXV.
Sometimes an abscess occurs under the tongue resembling a small frog, hindering the tongue from its natural function; and perhaps it enlarges so much that it fills the mouth.
And the procedure in this is to open the patient's mouth before the sun, and examine the abscess.
If therefore you see it of a dark color, or black, hard, and the patient has no feeling in it, then do not touch it, for it is cancer.
And if it is tending towards whiteness, in which there is moisture, then insert a hook into it, and cut it with a fine scalpel, and free it from every side. If blood overcomes you during your operation, then place crushed zegi on it until the blood ceases; then return to your operation until you extract it with its entirety.
Then wash it with vinegar and salt, and treat it with the appropriate remaining treatment until it is healed.
Concerning the treatment of an abscess of the two tonsils.
Chapter XXXVI.
Sometimes there occur between the throat, that is, glands, which are similar to algodad, which occur from outside; and they are called the two tonsils; which when you treat them with what we have said in the division, and they do not heal, then consider.
If therefore it is a hard abscess, of dark colour, with little feeling, then do not touch it with iron.
And if it is of red colour, and its root is thick, then do not touch it even with iron, fearing a flow of blood, rather leave it until it ripens. And then either you will pierce it, or it will break by itself.
And if it is of white colour, round, and its root is subtle, then it is necessary to cut it.
And the operation upon it is that you should consider before its operation, if its abscess has rested in quiet complete, or is lessening by some diminution; then therefore make the sick person sit before the sun, and his head be in your lap; and open his mouth, and let the servant take between his hands, and press his tongue downward with an instrument, whose shape is this.
Let it be made of silver, or bronze, which is as subtle as a knife.
When therefore you press with it the tongue, and the abscess is shown to you, and your sight falls upon it, then take a hook, and fix it in the tonsil, and pull it outward as much as you can, beyond which pull out with it some of the coverings.
Then cut it with an instrument, whose form follows this. It resembles shears; however, its extremities should be curved, so that the orifice of each of them is in a direct line with the other sharply; hence take care that it be made of tempered iron or dipped in alfuled.
If however this instrument is not present, then cut it with a spatula, whose form is this. Be sharp on one side, not sharp on the other.
And after you have cut one tonsil, cut the other according to this same method of incision.
Then after the incision, let the sick person gargle with cold water, or with vinegar and water.
If however a flow of blood should happen to him, let him gargle with water in which the peels of pomegranates have been boiled: or myrtle leaves, and similar things from astringents, until the flow of blood is stopped: then care for him until he is healed.
And sometimes other abscesses arise in the throat, besides the two tonsils. Therefore, cut it according to what I said about the incision of the two tonsils equally.
And indeed I myself have already treated a woman from an abscess that had arisen between her throat, which was drawing to blackness, with few senses, which had almost closed her throat; and the woman was breathing from a narrow passage, and I had already forbidden her eating and drinking water; and she had already come to death, if she had remained for one or two days.
And the abscess had risen so much that it had sent out two branches, until they had emerged above the two nostrils.
Therefore I quickly hastened, and I fixed a hook in one of them. Then I pulled it, and a large portion came out from it.
Then I cut it, where I grasped it from the nostril.
Then I did the same to that which came out of the other nostril.
Then I opened its mouth, and pressed its tongue; and afterwards I fixed a hook into the abscess; then I cut a portion from it, and only a little blood ran from it: therefore the woman's throat was healed, and she immediately began to drink water, then she took food.
Therefore, I did not cease to cut from that abscess many times for a long time, and the abscess would succeed in the place of that which I was cutting, until it was excessively prolonged, and along with it that which was in it.
