Therefore, I devised, and cauterized the abscess between the throat.
Therefore, it stood from the addition; then I walked away from it, and I did not know what God did with it after me.
Concerning the incision of the abscess of the uvula.
Chapter XXXVII.
When the catarrh descends to the uvula, and it becomes an abscess, and is long, then it is called a perpendicular; and if it is in the lower part thick, round, then it is called a grape.
When it is treated with what we said in the division, and the treatment does not help; and you see that the hot abscess has calmed down from it, and is subtle; then it is necessary to cut it.
And what is gathered from it, round, and has no length, and is blood-red, or brown, or black, and has no sensation; then it is necessary to avoid its incision. For there is fear in it for the sick person.
Therefore, when you see it according to the manner which I said, from its whiteness and length, you must make the sick person sit in front of the sun; and press his tongue with the instrument of which the description preceded; then fasten a hook into the uvula and pull it downwards; and cut it with one of the two instruments which I mentioned in the incision of the two tonsils.
And it is necessary not to cut from it except that which is added beyond the natural limit without addition.
For if you cut from it more, you will injure the voice and speech.
Then, after the incision, use that which we have described in the incision of the two tonsils, and care for it until it heals.
If, however, the sick person is terrified by the incision, then it is necessary to use ingenuity in cauterizing it without fear and panic.
And the method of the cautery on it is not, unless with sharp water; and it is, that you place the head of the sick person in your bosom. Then press his tongue with the instrument that we mentioned. Then take from the water which I said to you in the chapter on cauterization, and make with it an unquenched lime, and place it not thick, nor thin, and fill with it the concavity of this instrument, of which this is the shape.
Let the extremity of it on which the medicine is placed have a concavity, like the concavity of a spoon's radius.
And place the instrument with the medicine upon the uvula itself; and let the sick person lie on his side, so that saliva may run from his mouth from the internal medicine, so that nothing may descend from it to his throat and harm him.
Then hold your hand with the medicine. And you shall apply it to the uvula for a half-hour's quantity, until you see it blackened and the gnawing of the medicine settled.
And if you wish to take a clot, and wrap it on the extremity of the radius, and infuse the clot with the medicine, and introduce the radius with the clot into the cannula from above, until the clot adheres to the uvula.
Do this many times, until you achieve what you desire concerning the cauterization of the uvula. Then leave it; for it dries and falls off after three or four days.
If you need to repeat the medicine, repeat it.  And after the cauterization, wipe around the uvula with a cloth soaked in butter, and cleanse with whatever is around it from the medicine. And rinse with cold water.
And treat externally with embrocations, and internally with gargles, until healed.
This method of treatment is therefore healthier and further from fear.
And sometimes the uvula is treated with something finer than cautery and incision; for it is treated with fumigation, in this way. Take calamus, hyssop, oregano, rue, sseah, chamomile, and southernwood, and similar herbs: and gather them all, or some of them, in a pot, and immerse them in vinegar, and boil them. And let the pot be well-coated with clay; and let there be a hole in the middle of the pot, over which a concave instrument be placed in this manner.
Let it be made of silver or bronze; and let the end, in which is the pomegranate, be inserted into the sick person's mouth, until the vapor ascends to the uvula above the cannula, until the uvula is greatly darkened.
Then repeatedly over it many times, until it dries.
And beware, lest you perform this treatment at the beginning of the arrival of the abscess. For it usually adds to the abscess; and it is not fitting that you do it except at the decline of its hot stage.
If however this instrument is not present for you, then take a cannula, and attach a piece of egg-shell to its end; so that the mouth of the sick person is not burned; because the egg-shell prevents the vapor from burning the mouth; and this is from good treatment with health.
Concerning the extraction of thorns and bones from the throat.
Chapter XXXVIII.
Often a bone, or a fish bone, or something else adheres in the throat.
Therefore, it is necessary to extract what is apparent from them upon which the sight falls, after pressing the tongue with an instrument in the sun, and it is revealed to you what is in the throat.
And what is not apparent to you and is hidden in the throat, it is necessary to make the weak vomit before the food is digested in the stomach. Perhaps the attached thing will come out through vomiting.
Or let the weak swallow a piece of turnip, or a lettuce root, or swallow a piece of dry bread; or let him take a piece of soft dry sea sponge: and tie it to a thread, then swallow it. When therefore it reaches the place where there are the spines, or bone, and is extracted, then it comes out.
If therefore it does not come out with what we have said, then use an instrument of lead according to this shape.
Let it be thicker than a Radius a little, and at its extremity let there be a bend, which he shall introduce into his throat with ease; and let him have his head raised upwards.
And let him beware of touching his epiglottis, lest coughing befall him, and let him propel with it the bone, or spine, or let the physician introduce it with his hand.
But that which the sick person introduces is better, on account of his knowledge of the place of the attached thing; and let him propel it downwards, or draw his hand with the instrument upwards, the whole of it according to the quantity that is prepared for him, until it comes out.
Concerning the extraction of the Leech.
Chapter Thirty-Nine.
When the leech is treated with what I have said in the division concerning treatment with medicines, and does not help, then consider in the throat of the sick person before the sun, or in the sun, after you have pressed his tongue with the instrument which I have told you.
If therefore your sight falls upon the leech, extract it with a small hook, or with slender instruments properly.
If it is not possible with these, then take a concave tube, and introduce it into the throat of the sick person up to near the throat; then introduce into the concavity of the tube a heated iron with fire. Do this many times; and let the sick person abstain from water all day.
Then let him take a vessel full of cold water, and open his mouth in it; and let him wash his mouth with it, and not swallow anything from it; and let him move the water one hour after another with his hand. For the leech immediately, when it feels the water, falls.
However, if it does not emerge with what we have described, then fumigate the sick person with lice, or with asa with the instrument that I have described in the fumigation of the uvula.
Do that many times, because it will fall itself.
And the method of operation in fumigation is, that you take a pot, in which there are live prunes with fire; and the pot be covered with a lid, in whose middle be a hole; and the extremity of the instrument be placed in that hole.
Then emit the fumigation, and let the sick person place his mouth on the extremity of the tube, and close his mouth, so that the fumigation does not emerge, until you know that the fumigation has already reached the leech. For the leech will immediately fall.
If it does not fall, then repeat the fumigation many times.
And let the sick person endure thirst, and eat salt, and garlic, and do not drink water.
Therefore, it is necessary that it emerge with this regimen.
And this is the form of the instrument with which the leech is extracted from the throat, when it falls upon it visibly.
It is similar to forceps, as you see: except that it has this curvature which is inserted into the throat. And its extremity is similar to the mouth of a bird called a stork, in which there is the roughness of a file: when it grasps something, it does not let it go at all.
Concerning the opening of abscesses, and their incision, and care in general.
Chapter XL.
Abscesses have many kinds, and are of many types, according to the account of them, namely one, and one in this book: and they are diverse in their perforation and their incision in two ways.
One of which is from the species of abscess in itself, and because it comprises humidities.
And the second method is because of the places, in which it happens in the body.
Because an abscess occurring in the head is different from an abscess occurring in the anus; and an abscess occurring in a fleshy place is different from an abscess occurring in the joints; and each of them has its own judgment from the operation.
And of abscesses there are some which should not be perforated, unless after the maturation of the pus in them, and its completion; and of them there are some which should be perforated, while they are raw and have not matured according to completion.
Like abscesses which are near the joints.
Because an abscess, when it occurs near a joint, and continues to grow until the surrounding tissue is putrefied, perhaps the ligaments or nerves of that joint are corrupted: wherefore it is the more proximate cause of the contraction of that member.
Or it is an abscess near a main limb; because if you delay its perforation until it matures, it will harm either that limb or the main limb through it.
Or it is near the anus, therefore perforate it unripe; and it is not necessary to perforate a raw, incompletely digested abscess, except that which is near the anus, so that it does not putrefy deeply, and extend to the interior of the anus, and become a fistula, or become incurable at its end.
One should know the time of perforation of a matured abscess according to its completion: and it is by the cessation of pain, the decrease of fever, the reduction of redness, and of throbbing, and sharpness of the abscess head; and the other signs.
And it is necessary to make the same perforation in the lower and more prominent place of the abscess; and let the perforation extend along the body's length, if the abscesses are in the hands and feet, and in the places of the tendons, and cords, and nerves, and arteries, and lastly in all equal places where there is no reflection.
In those which are reflected, proceed with the perforation along that limb.
However, when the abscess is in fleshy places, then it is better to leave its perforation until its digestion is confirmed, as we said according to its completion.
For if you perforate it before that, the course of the virus from it is prolonged, and there is much dirt and filth, and perhaps its edges and depth will be hardened.
And some abscesses are perforated according to the breadth of the body, when necessary, or according to the part of the limb it affects.
And it is necessary that you make a small perforation in small abscesses, and a large perforation in large abscesses, or many incisions in proportion to the size of the abscess.
And there are abscesses in which the skin must be hollowed out, and incised when it has already become like a rag, and has reached a point where it is already dead, as happens in many boils and buboes.
And some of them are cut with a three-angled incision. And some of them are incised in the shape of a myrtle leaf.
Like an inguinal abscess.
And among them are some in which a round incision, and a crescent-shaped incision are administered, and similar incisions to these.
However, that which has no head, like mild, superficial abscesses, should be pierced with a superficial, or merely a flat, perforation.
And when the abscess is large, and has already collected much pus, and you pierce it, you should not be hasty and extract all the pus at that time; rather, extract only a portion of it. Then plug the abscess until another day. Then extract some more of the pus again.
Do this repeatedly according to a gradual approach, until it is discharged: especially if the patient is weak, or a pregnant woman, or a young child, or an aged and decrepit person; for the animal spirit of the creature is often resolved with the discharge of the pus; and perhaps the patient dies, and you do not perceive it.
Therefore, observe this chapter with great caution.
And after your perforation, with which you perforate these abscesses, it is necessary that you cleanse the wound: and consider. If therefore the wound of the abscess is small, or is a flat incision, then apply bandages of linen, or old cloth. And if the abscess is large, and there are many fissures in the perforation, then it is necessary that you introduce a bandage into every fissure until they meet.
And if in the abscess you have cut off a portion of skin, or have hollowed it out around the edges: then it is necessary that you fill it with old cloth, or as when a cloth is woven or trimmed with linen fuzz without moisture: and bind it until the third day.
Then discard it, and treat it with what is necessary of ointment, until it is healed.
If however a flow of blood occurs during your operation, then apply cold water and vinegar, after a cloth is soaked in them: and place it repeatedly on the place of the bleeding.
If however the flow of blood persists, then it is necessary that you administer the regimen, and treatment, and powders, which I have described in many places in this book of ours, and in the division.
If however it is in the time of winter, and the location of the abscess is of many nerves: then it is necessary that you infuse cushions with warm wine and oil: and place them over the locations.
And if it is in summer, and the places are fleshy: then it is necessary that you place cushions soaked in water and oil, or in wine and oil, and make the whole thing cold. Until when the third day shall have arrived, as we have said, then it is necessary that you open the abscess, and clean it, and administer to its treatment what is appropriate for it, until it is healed.
This is therefore what is necessary from the knowledge of the treatment of abscesses universally.
Following the path of distinction, however, I have already spoken of every abscess, how there is a way to its treatment in separate chapters.
Chapter XLI.
Concerning the incision of abscesses that occur on the scalp.
Small abscesses occur on the scalp, and are of the type of knots, which the coverings (tunics), which are like sacs, contain: as if they were the pullets' crop.
And their types are numerous.
Some are fatty; and some contain moisture similar to mud; and some contain moisture similar to gruels and porridges; and similar to these; and some are hard, stony.
And in the incision of all of them, and their extraction, there is no fear, provided that in their incision, an artery does not oppose.
And the operation in their incision is to test them with an instrument, whose form will come after these, which is called the Intromissum, until you know what it contains.
For if it contains moisture, then cut it by an incision along its length, by a superficial incision, according to this form.
And begin with an incision from line b. to line g.  When the moisture is evacuated, then scrape the container which holds that moisture, and do not leave anything from it at all.
For it often returns if anything remains from it.
Then immerse the cut in Egyptian ointment, if present; and if not, in water and salt; and fill the wound with it, and leave it until another day. For it will corrode what remained from the container.
Then again apply Egyptian ointment over the cut, a second, and third time, and if you need it, until you are confident that nothing remains from the container; then treat the wound with ointment until it is healed.
If the abscess contains a fatty lump, then cut it according to this form, the incision having the shape of a cross; and insert hooks between the two arms of the incision, and scrape it from every side; and try your strength in extracting the tunic which contains it.
If, however, an artery offers resistance, then do that which I will tell you later.
And similarly do in an abscess, if it is stony, by incision and treatment the same, according to what we said.
And the incision upon a stony abscess is easier, because it is of little blood and moisture. And sometimes there are some of these abscesses which are in the head in certain people, with which there is absolutely no moisture.
Which is: because I incised upon an abscess in the head of an old woman, and found an abscess like a solid and hard stone, rough, white, which could not be broken; and if anyone struck it with anything in throwing, it wounded them.
In other abscesses, however, occurring in the head, apart from these abscesses which occur in the heads of children, and at the roots of the ears, their incision should all be a superficial incision.
Or make their perforation always from their lower part, so that the course of pus may be relieved downwards.
Then treat it with whatever is suitable for them from the treatment.
Concerning the incision of tumors on the neck.
Chapter XLII.
These abscesses often occur on the neck, and under the armpits, and in the groin; and sometimes they occur in the rest of the body.
And concerning those that occur on the neck, there are single ones and many; and they are generated from each other; and every tumor among them is within its own tunic, just as it is in nodes and abscesses of the head, as we have recounted.
And the types of these tumors are many. And among them are some that are stony; and among them are those that contain humors; and among them are malignant ones, which do not obey treatment.
Therefore, in any one of them that you see to be well-disposed on touch, the proximity of whose colour is manifest to the skin, and which moves in every direction, and is not attached to the nerves of the neck, nor to the veins, nor to the artery, nor is deep: you should separate it by a superficial incision from the upper part to the lower part of the body along a line, b, to a line, g, and scrape it from every side; and extend the two lips of skin with a hook, or with many hooks, if you need it, as we said regarding abscesses of the head, and pull it out gradually.
And it is a precaution that a vein or a nerve not be cut.
And let the scalpel not be excessively sharp, so that it does not harm your hand with the incision, or disturb the sick person, and cut what is not necessary.
However, if you cut a vein or an artery, or it hinders your operation, then place crushed zegi or some powders that stop blood in the wound, and bind the wound, and leave it until the sharpness of the abscess subsides, and the wound softens; or begins to putrefy.
For the blood is stopped then.  Therefore return to your operation until you complete it.
Then, with your index finger, investigate if any remaining small tumors or other small things remain; therefore, cut and cleanse them.
If there is a large vein at the root of the tumor, then you should not cut the tumor from its root; rather, you should tie it with a strong doubled thread and leave it until it falls off by itself without harm.
Then fill the wound with old linen, which you have already soaked in Egyptian ointment. Then treat it.
If the entire tumor is cut, then you must bring together the two edges of the wound; and suture it immediately, after you see that there is no longer any excess fluid.
However, if you see that excess fluid remains due to the size of the tumor, then you should aim to cut its root and remove it; and apply the suture we have described.
And whatever humors the tumor contains, pierce it with a superficial puncture so that its place of maturation is apparent to you; and place the puncture from what follows below its body, as we have said.
Then, after the puncture, apply bandages with ointment, and similar things, to corrode what remains of the corruption, until when you see the wound already cleansed; then treat it with a wound healing ointment, until it is healed.
Chapter XLIII.
Concerning the incision of the epiglottis from an abscess occurring within the throat.
The ancients spoke of this incision in the epiglottis, and I have not seen anyone in our land who has performed it; and this is the record of their words.
In those suffering from quinsy, the incision of the epiglottis should be avoided; as it does not bring relief; for all the veins and lung are weak.
However, in those who have a hot abscess in the mouth, or throat, or both tonsils, when there is no illness in the canal, the incision of the epiglottis should be used, to avoid the harm resulting from suffocation.
Therefore, when we wish to do it, we should cut the epiglottis beneath three or four circles of the epiglottis, with a small incision across its width, in the space between two circles, so that the incision is in the membrane, not the cartilage.  And this place is suitable for the incision, as it is devoid of flesh, and the blood vessels are distant from it.
If the physician is timid, then it is necessary to extend the skin of the throat with a hook. Then he should split the skin until he reaches the canal, avoiding any blood vessels if he sees any.
Then he should cut the membrane, as we have described: and the incision of the canal is signified by the phlegm that flows from it, with the tearing, and the severance of the voice.
And leave the wound open for a time.
When therefore the time in which suffocation is feared has passed, bring together the two lips of the wound, and suture only the skin itself, without the cartilage.
Then administer medicines that cause flesh to grow until it is healed.
Says Albucasis. The summary of this account we have given is not appropriate unless, when they see that some of these abscesses have already blocked the throat of the sick person; and the sick person is close to death, and his breathing is about to cease; they go to cut the epiglottis, so that the sick person may breathe above the wound by a certain respiration and be saved from death: and for this reason they prescribed to leave the wound open until the malice of the illness is consumed, and its malice is for three days and likewise; then, therefore, they prescribed to suture the wound, and to treat it until it is healed.
And that which I myself saw was that a certain maid took a knife and thrust it into her throat, and with it cut a part of the lung passage.
And I was summoned to treat her, and I found her groaning, like the groan of a dead person.
I therefore probed the wound, and found little blood that had flowed from the wound. Therefore, I concluded that she had not cut a vein, nor a large artery; and air was escaping from the wound.
I therefore hastened and dressed the wound, and treated it until it was healthy; and nothing happened to the maid except hoarseness in her voice; and she returned after days to her former condition.
Therefore, I say that there is no fear in cutting the epiglottis.
On the incision of a hernia of the throat.
Chapter XLIV.
This abscess, called hernia of the throat, is a large abscess, according to the color of the body; and it is very common in women.
And it is according to two modes: either it is natural, or it is accidental.
In the natural one, certainly, there is no ingenuity.
The accidental one, however, is according to two modes: of which one is similar to fatty tumors. And the other mode is similar to the abscess that arises from the knotting of an artery. And in it there is fear, and one should not presume to cut into it with iron unless it is small; and you should probe it, and investigate it with an instrument. If therefore you find it similar to a fatty tumor, and it is not connected to any vein, then cut it, as tumors are cut; and extract it with whatever it contains from a cyst, if it is in a cyst; and if not, then examine all of it. Then treat the place with what is appropriate for treatment until it is healed.
Chapter XLV.
Concerning the incision on the types of Tumors.
There are many types of tumors, and I have already described all their types in the division.  And it is necessary to explain here the difference between a tumor and a boil, since this causes doubt.
Therefore, I say that when a boil is present, there is heat, fever, and intense pain until the ebullition of the superfluity subsides and the putrefaction is complete; then the fever and heat cease.
However, with a tumor there is no heat, nor fever, nor pain, and it contains them within sacks, which are proper sacs to them, and it is according to the color of the body; and its beginning is like a chickpea, and it becomes like a batecha, and larger and smaller.
And they are of two types: either fatty or containing moisture. And the colors of moisture are many, according to what I have said in the division.
Therefore, when you arrive at the treatment of a tumor, it is necessary to examine it, and first to investigate it with the instrument called the *Intromissum*, according to the form of which will be presented in the chapter following this.
And the method of investigation for abscesses and all tumors is to take this instrument and insert it into the most moist place you find in the abscess; and you should slowly rotate your fingers with it until you know that the instrument has already penetrated the skin. Then place your hand at the end, according to the size of the abscess.
Then withdraw the *Intromissum*, and consider what comes out in its wake.
If, therefore, a running moisture, of whatever color, comes out, then cut it with a superficial incision, as I have said in the other abscesses.
And if no moisture comes out in the wake of the *Intromissum*, then know that it is fatty.  Therefore, make a cruciform incision upon it, in this form, as I taught you; and suspend it with hooks, and carefully scrape the skin from every side; and protect the *kistin* if you can above it, in order to pull out the healthy tissue along with the tumor.
If, however, the *kistin* breaks during the operation, and it is not possible to extract the healthy tissue, for this often happens, then pull it out piece by piece until nothing remains of it.
For if a small or large amount remains of it, the tumor returns most often.
If, however, it defeats you, and a little remains of it, then fill the wound at the completion of your work with some sharp corrosive powders, and constrict the wound, and place upon it what sits as a hot abscess, and treat it with the rest of the treatment until it is healed.
If therefore the tumor is large, then sew its two lips, and treat it with what causes it to heal by scar tissue.
If, however, there is a pulsating or non-pulsating vein there, then hurry and fill the place with pounded *zegi*, and leave it bound for two or three days until the wound becomes putrid, and the boiling of the blood ceases; then return to cutting that which remains of the tumor.
Chapter XLVI.
Concerning the exterior instruments for cutting and piercing.
Among them are the shapes of instruments for insertion, and they are of three kinds.
For among them is a large one, and among them is a medium one, and among them is a small one.
Let them be made of tempered iron, with square ends, properly, so that they may readily enter the abscess.
And these are the shapes of instruments for probing, and, no, that is, perforating ones: and one of them is called a 'beris', that is, a perforator: and they are of three kinds.
For among them is a large one, and a medium one, and a small one.
They are suitable for investigating abscesses, openings, fistulas, and buboes, and for what is between the bones within them.
And others are made round, smooth, and soft, like large needles with which saddles are sewn, that is, of brass, or of bronze, or iron, or silver.
And the best of them are those made of brass.
And experience also makes them of black lead, which are suitable for testing fistulas, whose concavity has bends, so that they may be reflected with their softness along those bends.
And they are again of three kinds. Because from them are long, from them are medium, and from them are short, according to the quantity of the fistula's breadth and its constriction.
The shape of hooks, and they are of many kinds.
Because from them are simple ones, namely those which have one curve: and they are of three kinds as you see, Large, Medium, and Small.
And from them are blind hooks: and they are of three kinds.
And from them are hooks having two curves, and they are of three kinds: and from them are hooks having three curves, and they are of three kinds: and all these are necessary in their place.
And these are the shapes of scalpels, with which knots and abscesses are cut and scraped: and they are of three kinds: because from them there is a large, a medium, and a small one.
Their cutting edges are sharp, other edges are not sharp: and they are not placed like this, unless that with them there is help in scraping knots, in fear of cutting a vein, or nerve: and that the infirm person is healed by them, and finds a little peace from the burning which he finds in the scraping of the abscess.
They are made of air, and are similar to a radio, with which alcohol is made: and on the wide end is a hidden Spatumilis point: and in it runs to the interior, and to the exterior when you wish, as you see.
The shape of the spatulas that are hidden between the fingers when perforating abscesses: and the sick do not perceive them: and they are of three kinds, large, medium, and small.
The forms of cupping glasses with which the flow of blood is cut off: and they are of three kinds, large, medium, and small.
They are made of air or amber, round, slightly elongated, as you see: and let them be of a thinness.
And it is necessary that these types of cupping glasses be large and small, so that you cut off blood with speed when needed, and when there is no medicine present for you.
However, do not use them to cut off blood in every part of the body, and do not use them except on fleshy parts: such as the thighs of the leg, and the hip, and the arms, and the nipples, and the belly, and the groin, and similar fleshy, moist parts.
And sometimes smaller instruments are made from them, similar to hazelnuts, in their shape: with which blood is cut off when it flows from a phlebotomy site, or at the incision of a vein or artery: and sometimes they are made round, in this form.
Chapter XLVII.
Concerning the care of men's nipples which resemble women's nipples.
Sometimes the nipples of some men swell at the completion of sleep (that is, when they begin to be polluted in sleep) until they resemble women's nipples: and they remain foul-looking abscesses.
Therefore, whoever abhors that, must cut above the nipples with a lunar incision, according to this shape from line b. to line g. Then scrape off all the fat. Afterwards, fill the wound with a flesh-producing medicine. Then bring together the two lips of the wound with sutures, and care for it until it heals.
If, however, the nipple descends and softens because of its size, as happens with women, then it is necessary to slit it on its two upper sides with two similar lunate incisions, each contained with the other at the ends of each, until a larger line contains a smaller one, according to this shape from line b. to line g.  Then scrape the skin between the two lips, and remove the fat, and administer what we have said about sutures and powders, which is necessary from the treatment, until it heals.
If, however, you do not complete the incision that you ought to make, due to the patient's discomfort or due to blood flow, then you must fill the wound with a cloth soaked in a corrosive ointment, and leave it until it corrodes the remaining fat. Then care for it until it heals.
Concerning the perforation of an abscess that occurs under the armpit.
Chapter XLVIII.
These abscesses that occur under the armpit are of the type of hard Strophula, which contain cysts, and of those which contain moist humors.
Therefore, those of them that contain moist humors should be incised with a crescent-shaped incision, according to this form, from line b to line g.  When, therefore, all that is in the abscess is evacuated with pus, then fill it with old lint, and leave it until another day.
Then the lint should be removed, and treated with a suitable ointment for it, until it is healed.
If the abscess is of the type of knotted Strophulae, then cut on it, as we said in the section on Strophulae, equally.
If however the wound extends in this way to emit pus, then apply a cautery to it, according to what we said in what was previously stated.
Chapter XLIX.
Concerning the incision of an abscess that occurs due to an artery and vein.
When an artery is ruptured, and the skin above it becomes consolidated, then an abscess often occurs from this: and similarly, a vein also, so that an abscess and swelling occur in it.
And the signs by which it is recognized that the abscess and swelling are due to an artery and vein are: because, when the abscess is due to an artery, it is long, aggregated deep within the body; and when you press the abscess with your fingers, you feel as if there is a creaking sound to it.
And that which is due to a vein, is round on the surface of the body.
And incision upon this abscess is an error, and especially that which is under the armpits, and the groins, and the neck, and in many places of the body: and it is very large. Therefore, its treatment with iron should be avoided, and what is from them also in the extremities, or in the head, should be avoided.
Therefore, for that which is from them, from the swelling of the artery's opening, make an incision in the skin lengthwise. Then open the incision with hooks, afterward the artery; and free it from its tunics, until it is exposed.
Then introduce a needle beneath it, and cause it to penetrate to the other side, and tie the artery with a double thread in two places, according to what I taught you in the extraction of the two arteries which are in the temples.
Then pierce the place that is between the two ligatures with a spatula, until all the blood that is in it exits, and the abscess is resolved.
Then apply a treatment that produces pus, until the ligatures fall off; afterward, treat it with suitable ointments until it heals.
If there be an abscess because of the incision of a vein, then it is necessary that you hold with your hand what you are able of the abscess with the skin. Then introduce a needle beneath the place you held with your hand, and cause it to penetrate; and let a double thread be on it, until you extract it from the other side. Then bind the abscess with a good ligature, according to what I told you in the ligature of the grape, from two sides with two ends of the thread.
If however you fear that the thread will be pulled out, then introduce another needle with another thread under the entire abscess near the incision of the needle; press, and tighten your threads in four places.
Then cut the abscess in its middle, until what is in it comes out; then cut the excess skin, and leave what is bound from it.
Then place upon it the poultice you soaked in wine and oil. Then administer the treatment, which is done with lint and ointments, until it is healed.
Chapter L.
Concerning the abscess that arises from the twisting of nerves.
Just as an abscess occurs in the artery and vein, similarly an abscess occurs in the nerves: which occurs in them through blows, or arises from excessive work, and similar causes; and it happens most often in the places of assistance, or the heel, and in every place where the joints move: and it is a severe abscess.
Its color is similar to the rest of the body, and it is in many cases without pain; and when it is pressed with force, the afflicted person feels a sensation similar to numbness in it.
And it is not a deep-seated, accumulated abscess, rather it is beneath the skin, and it moves to every part, and does not progress forward, or backward.
Therefore, those that are in the joints should not be incised with iron. For perhaps contraction will result.
However, in those that are on the head, or on the forehead, slit the skin with a lancet. If it is a small abscess, then grasp it with forceps, and cut it away from its root.
And if it is large, then suspend it with a hook, and scrape it off; afterwards remove it, and join the wound with stitches; and treat it until it is healed.
Chapter LI.
Concerning the incision of an abscess that occurs in the belly.
Many people frequently have growths in their bellies and other parts of their bodies, which are called Fungalia, by reason of their resemblance to fungi.
Their root is slender, and their head is large, and their lips are turned inward. And some of them are small; and some of them grow very large.
And indeed I myself have seen a man who had two growths in his belly, which were more like fungi than other things: there was no difference between them, white, with slender roots, and their lips turned inward, and split, and moisture flowed from them constantly.
Therefore I cut them, and found in one about 10.8 ounces, and in the other about 6 ounces. And the procedure for cutting them is that, if the patient is moist, and the color of the growth is white, moist, with a slender root: then cut it with a wide spatula, and have cauteries ready in the fire.
For often a great deal of blood is expelled at its incision; therefore, hurry if blood overwhelms you, then cauterize it.
If you see the patient faint and afraid of the incision with the iron, then take a thread of lead, well-made, and bind it with it to the swelling; which method we have described, and leave it for two days.
Then add to the lead bandage. Therefore do not cease binding the lead, as often as it presses on the growth, until it is severed, and falls away of itself without effort.
If however the growth is of large root, then in its incision is some fear and dread, especially that which is in the belly.
However, it is possible to cut half of it. Then cauterize it, so that it does not return.
Then treat it until the wound heals.
And beware that you do not presume to cut a growth that is of dark color, of little feeling, of foul appearance; for it is indeed a cancerous abscess; and I will come with a consideration of Cancer after this.
Chapter LII.
Concerning the treatment of the navel's prominence.
The navel's prominence arises from many causes. Or from fissures in the skin covering the abdomen; and from it emerges wind, or an intestine, according to what happens in other ruptures. Or from blood flowing from a vein or an artery, as previously stated. Or from wind constricted within it.
If however it is due to a fissure in the skin, and the protrusion of a part: then its color resembles the color of the body, and it is smooth, without pain, and appears in various locations. And if it is due to the emergence of an intestine, then its location, along with what we have described, shows a more significant variety. When therefore you press it with your fingers, it hides; then it returns, and perhaps there is a sound with it; and it greatly increases at the entrance of the bath, and with strenuous exertion.
If however it is due to moisture, it is smooth, then it does not hide when you press it with your hand, nor does it contract, nor does it increase.
If however it is due to blood, then with these signs the abscess appears black from the sides.
If however it is due to a formed flesh, then the abscess is severe, large, hard, and fixed to a single size.
If however it is due to windiness, then its touch is smooth.
And the operation on it is, that you should then consider, if the protrusion of the navel is due to blood of artery or vein, or windiness: then you should refrain from its cure. For in it there is fear and terror, as I taught you in the chapter where I said abscesses due to artery and vein.
If the protrusion of the navel is due to the intestine or hernia, then you should command the sick person to hold his breath and stand stretched out. Then mark with ink around the entire navel. Afterwards, tell him to lie on his back in your hands. Then surround with a broad spatula the area around the navel over the place you marked with ink. Afterwards, extend the middle of the abscess upwards with a large hook. Then bind it firmly around with a thread, or with a silken cord of strong ligament: and let the knot of the ligament be tied.
Then open the middle of the abscess that has been extended above the ligament. If therefore you find it already grasped by the ligament, then pull out the tied part, and insert your index finger into it, and push the intestine inwards towards the belly; and if you find a fold, then take it with a hook and cut off its excess.
If, however, the artery or vein is contrary to you, then surround it much. Then return to your operation. And take two needles, and insert into them two strong threads; and insert the two needles around the circumference you have made around the abscess in the manner of a cross, making them penetrate.
Then tighten the abscess in four places above the needles; and if you wish, remove the needles and leave the place until the extended flesh rots and falls away on its own; or cut it when it becomes thin and rots.
Then treat it with what is necessary of ointments until it is healed.
If the protrusion of the navel is from flesh growing in it, or from moisture, then it is necessary to hollow out the abscess, as we have said. And extract the blood or moisture that you find in it.
Then treat it with that which hardens the place.
Chapter LIII.
Concerning the treatment of CANCER.
We have already spoken in the division of the types of Cancer, and how there is a way to treat it with medicines, and caution in treating it with iron, so that it is not ulcerated: and we have spoken of Cancer generated in the womb, and caution in treating it.
And the ancients said that sometimes Cancer is in a place where its eradication is possible, such as Cancer in the nipples or in the thigh, and similar parts, by removing the entire tumor, especially when it is small and beginning.
However, when it is old and large, one should not approach it.
For I could not heal anyone from it, nor did I see anyone before me who had succeeded in that.
And the treatment for it, when it is situated as we have said, is to proceed and relieve the afflicted person from black cholera at times. Then, if you find it with evident fullness, perform phlebotomy.
Then prepare the patient with the preparation in which he will be positioned for the operation.
Then apply the hooks suitable for the Cancer. Afterwards, hollow it out from every side with the skin, according to the probing, until nothing remains of its roots: and let the blood flow, and do not cut it quickly, rather express the areas, and wipe away all the thick blood with your hand, or with any instrument possible there.
If, however, a conflict occurs for you with the flow of thick blood from the incision of the artery, or vein: then cauterize the vein, until the blood is cut off. Then treat it with the remaining treatment, until it is healed.
Concerning the treatment of Dropsy.
Chapter LIII. We have already recounted in the division of the species of Dropsy, and how the collection of water occurs: and the signs of each species, and its treatment with medicines.
And that which is treated with iron is only a species of ascites alone: and it is not approached with iron in the species of tympanitis: nor in the species of hyposarca at all; for that is fatal.
When you have already treated this species of tympanitis Dropsy with medicines, and your treatment has not been successful, then consider. For if weakness has already overtaken the patient, or there is another illness with him besides dropsy, such as if there be cough with him, or looseness of the belly, and things similar to them, then beware of treating him with iron, because there is fear of it.
If however you see a patient of exuberant strength, and there is no illness with him other than dropsy alone, and he is neither a child nor an old man, then the method of operation in him is that you raise the patient standing between your hands, and a servant behind him will express his belly by pushing the water to the lower part of the chest.
Then take a pointed spatula, according to this form. Let it be of two sharp parts, long, with sharp ends like a spatula. However, let there be something of shortness gradually, so that you do not proceed with it in the operation all the way to the intestines, and injure him.
Then consider. For if there is generation of dropsy from the intestines, then it is necessary that you extend the incision from the navel a quantity of three fingers, downwards in a straight line above the pubis.
If however the generation of dropsy is due to a disease of the liver, then let your incision be to the left of the navel by three fingers.
And if its generation is due to the spleen, then let the incision be on the right side; and it is also not necessary that the incision be on the side on which the sick person wishes to lie, so that superfluities do not flow to that weak place.
Then pierce through the entire skin with an instrument. Then introduce it into that incision, and raise your hand with the spatula between the skin, and do so, as if you were to scrape it; and let the amount that is scraped be the amount of a fingernail, or approximately that.
Then pierce, doing so until the spatula reaches the empty space: and that is the space of the water. Then withdraw it, and introduce into the hole an instrument of this form. And it is similar to a cannula: it is made of silver, or bronze, or brass, smooth, polished; on whose lower part is a small hole, and on its sides are three holes, two on one side, and one on the other side; and the end of it is cut, according to the manner of a reed's incision, as you see; on its upper end is a ring.
For when the instrument reaches the water, then it itself descends immediately upon the instrument, therefore evacuate from the water in an hour a moderate quantity.
Because if you evacuate more from it than is necessary, perhaps the sick person dies in an hour by the resolution of his animal spirit; or a swoon happens to him that approaches death.
But evacuate from it according to the quantity of its virtue, and according to what the sick person's condition signifies to you from the strength of his pulse, and from his well-being.
Then extract the instrument, and retain the water.
That is, because it is immediately retained due to the skin that retains the opening which is above the sifac: about which I narrated, that you pierce it according to that method.
Then again use the instrument on the following day, if you see the sick person can tolerate it; and also extract a small amount from the water.
Do that for several days without fear, and guard yourself from error, until little remains of the water.
However, if you fear for the sick person and you remove a large quantity of water, then treat him with burial in warm sand, and with copious sweating in a bath and sun, and let him endure thirst; and treat him with drying medicines, until he is healed; and cauterize above the stomach, and the liver, and the spleen, after the extraction of water, according to what I have narrated in what preceded.
Concerning the treatment of infants born in places where their urinary passages are not perforated.
Chapter LV.
When any infant emerges from its mother's womb, and its urinary opening is not perforated, or the opening is narrow, or is in the wrong place.
Therefore, it is necessary to aid with its perforation immediately when it is born, using a very fine Spatumili, according to this method.
Then place a fine leaden peg in the opening, and tie it; and hold it for three days, or four.
When, therefore, it wishes to emit urine, it is removed, and it urinates. Then replace it.
And if you do not place the lead, it is possible that the urine which flows over the place will not allow it to close.
However, the one whose opening is constricted is medicated with lead, as we have said, for many days, until it is widened.
However, those in which the opening is in an incorrect place are harmed. That is because, of those who are born, the opening is at the end of the penis: wherefore they cannot urinate forward, until they raise the penis with their hand upward. Nor is there generation for this reason, because the semen cannot reach the uterus in a straight line: and it is a very foul sickness.
And the method of operation in that case is that the sick person be laid on their back. Then extend his penis with your left hand forcefully, and cut the head of the penis from the place of the glans, with a sharp blade, or a sharp scalpel, just as one would cut a reed or shave something, so that its middle resembles the head of the penis, and so that the opening comes into the middle in the way that it ought to.
And beware of blood flow during your operation. For it often happens. Therefore, apply to it whatever stops the bleeding, and treat the wound until it is healed.
Concerning what happens in the foreskin and head of the penis, and corruption, blackness, and adhesion.
Chapter LVI.
These afflictions frequently occur in the penis, and there is a prominence of foul flesh.
And from it some are malignant, and others not malignant.
Therefore, the non-malignant one should be suspended with a fine hook and cut away, until the entire thing is cleansed. Then place over it a poultice soaked in Egyptian unguent. Then treat it afterward with the unguent of dyafinicon, until it is healed.
If, however, it is malignant, of foul color, then it is necessary to apply a cautery to it after its incision and scraping.
If, however, the Albather is in the foreskin of an uncircumcised captive, and a part of the Albather is within the foreskin and a part of it outside, then it is necessary to remove the Albather, which is inside first, until when it is consolidated, then treat it externally; because when you treat it simultaneously, the foreskin is not safe from being pierced.
And they also occur in the testicles, and in the foreskin, blackness and corruption. Therefore, it is necessary to cauterize all that is already blackened and that wishes to corrupt, or that is already corrupted.
Then apply over it, after that, a paste made of honey mixed with the ground flour of pomegranate bark and pounded herbs.
Then treat it with the rest of the treatment until it is healed.
If however a flow of blood occurs, then apply a cautery with a lunar cautery, according to this form.
For cautery is helpful for two conditions simultaneously, namely for a flow of blood and a wound when it is corrupted.
If the head of the penis is corroded and destroyed entirely within an hour, then it is necessary to insert a lead cannula into the urethra so that the patient may urinate over it.
However, when the foreskin adheres to the head of the penis, and this adhesion occurs only in those whose foreskin is healthy and does not require circumcision, and sometimes it happens due to a wound or abscess: therefore, it is necessary to scrape it with a blunt Spatumili until the ligament is loosened and the head of the penis is freed from all sides.
If it is difficult to discern these things precisely, then it is necessary to scrape some of the head of the penis and foreskin. This is because when the foreskin is thin, it might perhaps be perforated quickly due to its thinness.
Then separate the foreskin and the head of the penis with a thin linen cloth, which has already been soaked in cold water, so that they do not adhere again.
Then treat it with astringent wine until it is consolidated.
Chapter LVII.
Concerning the cleansing of infants and the treatment of errors that befall them.
Circumcision is not a solution of continuity, like other wounds; however, since it is by our operation, by our will, and its use is proper to children, it is necessary that we signify in it a better operation and a smoother path leading to health.
Therefore, I say that the ancients did not mention circumcision in any of their books, since they do not use it in their laws, and there is nothing about it except what we have acquired through experience.
This is because I have found the majority of surgeons and phlebotomists using cleansing, circumcising with a scalpel and scissors, and using ligatures and tying with thread and incisions with a knife.
And I myself have already tested all these methods, and have not found a better cleansing than with scissors and a ligature with thread.
Because in cleansing with a scalpel, the skin often slips away. Since the skin of the foreskin has two layers, therefore perhaps the upper skin is incised, and the lower layer remains; therefore, it is necessary to make an incision to cut the other, and future pain will result. And with the cleansing that is done with forceps, that is, digital, which the surgeons use, the incision of the extremity of the male member is not secured, because perhaps it enters its opening.
In cleansing with a knife, perhaps the instrument slips, and corrupts your operation; and the skin of the boy is of short nature, and often they are born such that they do not require cleansing; and I have already seen that.
However, cleansing with scissors, and ligation with thread, has shown me a good method: because scissors are proportional to the incision, because the sharpness that is above is like the sharpness that is below. Therefore, when you press your hand, the two sharpnesses are proportional, and they cut according to one operation at one time: and a compression of similar shape is made, containing the skin of the penis on all sides, with which there is no error at all.
And the method of the proper operation is, that you should make the boy, especially if he is one of those who understand a little, understand that you do not do anything but tie a thread to his penis: and you leave him until another day.
Then make him rejoice and be happy in every way possible to you, from that which his intellect receives. Then make him stand between your prepared hands, and let him not be seated: and hide the scissors in your sleeve, or under your foot, so that the boy sees neither them nor anything of the instruments at all.
Then insert your hand into his penis, and blow into the skin, and pull it upward until the head of the penis emerges. Then cleanse it of the filth that gathers on it.
Then tie the marked place with a double thread. Afterwards, tie a second ligature a little lower. Then hold the lower ligature site with your index finger and thumb, with good retention, and cut between the two ligatures. Then lift the skin upwards quickly, and pull out the rod's head. Then let the blood run slowly (for this is better), and put powder on top; and on top of the powder a cloth on which is spread the yolk of a hard-boiled egg in rosewater, mixed with good fresh rose oil; and leave it on it until the next day; and treat it with the rest of the treatment, until it is healed.
The form of the scissors for cleaning is this.
Let the two ends be blunt and inclined; so that a nail fragment does not protrude from them; and the length of the two sharpened parts should be equal to the length of the two handles.
A mistake, however, occurring in cleaning is: that perhaps the inner skin is completely or partially escaped at the incision.
Therefore, it is necessary that you extend it from your hour with your fingernail before the abscess of the place; and cut it according to equality.
If you cannot hold it with your fingernail, then pull it out with a hook and cut it.
If, however, three days have passed, and that which is under the rod remains swollen, abscessed: then leave it until the abscess is ripe, and carefully peel it away, and cut it according to what has been prepared for you: and beware of the head of the rod. For it will not harm it.
Therefore, treat it with what consolidates wounds from the powders, which we have narrated in the treatise on powders: and if it is incised from the skin beyond measure, and escapes upwards, that will not harm it either.
Therefore, treat it with what we have said, until it is healed.
Chapter LVIII.
Concerning the extraction of retained urine in the bladder.
Retention of urine in the bladder occurs from obstruction caused by a stone, or congealed blood, or pus, or a grown flesh, and similar things to those which we have mentioned from the methods of treatment in the division.
And if the urine is not released, and you see that its retention is due to a stone which is already in the neck of the bladder, then it is necessary for him to be bent over his knees. Then let his feet be placed upon his back, and let him stretch his abdomen from the upper part of his back, and let the sick man strain his breath as much as possible. Thus it is forced with the neck of the bladder to expel the stone backwards: wherefore the urine is released.
If it is not released with what we have said, and the matter is severe on the sick person, then it is necessary that you administer its extraction with an instrument, which is called a syringe, of which this is the form: It should be of silver, and be thin, smooth, concave, like a bird's feather quill in thinness, a length like one and a half handspans; to which there should be a thin tube at its head.
And the method of the urine's exit through it is, that you take a doubled thread, and tie to its end wool, or tow, a good ligature; and you introduce the thread's end into the lower part of the syringe; and cut with scissors if any excess of wool remains, but introduce it into the tube with force, as with a lever.
Then anoint the syringe with oil, or with butter, and the white of an egg; and make the sick person sit upon a seat, and embrocate the bladder, and its urethra with moist oils, and olive oil, and lukewarm water.
Then introduce the syringe into the urethra with ease, until it reaches the root of the urethra.
Then double it upwards from the navel.
Then force the syringe within it, until it reaches the bladder, and let the sick person feel it has now reached the empty place; and this is not done according to this order, unless because the passage, in which it goes, is in which there is twisting.
Then extend the thread with wool gradually. For urine will follow the wool.
Then draw it out, and cause the urine to run through the urine passage; and again syringe, and do not cease to do it, until the bladder is emptied, and the sick person finds relief.
Concerning the quality of bladder clysterization with a Clepsydra.
Chapter LIX.
When a sore occurs in the bladder, or blood is clotted in it, or pus is collected in it, and force is used to pour water and medicines into it: then let it be done with the instrument called Clepsydra, and this is its form.
For it is deaf, that is, closed. And in it are three openings, two on one side, and one on the other side, as you see.
And the hollow place, in which that which propels it is situated, shall be according to the quantity that fills it without addition, until when something is drawn with it, moisture is drawn out: and when it is propelled, it expels it to a distance; according to what the clepsydra does, with which naphta, that is, Greek fire, is projected in naval battles.
Therefore, when force is used to project the moisture into the bladder, introduce the extremity of the clepsydra into the moisture, and draw with that which propels it upward, for moisture is drawn into the concavity of the clepsydra.
Then introduce its extremity into the tube, according to what we have described in the syringe. Then propel the moisture with that which propels it, for that moisture will reach the bladder immediately, so that the sick person feels it.
A slender form of clyster, by which the bladder is cleansed.
Let it be made of silver, or brass: whose head be higher, resembling a small alca, and beneath it a sinus into which a ligature may fall.
Then take a ram's bladder, and place in it the moisture with which you wish to cleanse the bladder. Then tie it between two sinuses with a strong ligature made of doubled thread; and warm that moisture a little over the fire.
Then insert the end of the clyster into the tube, then squeeze your hand over the bladder with a suitable constriction, until the sick person feels that the moisture has already reached the bladder.
If, however, you do not have a bladder present, then take a piece of parchment, and make a circle from it, and pierce it all around its circumference. Then insert a strong thread into those holes, as they revolve. Then bring the circle together, as a vessel is brought together; after you place in it whatever you want of moistures, and oils, and waters.
Then tie it to the instrument, and do with it as you did with the bladder by squeezing with your hand, until the moisture reaches the bladder.
Chapter LX.
Concerning the extraction of the Stone.
We have already discussed the division of the types of stone, and its treatment with medicines; and I have described the difference between the stone generated in the kidneys and the bladder, and the signs of each: and I have explained the stone in which operation with the hand and incision is necessary, and it is the stone generated in the bladder properly; and the one that adheres to the urethra; and I am narrating it with clear explanation and brevity.
I say therefore, that stone generated in the bladder, most often occurs in children.
And among its signs is that the urine exits from the bladder like water in its fineness, and white sand appears in it, and the infirm person rubs their urethra, and suffers with it, and often hangs down. Then it is erected, and the anus emerges with it in many of them.
And the cure is easy for children, as long as they reach fourteen years. And it is difficult in the elderly. However, in young people, it is moderate, in that which is between those things: and the cure is easier for him whose stone is larger; and if it is small, the cure is conversely difficult.
Therefore, when we come to the treatment, it is necessary first to cleanse the sick person with an enema, which will remove all the stool that is in their intestines; for it sometimes hinders the finding of the stone during examination.
Then let the sick person be taken with their feet, and shaken and moved downwards, so that the stone descends to the depth of the bladder, or leaps from a high place sometimes.
Then make him sit between your hands prepared, and let his hands be under his thighs, so that the entire bladder becomes inclined downwards.
Then examine him and touch him externally.
If therefore you feel a stone in the space, then immediately proceed with incision upon it.
If however it does not fall under your touch at all, then you must wipe your index finger with the oil of your left hand, if the patient is a child, or your middle finger if he is a fully grown young man, and insert it into his anus and examine for the stone, until it is under your finger, and gradually turn it towards the neck of the bladder.
Then press upon it with your finger, and drive it outwards against the place whose incision you wish, and command a servant to press the bladder with his hand, and command another servant to extend the testicles upwards with his right hand, and with his other hand to remove the skin that is under the testicles on the side of the place where the incision is.
Then introduce a spatula, the shape of which is this. And find it in the space between the anus and the testicles, and not in the middle, to the left of the buttocks, and let the incision be over the stone, and your finger be in the anus, and let the incision be transverse, so that the incision be wide externally and narrow internally, according to the amount that it may be possible for the stone to exit through it no larger.
For perhaps the finger, which is in the anus, compresses the stone at the incision, and it exits without difficulty.
And know that if the stone has angles and edges, therefore its exit becomes difficult because of that.
And if it is smooth, like a gland, and round, then its exit is easy.
Therefore, to the one which has angles and edges, add in the fissure.
If it does not exit thus, then it is necessary that you devise above it: either that you grasp it above with a suitable instrument, whose extremity is like the Moon, which grasps the stone and does not slip from it; or that you introduce under it a slender instrument with a curved extremity.
If however you cannot above it, then enlarge the opening a little. If something from the blood overcomes you, cut it off with a zegi.
If however the stones are more than one, then first drive the large one towards the bladder's opening. Then cut above it. Afterwards drive the small one after that: and similarly do if there are more than two.
If it is very large, then ignorance it is to cut above it with a large incision. Because it happens to the sick person one of two things, either that they die or that a constant dripping of urine occurs to them: because the place is not completely consolidated.
But administer its expulsion, until it comes out: or make incisions in its fracture with scissors, until you extract it piece by piece.
When therefore you have completed your operation, then fill the wound with frankincense, and aloe, and dragon's blood: and bind it, and place upon it a cloth soaked in oil and wine, or rose oil, and cold water: so that a hot abscess might settle.
Then lay the sick person on their back, and do not loosen the bandage, until the third day. When therefore you have loosened it, embrocate the place with water and much oil.
Then treat it with dyafinicon ointment, and basilicon ointment, until it is healed.
If however in the wound a hot abscess is added, and corrosives, and similar things: such as if blood should congeal in the bladder, and the urine be prevented from it: and this is recognized from the passing of blood with the urine. Therefore insert your finger into the wound, and extract the blood. For if it remains, it provokes corruption of the bladder and its putrefaction.
Then wash the wound with vinegar, water, and salt, and apply whatever is appropriate from the treatments, until it is healed.
And it is necessary in the entire period of treatment, to tie the two hips, and to apply, for the sake of the medicines that are placed on the place.
If the stone is small, and is in the passage of the penis, and is fixed in it, and prevents the exit of urine: then treat it with what I mention before you reach the incision. For often you will be spared the incision by this treatment, for I have already proven it.
And it is, that you take a perforator of iron, whose shape is this, triangular end, pointed, fixed in wood.
Then take thread, and tie the penis with it under the stone so that it does not return to the bladder.
Then introduce the piercing iron into the penis with ease, until the perforator reaches the stone itself: and turn the perforator with your hand on the stone gradually: and try to pierce it, until you make it penetrate on the other side.
For the urine is discharged immediately.
Then press your hand upon that which remains of the stone from the outside of the penis. For it will break, and exit with the urine, and the sick person is cured.
However, if this treatment is not prepared for you, because of something that prevents it: then tie a thread under the stone, and another thread above the stone. Then cut above the stone, on the penis itself, between the two ligatures. Then extract it. Afterwards, loosen the ligature, and cleanse the congealed blood, which forms in the wound.
And the ligature of thread under the stone is not necessary, unless so that it does not return to the bladder; and the other ligature above, unless so that, when the thread is loosened after the stone's expulsion, the skin returns to its place and covers the wound; and for that reason it is necessary for you to raise the skin upwards, when you tie the upper thread, so that it returns to its proper position and covers the wound, as we said.
Chapter LXI.
Concerning the extraction of the stone in women.
Little stone is generated in women.
However, if a stone happens to any of them, then the cure is difficult, and it is forbidden because of many ways.
One of them is that the woman is perhaps a virgin.
And the second is that you do not find a woman who reveals herself to a doctor when she is chaste, or from those having husbands.
The third is that you do not find a woman well versed in this art, especially the operation with the hand.
And the fourth is the distance from the location of the stone: therefore it needs a deep incision, and in that is fear.
If necessity provokes it, then it is necessary that you take a female physician, well-versed: and few are found.
If however you entrust it to her, then seek a chaste, skilled physician, and present a skilled female midwife knowledgeable in women's matters, or a woman who hints at part of the art in this skill: make her present, and especially for her, do all that you order her from the inquiry about the stone.
First of all, that he should look to see if she is a virgin: then it is necessary that he insert his finger into her anus, and search for the stone.
If therefore he finds it, he should confine it under his finger, and then instruct the midwife to insert her finger into the vagina of the infirm woman, and inquire about the stone, after placing her left hand on the bladder and compressing it with a good compression.
If therefore he finds the stone, then it is necessary that he gradually move it from the orifice of the bladder downwards with all his strength, until he reaches it with the root of the thigh.
Then cut upon it at the midpoint of the vulva near the root of the thigh, from whichever side it is prepared for him, and feels the stone in that part: and his finger not to be removed from the stone, confining it underneath: and let the incision be small at first. Then insert a reed over that incision.
Then insert a reed over that incision. If therefore he feels the stone, then let him add to the incision according to the proportionate amount that the stone emerges from it.
And know that the kinds of stone are many. For of these there is a small one, and a large one, a smooth one and a rough one, a long one, and a round one having branches.
Know therefore its species, so that this may signify to you concerning that which you wish.
If therefore blood conquers you, then add in that place crushed zegi, and hold it one hour, until the blood is severed.
Then return to your operation, until the stone emerges; and do so, preparing for yourself from the instruments, which I said in the extraction of the stone in men, so that you may assist with them in your operation.
If, however, a flow of blood conquers you, and you know that the expulsion of blood is from an artery that is cut: then place powder upon the spot, and bind it with bandages of suitable constriction; and do not change that; and leave the stone, and do not extract it. Perhaps, indeed, the weak woman will die. Then treat the wound.
When, therefore, the sharpness of the blood subsides after days, and the place putrefies, then return to your operation, until the stone emerges.
Chapter LXVIII.
Concerning the incision of a watery hernia.
A watery swelling is not present unless there is a gathering of moisture in a white sifac, which is under the skin of the testicle containing the egg: and it is called sifac.
And sometimes it is in its own membrane, which nature has prepared for it in the part of the egg, until it becomes putrid: which is another egg, and there is skin of the testicle between the white sifac which we mentioned; and this does not happen except rarely.
And the generation of this swelling indeed is from the weakness which happens to the testicles; wherefore this matter is poured out to them. And sometimes it happens from a blow upon the testicles.
And this moisture has many colours. For either its colour inclines to yellowness, or it is blood-red, or foul, black, or watery white, and that is what is more.
And the signs by which it is known where the aggregation of water is, are these. For if it is in the white sifac we have mentioned, then the abscess is round in length somewhat like the shape of an egg: and the testicle does not appear, because the moisture contains it from every side.
And if the moisture is in the proper covering to itself, then the abscess is round with part of an egg, and because of this a man estimates that it is another egg.
And if the moisture is between the skin of the testicles and the white sifac, then it falls under the senses.
Therefore, when you desire knowledge of the color of the moisture, then experience the color of the moisture in the abscess with the inserted square, whose form preceded. Therefore, according to that which emerges in the trace of the Insertion, judge of that which is between it.
When, therefore, we come to the treatment with iron, then it is necessary that you command the sick person to be bled, if it is possible for him, and you see his body full.
Then let him be laid upon his back upon a slightly raised object, and place many cloths beneath him.
Then sit you upon his left side, and command the attendant to sit on his right, who shall extend his rod to one of the two sides of the scrotum skin, and to the part of the navel of the belly.
Then take a broad spatula and cut the skin of the scrotum from the middle lengthwise, up to near the pubic bone; and let the incision be made along the straight equidistant line that divides the scrotum skin, until it reaches the white, encompassing sifac: and scrape it and observe, or beware of this, that you sever it; and let your scraping, by which you scrape it, be from the part that hangs with the egg more; and delicately examine with the scraping according to the amount that is possible for you.
Then pierce the sifac full of water with a wide perforation, and extract all the water.
Then separate the two edges of the incision with hooks, and extend the sifac upward, and do not touch the skin of the scrotum encompassing it: and cut the sifac however it is possible for you to cut it, either with its entirety, or in fragments, especially its thin side. For if you do not carefully investigate, there is no security in its incision, lest the water return.
If, however, the egg protrudes externally from its skin during the time of your operation, then when you have completed the incision of the sifac, replace it. Then bring together the two edges of the scrotal skin with sutures.
Then treat it like other wounds, until it heals.
If you find a corrupted egg due to another blackness, then it is necessary to tie the vessels, which are the suspensory ones, fearing a flow of blood.
Then sever the testicle with its suspensory ligament, and remove the egg; afterward, treat it with what we have said.
If however water is collected in two parts, then know that there are two branches: therefore, cut the other part similarly to what you did to the other one.
And if it is equal to you, so that it is one operation, then do it; then wipe away the blood. And introduce into the sections wool soaked in oil, or in rose oil; and place outside another wool which you have soaked in wine and oil, and spread it over the two testicles and the lower belly; and place over it doubled cloths, and they are pillows; and tie them above with a bandage, which has six sections in this form.
Then take two cloths, and sew them together, and fill them with dyed wool, according to this likeness; and let the edges with which it is pressed be made of linen or wet wool; and let them be gathered with a constriction at the lumbar region of the breeches; and one of the edges shall take in from the anus up to the back, and the second edge shall take in from the side of the pubic bone; and two edges over the two thighs below, and two other edges over the two groins, and the whole constriction shall be gathered in the lumbar region of the breeches.
And sometimes this branch is artificially treated with a cautery in place of the incision with iron; and it is that you take a thin, knife-like cautery, and cut with it the skin of the testicles; and it is heated, as we have described; until when a white substance containing water is revealed; then take the cautery, according to this form.
It is similar to the Greek letter.
Then pierce it with that, and let it be heated, until all the moisture comes out.
Then stretch the sifac with hooks, and scrape it with a heated thin cautery, and cut it as much as possible, until you remove the whole of it.
And beware lest the fire touch the egg.
And know that this operation is safer, and further from blood flow, and better than an operation with a cut.
If, however, contrariety happens to you during the hour of cutting, or during the hour of cautery, a hot abscess, or blood flow, or anything else: then it is necessary that you cure whatever contrariety happens to you from it with the cure that suits it, according to what has been stated before.
If, however, the patient is fearful and does not await this operation, because of its horror: then administer perforation as I describe it: and it is so.
When it is certified to you by what we said about the signs that the limb is watery, then it is necessary that you make the patient sit on a raised seat.
Then perforate the abscess in its lower part with a wide spatula: and place the perforation along the length of the body, and let the opening be wide, until all the water runs out.
Then place a poultice over the affected area, and bind it, and leave it so that the remaining water runs out; then treat it until the wound heals.
If, however, there is an obstruction to the escape of the water at the time you perforate it, and this is only because the white membrane causes obstruction in the wound opening and prevents the water from exiting, then it is necessary that you insert a pen into the wound, or an instrument with which the water of the dropsy is drawn out, of which the form preceded; or add a little to the wound treatment.
And know that the water returns, and gathers again after six months, or about that time. When it therefore gathers again, then it must be perforated in the same way, and the patient will pass his days.
Chapter LXIII.
Concerning the treatment of fleshy tumors.
Know that in the incision upon this tumor, there is fear leading to harm in many dispositions. Therefore, it seems to me that its release is to be saved from it.
And I am remembering the operation on it, and the appearance of the abscess.
I say therefore that many abscesses occur in the bodies from which the testicles are composed; and it happens from many causes, namely.
Or from an acute or acrid superfluity which is poured forth to the testicles. Or from a blow.
And the color of the abscess is according to the color of the body, and no pain is found in it; and it is a serious abscess.
And perhaps it is a stony abscess, whose color is dark, and there is no sensation in it.
