And sometimes it arises from a knotting of the arteries and their swelling, as its mention has already preceded, or from a swelling of the veins.
Therefore, that which arises from the swelling of the arteries is recognized by the disposition of the abscess when you press it with your fingers. And you should not touch it entirely.
But that which arises from the swelling of the veins, nothing spreads from it upon compression with the fingers.
And it is possible that a section be made upon this type, and upon the other flesh: and it is like cutting the skin of the testicles. Then raise the egg upwards, and pull it out with a white sifach: and release the suspensory from the vessels, and tie the vessels: and cut the suspensory after you have freed it from every part from the parts of the egg.
If therefore the egg is consolidated with those born flesh, then it is necessary to pull the egg and cut it.
And if there is adherence between some of the siphaket, or in that which is between the vessels, then it is necessary to release all that adherence, and cut it with a round incision.
If however there is a birth of flesh in the place of adherence which is behind, then it is necessary to cut all of it, and extract the egg, as we have said.
When therefore your operation is complete, then fill the wound with wool infused in rose oil and wine; then treat it with the rest of the treatment, until it is healed.
Chapter LXIII.
Of the treatment of Ramicis, which is with the Vite.
Uitis is an abscess enveloped by a certain covering, similar to a bunch of grapes, with softening of the testicles; and it makes difficult for the infirm person movement and exercise, and walking.
And in the treatment of this illness there is fear, because of that which preceded it, remembering.
But it is necessary, as we say, to perform the operation on it according to what the first ones did.
I say therefore, that it is necessary to make the infirm person sit upon a raised seat. Then push down the suspensory of the testicles. Then grasp the skin of the testicles with your fingers, along with the vessels that are close to the penis; and let another attendant hold them, and stretch them with a forceful extension.
Then, cut with a broad, sharp spatula, a transverse incision directly over the vessels, until the vessels are exposed.
Then, scrape from every side, as we told you in the extraction of the arteries that are in the temples.
Afterwards, insert a needle with a double thread into them, and tie it in the first of the places where the veins occur: and tie it again in their last one. Then cut it in the middle, straight, along the length of the body, and remove what is aggregated from the putrid, feculent humors.
Then treat the wound with the treatment of the other wounds, in which the force generates pus, so that the vessels that nourish one testicle fall. For it is not evil through it.
However, if it happens to all the vessels, then it is necessary that the testicles be removed along with the vessels: so that the egg is not deprived of nourishment, on account of the incision, and withers, or dries up, and there is no benefit with them.
Chapter LXV.
Concerning the treatment of intestinal colic.
The outcome of this colic is from a section, which occurs in the distended sifach above the belly in the groin region from the mirach of the belly.
Why do the intestines descend from that rupture to one testicle. And this rupture is either from the sifach, or from its extension.
And these two types occur from many causes: either from a blow, or a jump, or a shout, or the lifting of a heavy object, and similar things.
And the sign, when it is from the extension of the sifach. It is that which happens gradually over a long time, and does not happen suddenly: and it is an abscess equal towards the depth; because the sifach compresses the intestine.
And the sign of it, when it is from the section of the sifach, is that it causes a sudden, intense pain at the beginning; and the abscess is different, appearing under the skin nearby; and that is because of the exit of the intestine, and its course outwards through the sifach.
And sometimes it emerges with the intestine as a zirbum; and this is called a Ramex intestinalis, and zirbia; and sometimes it happens with flatulence.
And sometimes faeces run into the intestine and are retained there; and with it there is harm to the sick person, since it causes a foul pain, and especially when pressed.
And in the treatment of these types of illnesses, there is fear with iron; therefore, a fall must be avoided in it.
And the method of operation is to instruct the sick person to draw back the intestine with his own hand into the inner cavity of his body, if it submits to being drawn back. Then he should be laid back on his neck between your hands, and raise his legs.
Then extend the skin that follows the skin upwards, and cut the skin of the testicles all the way along its length. Then fix hooks in the two lips of the incision, according to what you need to open the incision with them; and let the incision be according to the size that it is possible for the egg to emerge from it.
Then scrape the siphak, and what is beneath the skin of the testicles, until when the white, hard siphak is uncovered on every side; then insert your index finger into that which follows the testicle, into that which is between the white siphak, which is beneath the skin of the egg, and the second siphak, and sever with it the adhesion which is on the back part of the egg.
Then move the skin of the testicles inwards with your right hand, and with this extend the white siphak upwards with your left hand, and lift the egg with the siphak towards the part of the incision; and command the servant to extend the egg upwards; and you sever the adhesion that is behind the completed severance.
And check with your fingers that there is nothing of the intestine wrapped around the hard white siphak. If however you find any of it, then push it towards the lower belly.
Then take a needle, in which is a thick thread, made of linen from ten threads; and insert it at the last siphak, that is, the tunic, which is beneath the skin of the testicles that follows the incision; then cut the ends that project from the thread until there are four threads. Then arrange some of them over some others with a figure that is in the shape of a cross; and tie with them the alsifach, of which we spoke, because it is beneath the skin of the testicles by a strong ligature, until no vessel that nourishes them can send anything to them, so that a hot abscess does not arise from it. And place another ligature outside the first ligature, distant from it by less than two fingers. And after these two ligatures, leave from the siphak, which is beneath the skin of the testicles, a quantity the size of a finger; and cut the rest entirely around, and remove the egg from it.
Then cut in the lower part of the skin of the testicles, by the incision from which blood and pus flow, as we have related in what was said before.
Then apply wool soaked in oil, and place it in the wound; and apply the ligature that we have described. And sometimes the white siphak that we mentioned is cauterized after its incision, fearing a flow of blood. For this often happens. And leave the ligature until it falls off by itself.
But if its falling is delayed, then it should be soaked with warm water, and place upon it that which we have mentioned, until it falls off.
Then treat the wound with the rest of the treatment for wounds, until it is healed.
Chapter LXVI.
Concerning the windy Ramice.
In this Ramice, I have seen no one who dared to use iron for its treatment.
And the first ones said it is like what we have described in the Ramice that is with the Vite. That is, to tie the vessels after the incision, to place them below with ease; then cut in the middle; and lastly, treat with that which opens it, until the vessels fall; and treat the wound, as we said, until it is healed.
Chapter LXVII.
Concerning the rupture that occurs in the groin.
When rupture occurs in the groin, as we said, and the area protrudes, and nothing from the intestines descends into the testicles; and if it does descend, it is little, and returns every hour; but if the condition persists, a fissure is added to the sifach, until the intestines descend into the sifach.
And that occurs from the extension of the sifach, which is in the groin, as we said. That is because the sifach extends; then the area softens, and the place becomes suppurative, and protrudes.
And its treatment is with a cautery, as its preceding description stated.
And sometimes it is treated with an iron, according to this method: and it is that the sick person lies on their back between your hands. Then cut the suppurating and protruding area of the rupture, with a cut across its width, according to the size of three fingers.
Then squeeze the alsifak and those things under the skin until, when it is uncovered, a white sifach is seen, which is under the skin that follows it.
Then take a probe, and place it on the protruding area of the sifach, and press it into the depth of the belly.
Then sew the two protruding areas on the end of the probe from the sifach, and connect one of them to the other with a suture; then pull out the end of the probe.
And do not cut the alsifach completely, nor stretch the egg, nor anything else, as I have made you know in the treatment of intestinal sores.
Then treat it with the treatment for wounds. When therefore you cut the threads, cleanse it and restore the wound until it is healed.
For the sisach contracts, and does not add a prominence of the rupture.
And a cautery is better in this place, because it is closer to health.
Chapter LXVIII.
Concerning the softening of the skin of the testicles.
Often the skin of the testicles softens in some men, on account of which the flesh between them softens, and its appearance becomes unsightly.
Therefore it is necessary for whoever is asked to treat it, to make the sick person lie on his back, and to cut away all the softened skin over the natural skin.
Then he shall bring together the two lips with stitches: and if you wish, stitch first the excess softened skin with three or four stitches, and make the stitch firm.
Then cut between the stitches.
Then treat the rest of the wounds with care, until they are healed, and the stitches fall out.
Chapter LXIX.
Concerning Castration.
Know that castration is excommunicated in our law, and because of that it would be permissible for me not to remember it in this book of mine; and its remembrance is not necessary unless for two ways.
One of which is that it should be in the knowledge of the physician when questioned about it; and so that he may know the treatment for him who has suffered it.
And the other way is that we often need the castration of certain animals, for our benefit by it, such as rams, boars, and cats, and similar animals.
Therefore, I say that castration is done according to two ways. Or by attrition. Or by fissure and section.
In that which is done by attrition, the method of operation is: that we should make the animal sit in warm water, until its testicles soften and relax, and hang down; then rub them with your hand until they dissolve and are no longer apparent to the touch.
In castration by fissure and section, it is necessary that you hold the animal and press the skin of its testicles with your left hand.
Then tie the suspensory, and cut above each testicle with one cut, until two testicles are separated; then cut them after you have flayed each one, and do not leave on them from sifak anything except thin sifak that is over the vessels.
And this method of castration is better than that done by attrition, because attrition perhaps leaves something of the testicles, wherefore the animal desires intercourse.
Then treat the wound until it heals.
Chapter LXX.
Concerning the treatment of a hermaphrodite, there are two methods.
A hermaphrodite is from males in two ways. One of them is because there appears in it, in the space that follows, or in the skin of the testicles, in the area between the two testicles, a shape like a woman's vagina, in which there are hairs; and sometimes urine runs from that which is in the skin of the testicles.
In women, however, there is one type, and it is a woman's vagina above the pubis, like small male testicles, protruding outwards; of which one is like a man's penis, and two like two testicles.
And the treatment of three types, two from men, and one type from women, is: because it is necessary to cut away the added flesh, until the impression of them is gone. Then treat them with the treatment of the rest of the wounds.
In the second type of men, however, from which urine issues, which is in the skin of the testicles, there is no operation, nor is there any cure at all.
Chapter LXXI.
Concerning the incision of the tentigo, and the protruding flesh in women's vaginas.
Tentigo perhaps grows over a natural part, until its appearance becomes ugly and disfigured; and sometimes in some women it becomes so large that it expands like in men, and reaches to the point of coitus.
Therefore, you must hold the excess of the tentigo with your hand, or with a hook, and cut it; and do not proceed too deep in the incision, especially in the deep roots, so that a flow of blood does not occur.
Then treat it with the treatment for wounds, until it heals.
However, the flesh that arises is flesh which grows in the orifice of the womb, until it fills it; and perhaps it extends outwards in the manner of a tail; and on account of that some earlier ones named the illness the tailed sickness. Therefore, it is necessary to cut it, as we cut the tentigo, and to treat it until it is healed.
Concerning the treatment of Alratica, which is the non-perforated vagina of a woman.
Chapter LXXII.
Alratica is, that is, the vagina of a woman that is not perforated, or has a small opening. And it is either natural or accidental.
Accidental is from an illness that preceded: and it indeed happens either from added flesh growth, or a thin or thick sifach; and it occurs either deep within the womb, or on its sides, or above it, or below it; and it hinders intercourse and conception, and childbirth, and perhaps it hinders menstruation.
And knowledge of it is gained through the obstetrician's observation, if an obvious blockage is near.
If however it is not obvious, then seek it with your fingers, or with a probe.
If however the blockage is from a thin sifach, and it is near the two lips: then hurry and break it apart.
And it is, that you place cushions upon the two lips.
Then place your index fingers of both hands together, and let the woman be upon her back with her legs spread. Then extend the two lips with strength, until the thin sifach is broken, and the blockage is opened.
Then take wool, and soak it in oil, and place it over the place: and let the woman do this every day, so that the place is not consolidated another time.
If however the sifach is thick, dense: then it is necessary that you cut it with a broad spatula similar to a myrtle leaf.
If however the blockage is born of flesh, then suspend it with hooks, and cut it: and let there be with you medicines that stop the flow of blood without causing biting, such as acacia, and dragon's blood, and frankincense mixed with the white of an egg.
Then administer a wide lead cannula, so that the wound does not quickly become consolidated, hold it for a few days: and administer linen from dry flax, then treat with the rest of the treatment, until it is healed.
And sometimes there is born in the womb the birth of other flesh: it is therefore necessary that they are cut according to this method: as long as it is not a cancerous abscess, for a cancerous abscess which is in the womb, do not use a contrary action with iron at all.
Chapter LXXIII.
Concerning the treatment of hemorrhoids, Athehelil, and red Bothor in the vulvas of women.
Hemorrhoids are indeed an opening of the veins' orifices, until much blood flows from them constantly.
When hemorrhoids age, they become thelil; and I have already spoken in the division of their types and their signs; and I will say here which of them receive a cure, and which do not.
Therefore I say that hemorrhoids which are in the orifice of the womb, and do not appear to the senses, then there is no cure with iron for them.
However, those of them which are in the orifice of the womb over which sense falls, are those which are cured.
Therefore, it is necessary that you introduce the woman into a cool house. Then, extend the thelil with scissors, or with a rough cloth, and cut them from their roots.
Then powder over them at the flow of blood from one of the powdered incisions without mortification: such as acacia, and dragon's blood, and frankincense, and similar things to them.
Then let him push her feet against the wall for one hour. Afterwards, place her in cold water.
If the flow of blood persists, then have her sit in a decoction of sumac, and quince bark, and gallnuts, and similar things.
Then take barley flour, and make it into a paste with honey and vinegar, and make a plaster with it on her back.
If blood is thereby cut off, it is good; and if not, make cupping adhere to the back and the armpits without incision.
Then take wool, and immerse it in the juice of tarathit, or the juice of arnogloss, or the juice of alaulat branches, that is, aglanter, or Pontic wine, and apply it to the affected area.
When the abscess subsides, then treat the area with ointments until it heals.
Albarth, however, is red, similar to alhaasse heads, and is of harsh appearance.
Therefore, you should cut away what appears from it, according to what I said in the thelil equally; and treat it until it heals.
Chapter LXXIV.
Of the perforation of the exit which occurs in the womb.
Many kinds of abscesses occur in the womb, such as cancer, and stony abscess, and ulcers, and adubelati, and alekilati, that is, herpes, carbuncle, and fistulas, and hemorrhoids, and cracks, and thelil, and hot abscess.
And I have already discussed all these illnesses and their types, and their signs, and their cures in the division: it is necessary that I say in this treatise about the hot abscess which occurs in the womb, when it is from the abscesses that collect pus, and you wish to know how its opening is made with an iron.
Therefore, it is necessary to consider. Then, if the pain of the abscess in its inception is sharp with its percussion, and burning, and fever; and you see it reddening; if the feeling falls upon it, then do not hurry with its perforation; and treat it with that which helps in its maturation, until when the sharpness of the occurrences subsides.
Then it is necessary that you make the woman sit on a seat that slants with two cushions, and lean back, or rest on her back, and draw her feet towards the lower part of her abdomen; and let her thighs be spread open, and place her arms under her knees, and bind each with a suitable bandage to it.
Then let the midwife sit on the right side, and administer the instrument with which the opening of the womb is opened.
And it is necessary when you wish to administer this instrument, that you fix with the radius the depth of the woman's womb; so that it is not that which enters from the instrument more than the depth of the womb, and harms the weak one.
However, if the instrument is more than the depth, then it is necessary that you place cushions on the cover of the womb's depth, so that the entire entry of the instrument into the womb's depth is prevented; and it is necessary that it place a turning mechanism, which runs along the instrument, from the upper side; and so that the minister holds the instrument and turns the mechanism, until the womb's depth is opened.
When therefore the exit appears, and it is touched by the hand, and is soft and gentle, then it is necessary to separate it at its head with a wide spatula.
When therefore the pus is evacuated, then it is necessary to place on the wound a linen cloth soaked in rose oil, or green oil, in which there is some astringency; and place the cloth, and extract the section in the depth of the womb.
And place externally on the womb, and on the pubic hair, clean wool soaked in water in which mallows have been boiled.
Then treat it after two days with an ointment suitable to it, until it is healed.
And sometimes the womb and the wound are washed, so that it is washed out with water and honey; then with water in which the lily root, or long aristolochia, has been boiled; then return to the ointment.
If the wound is hidden inside the womb, then it is necessary that you refrain from treating it with iron, rather treat it with what we said in the division.
Chapter LXX.
Doctrine of Midwives, how living embryos, having emerged in an unnatural manner, are to be treated.
It is necessary for the midwife to first know the form of natural childbirth.
Therefore, among its signs is this: when you see a woman pressing downwards, desiring to smell the air, and feeling relieved from the weight upon her in labor, and the exit of the embryo is hastened, then know that this birth is according to the natural form, and its exit is upon her head, and the afterbirth with it; or suspended, or hanging from the navel.
Therefore, when you see these signs, then it is necessary that her belly be pressed, so that the embryo descends quickly.
For when it descends upon her head, the afterbirth descends with it, and it is cleansed from those superfluous matters by good cleansing.
However, any birth other than this is unnatural, blameworthy.
Because sometimes the fetus emerges upon its feet, and its body emerges before its head, and its feet and hands together, or one foot, or its head and hands, or the belly; and perhaps it is turned upon its neck, and similar, blameworthy forms.
Therefore, it is necessary for the midwife to be prudent, subtle in all these things, and to beware of error and deception; and I am showing every form of them, and how the nature is in it, and how it is signified, and how to stand over it.
When the fetus emerges upon its head in its natural exit, and it becomes forceful upon the woman, and the birth becomes difficult for her, and you see her strength already weakened: then have her sit upon a seat, or let women hold her; and moisten her vulva with decocted fenugreek and with moist oils.
Then let the midwife insert a small spatula between her fingers, and with it split the secundine, and cut it with her fingernail, until that which is in it runs out by moisture; and press the woman's belly, until the fetus descends.
If however it does not descend, then it is necessary for the woman to be given an enema of fenugreek mucilage with sesame oil.
Then command her, after the enema, to compress herself; and make her sneeze with seasonings, and hold her mouth and nose for one hour. For the fetus will immediately emerge.
If the two hands of the fetus emerge first, then it is necessary to gently and gradually return them. If however it does not yield upon entering, then place the woman upon a seat, and elevate her feet upwards; then shake the seat on the ground; and let the woman be held so that she does not fall during the shaking.
If therefore the hands do not emerge, and the fetus dies, then cut them and extract the remaining fetus; and tie its hand with cloth and extract it with evenness; for it will emerge.
The exit of the embryo is upon its feet. When the fetus emerges upon its feet, then it is necessary to push them upwards; then turn the fetus gradually; afterward, straighten it.
Then take one of its feet, and extend it gradually.
When therefore they emerge up to the hips, then order that it be compressed, and make it sneeze with condiments; for it will emerge, if God wills.
And if it does not emerge with what we have recounted, then return the fetus gradually, until you place it in its natural form. For it will then emerge with ease.
If it is prevented with all that we have recounted, then take from marshmallow mucilage, and sesame oil, and dissolved gum, and pound the whole in a mortar with a good pounding; then line with it the vulva of the woman, and the lower part of her abdomen. Then make her sit in lukewarm water, until the hypochondria are reached.
When therefore you see that her lower parts are now warmed, then make her a poultice of myrrh and apply it. When therefore the poultice has held for one hour, then make her sit on a seat. Then make her sneeze; and compress her mouth and nose; and press the lower part of her abdomen with a gentle pressure. For the foetus will depart immediately.
The exit of the foetus is upon the knees and hands. When it emerges in this manner, then gently insert her hands. Then the woman shall be placed on her back, and her feet shall hang downwards; and she shall lie on the bed, and fold her hands. Then press gently on her abdomen. Then draw out what is under her feet from the cushions, until it is prepared in the lower part. If the fetus does not emerge, then take her feet and move them with forceful motion. Then press gently on that which is above the hypochondria, until the fetus ascends upwards. Then let her insert her hands, and gently adjust the fetus; and command the woman to bear down until the fetus emerges.
The exit of the fetus hangs sideways from the hands. His hand be applied in the reduction. If however he cannot reduce it, then raise the woman, and make her walk.
If however she cannot walk, then have her lie on the bed, and shake her legs with a violent shaking.
Then administer the liniment that you prepared from the mucilages.
If she receives it through childbirth, then have her sit on a seat. Then strive in reducing the hand, and arrange the foetus according to its natural form; and make the woman sneeze, and command her to compress herself, until it emerges.
The exit of the foetus is upon her neck, with outstretched hands, and its face is towards the back of its mother. The midwife should hold the foetus's hand, then gradually turn it, and straighten it with ease.
When it is straightened, then place upon its vulva what we have narrated concerning oil and mucilages.
Then command her to move slowly. When you have done that, then make her sit on a seat, and command her to lean to the right side. Then command her to compress it: and make her sneeze with condiments.
The exit of the prepared fetus on its side. When it descends to the opening of the womb in this manner with the afterbirth, then cut the afterbirth with your fingernail until it is evacuated from the moisture.
If therefore it hangs over the face of the fetus, and its neck, then cut it from the navel: so that it is not retained, and dies.
Then push the fetus inward until you place it as it should be: and command the woman to compress herself several times: for it will come out through a light exit.
The exit of twins, or of many fetuses. Know that twins are often born, and sometimes three and four are born. However, that is unusual and rarely happens.
Five, however, are unnatural and do not entirely live: and they are contained, however numerous, by a single afterbirth; and they are separated by a membrane, distinguishing them, tied to the umbilical cord of each of them.
And sometimes they emerge like a single fetus emerges by natural, easy exit; and they emerge with an unpraised exit, as we have narrated.
If some of them are difficult at birth, and reach the unnatural figures we have described, then treat them as we have narrated; and let the midwife be skilled, and let her act with ease and slowness, and be ingenious according to every shape, along with what is prepared for her from the ingenuity leading to health.
A reminder of what is formed in the womb from embryos, and miscarriages.
Sometimes one, two, three, four, five, six, seven, and more than ten are formed in the womb; and it has been verified with me that a woman gave birth to seven, and another to fifteen, all formed, by miscarriage.
Which was done by the measurement of the glorious wise one.
Therefore, all this must be within the knowledge of the Midwife. For perhaps a similar case will come to her; therefore, let her oppose it with that which is necessary.
Chapter LXXVI.
Concerning the extraction of a dead fetus.
When you treat a dead fetus with what I said in the division of medication, and it does not emerge, and you are forced to use an operation with your hand, then it is necessary to consider.
If, therefore, the woman is of sound constitution, and is not afflicted by any of the accidents from which harm is feared for her: then it is necessary that the woman be laid upon a bed, upon her back, and her head be inclined downwards, and her legs raised; and the attendant should hold her from two sides, or she should be bound to the bed, so that her body is not moved during the extraction of the fetus.
Then moisten the mouth of the womb with moist oils, along with the mucilage of marshmallow, fenugreek, and flaxseed.
Then the midwife should anoint her hand with these oils and mucilages; then she should introduce it into the womb with ease. Then she should seek, with it, the place where she can fix the hooks from the fetus, and the suitable places for that.
Therefore, consider then, if the fetus is prepared to descend upon its head: then fix the hooks into its eyes, or into its neck, or into its mouth, or into its palate, or under its chin, or into its fork, or into the places near the ribs, and under the hypochondria.
If however the descent is upon its feet, then in its pubic bone and its middle ribs.
And it is necessary that she hold the hook with her right hand, and place its curve in that which is between the fingers of her left hand, and introduce it with her hand with ease, and fix the hook in any of those places that we have mentioned, until it reaches the empty space.
Then she should fix another hook, or a third if needed, in opposition to it, so that there is an equal contraction, and it does not deviate to one side.
Then let it be extended with equal extension, and let the extension not be in a straight line only, but rather let it be moved in every direction, so that its exit may be eased, as happens with a tooth during its extraction.
And it is necessary, in that which is between them, that the extension be softened. If a part of it is retained, then the midwife should anoint some fingers with oil, and introduce them from the side, so as to revolve with them that which is retained.
If therefore a part of the fetus emerges, then the hooks should be shifted to another location that is higher; do this until the entire fetus emerges.
If therefore a hand emerges before the other, and it is not possible to return it, because of its constriction, then it is necessary that a cloth be wrapped around it. Then pull it out until when the whole emerges, cut it either at the junction of the sole, or at the elbow; and thus it is necessary that you do to the other hand, and to the two feet.
If however the fetus' head is large, or constriction occurs in its exit, or water has gathered in its head: then it is necessary that a thorny Spatumile be introduced between the fingers, and that it split the head with it, and extract the water; or that it be broken with an instrument called Almisdac, whose form comes with the forms of the other instruments in the chapter that is after this one.
Similarly, he should do the same if the fetus is naturally of a large head, then it is necessary to cut it with algengematu, or to extract it with misdach, as we said. Then pull out the bones with forceps.
If the head emerges and is constricted at the fork, then it should be cut, until the moisture in the chest is discharged.
If however it does not become entangled, nor yields to exit, then cut the head into pieces, as far as possible for you.
If the lower part of the belly is abscessed or has dropsy, then it is necessary to pierce it until moisture runs from it.
If the fetus is above its feet, then its extraction is easy, and its direction towards the mouth of the womb is slight.
If however it is constricted at the belly or chest, then it is necessary to extract it with a cloth that you have wrapped around your hand: and cut the belly or chest until what is in them flows out.
If however the remaining limbs are removed, and the head returns, then the midwife should insert her left hand. If therefore the mouth of the womb is open, then she should insert her hand deep into the womb, and search for the head with it, and pull it with her fingers up to the mouth of the womb: then insert one hook, or two hooks, into it, and pull it with it.
If however the mouth of the womb is constricted, due to a hot abscess that occurs to it, then it is not necessary to labor over it, rather it is necessary then to administer the effusion of moist, oily things, and administer plasters, and she should sit in waters that soften and moisten.
If the fetus is on its side, then it is possible to straighten it; then administer what we said regarding a living fetus.
If that is not possible, then it should be cut into pieces and extracted.
And it is necessary that you not leave any part of the afterbirth inside at all.
When therefore you have completed your operation, then administer what we have said regarding hot abscesses that occur in the wombs.
If a flow of blood occurs, then it should be immersed in astringent waters, and administer the remaining treatment.
And indeed I have already seen a woman in whose womb a dead fetus was: afterwards she became pregnant again over the same one, then another fetus also died. Therefore, after a long time, an abscess occurred in her navel, and it became swollen until it opened: and it began to discharge pus.
Therefore, I was called to treat her, and I medicated her for a long time: and the wound did not heal.
Therefore, I placed upon it some plasters of strong attraction: and the bone protruded from the place. Then a few days passed, and another bone protruded. Therefore, I was amazed by this, since there is no bone in the belly.
Therefore, I thought that they were from the bones of a dead fetus.
Then I investigated the wound, and extracted many bones, the woman being in better condition: and she lived thus for a long time, and a little pus issued from the place.
And I did not consider this foreign, except because there is knowledge and assistance in it for that which the skilled physician administers with the hand in his treatment.
Chapter LXXVII.
Concerning the forms of instruments necessary for the extraction of a fetus.
The form of the hinge with which the opening is made.
Here below is the form of a Strappado, in which books are straightened, to which are equally attached two hinges on two extremities of two pieces of wood. However, this hinge should be finer than the strappado; and to be made, it should be either of ebony or of boxwood.
And the width of each piece of wood should be about two fingers; and the edge of each of the two is about one finger, and the length of each of them is a palm and a half; and in the middle of the two pieces of wood are two additions of the same kind of wood, which are now firmly attached to both, whose length is half a palm and a little more; and the width of each is about two fingers, or a little more.
And these two additions are inserted into the opening, so that it opens with them when you turn the two hinges.
Another form, similar to it, finer, and lighter.
Let it be made of ebony wood, or boxwood, according to the likeness of forceps. However, at its end there are two additions, as you see here above: and the length of each addition of them is about a palm: and the width of each is two fingers.
When therefore you wish to open the womb with it, then make the woman sit on a bed, with her feet hanging down, and opening what is between her legs. Then introduce these two joined additions into the womb: and you hold the lower end of the instrument between her thighs. Then open your hand with the instrument, as you do with scissors, equally according to the amount of opening of the mouth of the womb, until the midwife does what she wants.
Another form of dizziness, which the ancients called the form of the impelling force by which the fetus is propelled.
The Almisdac form, by which the large head is crushed: and it is likened to a pair of forceps, with teeth on the end, as you see.
And sometimes it is elongated, like forceps of this form, as you see, which have teeth like saw teeth, with which it is cut and crushed.
The form of a hook with a single spine.
The form of a hook with two spines.
The form of a scalpel with two ends for severing the head.
The form of two broad scalpels.
All these instruments are exercised, and the more their types are multiplied, and prepared by the craftsman, the quicker is his operation, and the higher esteemed among men, because of their quantity.
Therefore, do not despise any instrument, unless it is prepared for you. Their necessity is indeed inexcusable.
Chapter LXXVIII.
Of the extraction of the Secundine.
When the Secundine is retained at childbirth, then it is necessary to instruct the woman to retain her breath. Then make her sneeze with condiments; and press your hand upon her mouth and nostrils. If therefore it emerges by this means, it is good.
If not, then take a pot, and pierce a hole in the lid; and place in it herbs that open the mother's mouth, such as calamint, rue, anise, chamomile, sseham, cinnamon wood, and centaury; take all these herbs, or some of them, and immerse them in water, and place them on the fire.
Then place a reed plug over the hole in the pot's lid, and the other end in the woman's womb; and let her hold it until the vapor reaches the concavity of the womb.
Then make her sneeze, as we said. For the afterbirth will emerge with ease.
If however it remains after this treatment, and does not emerge, then order the midwife to immerse her hand in sesame oil, or marshmallow mucilage. Then let her introduce it into the vagina, and seek out the afterbirth with it. When therefore she finds it, then let her take it, and extend it gradually.
If however it be attached deep within the womb, then let her introduce her hand, as we have described, until when she has found the afterbirth, and extract it gradually in a straight line, so that the womb does not fall during violent traction; rather, it should move easily to the right and left sides, then the amount of traction should be added. For it then obeys, and is preserved, and escapes from adhesion.
If the opening of the womb is constricted, as we have already described, treat it with sneezing and with medicine from a pot containing herbs.
If, however, it does not emerge with all that we have described, then beware of tiring yourself out in repeated attempts to extract it.
But it is necessary that whatever emerges from the womb to the opening of the womb be tied to the woman's hip. Then administer an enema to her with the tetrapharmacon ointment. For it will indeed cause it to putrefy after days, and it will dissolve and emerge.
However, when it putrefies, a foul odor rises from it to the stomach and head, and harms the weak one. Therefore, suitable fumigations should be administered to it.
Some of the ancients, however, have already experienced the fumigation of nasturtium and dried figs.
Form of the instrument with which a woman is fumigated during the retention of the Secundinae and menses.
Let it be made of glass similar to alcama, or of earthenware: and let the wide end be placed over the fire: and let the fumigation be placed on the prunes: let it hold it until that fumigation is consumed: and again another.
Chapter LXXIX.
Concerning the treatment of a non-perforated anus.
Many children are born with their anuses not perforated, which have already been obstructed by a subtle sifac. Therefore, the midwife should pierce that sifac with her finger; and if not, she should pierce it with a sharp spatulum, and beware of injuring the surrounding flesh.
Then place upon it wool soaked in wine and oil. Then treat it with ointments until it heals.
If you fear that it will be obstructed again, then insert a lead cannula into the opening for many days, and remove it when the child wishes to defecate.
And blockage of the anus also occurs from the consolidation of a wound, or an abscess. Therefore, that consolidation must be cut, then treated with what we have said about treatment and lead.
Chapter LXXX.
Concerning the treatment of fistulas that occur in the lower part.
Fistulas that occur in the lower part are nodules and enlargements that occur near the anus externally, or in the space of one of the parts, and are either one fistula or several.
When that nodule has become old, it opens, and watery white moisture, or thin pus, runs from it.
And sometimes there are penetrating fistulas from these. Therefore, penetrating ones are recognized by what emerges from defecation and flatulence when the sick person uses defecation, and perhaps worms emerge from them.
And some of these fistulas, when penetrating, are in the space extending to the bladder and to the urethra. And some of them penetrate as far as the hip joint, and as far as the tailbone.
By this, a penetrating fistula to the anus is recognized, even if not penetrating, that you insert your index finger into the anus, and insert a thin probe into the fistula, from air, or iron, when there is no winding.
If there is winding in it, then insert a thin lead probe or a hair from a horse's tail into it, until you feel the probe or hair in your finger.
If you do not feel it completely, and nothing comes out of the opening in terms of excretion, or wind, or a worm as I said, then know that it is not penetrating; therefore, hasten to the cure.
And when the fistula is penetrating to the bladder or to the urinary passage, then its significance is the excretion of urine from it, and the prohibition of it from this, that the place be consolidated with medicines.
If however it is penetrating to the hip joint or to the hip bone, then its sign is that the probe reaches there, if there is no winding in it to the bone, and the finding of pain in the surrounding area, and that pus comes out of it always, and that the medication does not work in it, nor is it consolidated with ointment.
And in all these penetrating fistulas there is no cure at all; and their cure is labor and vanity for him, from foolish physicians, upon whom it is formed.
In that one, however, which is not penetrating and not ancient, healing is hoped for with a saw, according to what I tell you, and I have already tested: and it is, that the sick person lie between your hands on his back, and raise his legs above his hips, and his hips be inclined towards his stomach. Then insert a lead or copper probe, if there is winding in the fistula, until you know where the probe reaches.
If, however, the sick person feels it near the anus, then you must insert your index finger into the anus.
If therefore you feel the probe in your finger, having already penetrated by itself, detected, beyond what you feel between your finger and the fistula or flesh; then therefore know certainly that it is penetrating; therefore do not labour on it, because there is no healing of it, as we said.
However, they said that in some men the flesh is healed, and from the medication by which help is hoped for, it is that you apply a thin cautery according to the breadth of the fistula, as previously mentioned, and you insert it, heated, into the fistula until it reaches around the anus. Then repeat it twice or thrice, until you know that all those added, filtered flesh-like, bird-feather-tube tissues are now cauterised.
Then treat it with lint wrapped in butter, until those cauterised tissues come away; then treat it with ointments that generate flesh and consolidate it.
If therefore it is healed, it is good.
And if not, then it is never healed with any other treatment.
And if the probe exits, and does not reach your finger in the anus, and there is a thick layer of flesh between it and the probe, or similarly, and you see a fistula following the skin's surface, then cut the skin from the beginning of the fistula, and you proceed cutting with the probe that is in the fistula until the cut reaches where the probe's end reaches, and the probe is freed and falls out.
Then cleanse those filtered flesh, which resemble the hollow of a bird's feather, so that nothing remains of them.
However, if blood overcomes you, and separates you from your operation so that you cut those flesh: then better it is done, and quicker aid, to cauterize it with fire, or with a sharp medicine.
Because cautery with fire aggregates good habits, burning those added flesh, and removing blood, and drying out the moisture.
Then treat it with linen wrappings soaked in butter, or pounded herbs, until the place discharges pus, and those burned flesh exit in pus.
Then treat the wound with consolidating ointments, which generate hard flesh: and there are medicines in which there is astringency and drying.
For when it is restored, then it is already healed: and you do not fear repetition.
If however the fistula has already reached the depth of the anus, and extends from the superfluity of the body: then introduce your finger into the anus, and investigate with it.
If therefore you feel a tension, and between you and it is a membrane of pus or flesh, and it is close to penetration: then there is no operation in it, unless according to the way of trust and hope.
That is, because one of three methods is administered in it: either to cauterize it, as we said; or to dissect it, until you reach its concavity, so that it is possible for you to insert ligatures, and cure from close by. And do not proceed much with the incision, so as not to sever the muscle containing the anus, and bring about an unforeseen exit of the evacuation of the bowels. Then treat it with what we said.
For perhaps it will be healed; or you will draw out the membrane, and cleanse it after cutting it close to the anus, as we said, either with a probe, or with another instrument of a sharp tip. Then cleanse those filtered flesh that are in the incision, until where you can with scrutiny.
Then try to restore the whole wound with an incision, and the upper opening where it consolidates; and an open fistula remains in the inner part of the anus, and it will be easier on the patient.
And sometimes the fistula is sutured in this way: and it is, when you insert a probe into the fistula; and it is on the side of the anus around the surface of the body with skin and margin of the anus; then take a probe with a perforated tip, which is like an aleskef needle, that is, preparing leather, according to this form. And insert into it a thread wrapped with five threads, and about it. Then insert the probe with the thread into the fistula, until it reaches its concavity.
If therefore it is penetrating into the inner margin of the anus nearby, then pull the thread out of that opening: so that you insert your finger into the anus, and pull out the end of the thread, and join the two ends together; and tie each, and leave it for one or two days; and the more the thread penetrates the flesh, the better tie it, until those pieces of flesh between the two ends of the thread are cut and fall away; then treat the wound until it consolidates and heals.
If however the opening of the fistula is not penetrating, then make it penetrate in whichever way is prepared for you, unless it is very deep. For you do not have the means to do that on account of the muscle, so as not to injure it.
Then do with it as we said, until it heals.
A pointed spatula, with which the fistula is split, whose curve is very sharp, and the other part is not sharp, so that it does not injure what is unnecessary, as you see.
Chapter Eighty-One.
Concerning the suturing of hemorrhoids, from which blood flows from their incision: and the treatment of fissures.
Hemorrhoids are according to two modes. For they are either inside the anus, similar to sacks of coals or plums, or as if they were grapes themselves. And from them are small ones and large ones: and blood flows from them always, and there is one, and there are many. And they are outside the anus, and at its extremities.
However, those that are outside the anus, are mostly few and moist, from which yellow water, or little blood runs with constant flow; and they are according to the color of the body.
And the treatment of that which is inside the anus is, that you should command the sick person to strain; and it is to be pressed out, until it exits the anus, and the athelils appear to you. Therefore also suspend them with hooks; or hold them with your nail. Then cut them at their roots.
If however the hooks do not hold on to them, because of their moisture and their softening: then take them with a rough cloth, or with cotton from doubled wool, and pull them out with your fingers. Then cut them, and after the incision sprinkle some hot powders on them, to act as a cautery. Or cauterize them according to what preceded in the chapter on cauterization. Then treat them with the rest of the treatment, until they are healed.
If the anus does not yield to evacuation, then administer an enema to the sick person containing a small amount of mordicant, so that it is washed with what is in the anus, and it yields to evacuation quickly when the sick person expresses himself.
If however the piles are external from the anus, then their removal is easy and simple: and it is as if you grasp them with your fingernail, or grasp them with a hook, and cut them off. Then treat them with what we said, until they are healed.
And he who abhors incision with iron, then it is necessary that you administer their suture according to this method. Take a wrapped thread, and insert it into the needle. Then pull the piles upward, and make it penetrate with the needle at its root on the other side: and wrap the two ends of the thread, at the lower part of the needle, and let them be laterally: and tie the piles tightly with a strong ligature. Then tie the thread, and remove the needle.
Do this with all the piles, and leave one of them:  nor let it be such that blood flows from it excessively.
Then place over the anus a cloth soaked in rose or cedar oil, and put it over the anus; and instruct the patient to rest. Then leave it until it falls off.
When therefore the piles fall, treat them with ointments and other remedies until they are healed.
Concerning the treatment of Fissures. For they often occur from the drying out of stool and the constriction of nature.
When, therefore, they become old and medicine is of no avail, then it is necessary to scrape them with a pointed spatula, or with your fingernail, until they become moist, and the upper layer which prevents their consolidation is removed. Then treat them until they are consolidated as they should be.
If however they do not consolidate, then scrape them again with a more forceful instrument than the first, until they bleed profusely and are scraped, then treat them until they are healed.
Concerning the treatment of inverted and non-inverted almirmar, and of dry athelil and formicae.
Chapter LXXXII.
Know that almirmar is nothing other than a round swelling the color of the body, similar to the head of a nail: and it forms throughout the body, particularly in the lower parts of the feet and the toes: and pain results from them during walking.
Therefore, it is necessary to cut what is around the nail, and hold it with shears or with a hook, and uproot it from its root.
Then treat it; and if you wish, cauterize it according to what has preceded in its chapter.
From dry athelil, one is a small, rough, filtered, round elevation on the surface of the body. And they frequently occur on the hands of children.
And its treatment is to stretch the athelil and cut it, or to bind it with silk thread or with a thread until it falls off. And if you wish, cauterize it according to what was mentioned before, with fire or with a sharp medicine; and now the inverted athelil are eradicated, and the non-inverted ones with the instrument that I shall name.
A formica is also a small, filtered, thick elevation on the surface of the body, going deep within; and when it encounters cold, a sensation similar to an ant's bite is felt in it and spreads throughout the body; and most of it occurs on the hands.
And its treatment is to take a cannula from a goose's feather, or a strong vulture's feather; and place that cannula on the Formica until the cannula's circle surrounds its parts. Then turn your hand with the cannula until you separate what is around the Formica; and put it deep into the flesh, and eradicate the Formica from its root.
And if you wish, do it with a cannula of air or iron, according to this form.
Let the upper part of the cannula be drawn to a height, to ease the grasp and turning of the wrapped-up object above the finger.
And if you wish, cauterize the Formica according to what was previously stated in the cauterization of Athelil in the chapter on the cautery.
Chapter LXXXIII.
Concerning the shapes of instruments used for enemas in diseases of the anus, and for bowel evacuations, and colic.
Sometimes enemas are made of silver, or brass, or cast bronze, or hammered bronze.
And sometimes these instruments are made small or large, according to those in which they are administered.
Therefore, that which is administered in the treatment of young children should be small; and for those whose anuses are weak or painful, so too should their enemas be.
Large enema.
Its tube, that is, the upper head to which the pouch is tied, should be wide in this manner; and it should have a distinction so that the pouch is tied as you see, and its lower extremity which is inserted into the anus should be closed, that is, not open, smooth, very, drawing toward thinness; and on one side of it there should be two openings, and on the other side one opening.
And the size of the opening should be according to the thickness of the fingers, a little larger; and let the pouch in which the medicines are placed be made from the bladder of an animal, or from parchment, which should be in the shape of a small asofrat; whose root should be one palm.
And let it be pierced with many holes as it is rolled, and let the space between each hole be the thickness of a finger. Then insert strong thread into those holes, made of ten threads, or about that many, and stitch the pouch to it as an asofrat. When, therefore, the pouch is placed in it, it should be tied at the head of the enema between two distinct constrictions by a strong binding. Then administer the enema with it.
And this is the form of the pouches here depicted, and the holes.
However, the quality of administering the enema with it is: that you fill the pouch, or bladder, with medicine that is warmed according to the patient's tolerance; and beware that it is not too hot or too cold.
Then the patient shall lie on his back, and raise his legs upwards; and place beneath him a cushion, that is, a hide upon which people lie, or a linen cloth.
Then anoint the end of the clyster with oil, or with egg white, or with the mucilage of fenugreek, or similar things. Then introduce it into the anus with ease. Then let the attendant take the pouch with both hands together, and squeeze it with force, until it has emptied all that is in the clyster into the intestine.
Then remove the clyster, and let the patient raise his legs to the wall.
If the medicine prompts him to defecate, then let him hold it as long as he can. And if he sleeps with it all night, it is more beneficial.
If, however, you clyster him in diseases of looseness of the belly, and his discharge is hasty, then clyster him again, until he is cured.
If there are hemorrhoids or damaging abscesses in the anus, then let the clyster be very thin, gentle, from the clysters with which children are clystered.
And this should be the form of a thin clyster, above which the skin is tight.
Chapter Eighty-four.
I have already stated in the division concerning the treatment of wounds that it was fitting for the book, and I will state here the treatment of them with the hand and with remedies according to completion.
Therefore, I say that wounds are diversified according to that by which the wounds are made, and according to the place upon which the wound falls.
Therefore, the things by which wounds are often made are like the throwing of a stone; or the cutting of a sword or knife; or the striking with a spear, or wood, or arrow; and by similar things from many things.
And the wounds that are made according to the places of the body are, such as a wound that falls upon the head, or neck, or chest, or belly, or liver; and similar ones from those members.
And I am narrating the treatments of certain wounds, that I may set forth that reasoning and rule over the remaining wounds.
And I will begin with simple wounds of the head, because the recollection of compound wounds will come at the beginning of the third chapter.
I say therefore. When a simple wound occurs on the head, and there is no fracture of the bone: then consider, if it is from the throwing of a stone, or similar things, and it has only broken the skin; and is a large wound, and you fear a bad outcome of a hot abscess: then hurry, and perform a cephalic phlebotomy immediately; and do not delay it; and draw from its blood according to the quantity of its strength; if nothing is preventing it from phlebotomy, especially if the blood issuing from the wound is little. And let the phlebotomy be on the opposite side of the wounded part.
And let the phlebotomy be on the opposite side of the wounded part. And let the patient beware of repletion, and wine, and heavy foods.
If, however, something prevents lessening, then lessen from food, and let him not approach meats, nor wines.
And if a hot abscess occurs on the wound, apply a poultice steeped in rose oil alone, or with wine containing astringency.
If, however, you are sure it is not a hot abscess, then if the wound is recent with its own blood, and the air has not changed it, apply the powder I will describe shortly; and if the air has already changed it, then apply some ointments until it is healed.
If, however, the wound is large, or from a sword cut, and similar things: and its edges do not come together by means of bandages, then bring them together with sutures, according to what I will describe in wounds of the belly concerning their sutures.
If, however, the wound is already exposed by the bone, and hangs, and there is no retention of it except in a small suspension, then cut it. Then treat the wound with medicines, and restore the hard flesh in place of the skin, and command the sick person to be nourished with foods in which there is thickness, such as heads [of grain] and cooked wheat, until it is like porridge, and things similar to these.
If you find putrefaction in the skin, and it does not adhere to the bone, then cut away all the putrefied part and cast it off; then treat it.
If, however, in these wounds there is an artery, or vein from which blood flows: and it is not stopped by medicines, then investigate the artery. If therefore you find it not severed, then sever it with a spatula, or tie it. If necessity demands, when what we have said is not helpful, then cauterize it until the blood is stopped.
If, however, these wounds are small, simple, then their remedy is easy, slight: so that you powder them with this powder, because it consolidates them before the air alters them.
Method of the flesh-growing powder. Take one part of frankincense, two parts of dragon's blood, and three parts of quicklime; grind the whole thoroughly, and sift it; and fill the wound with it, and bind it with a good bandage, until it adheres well to it, and clots with the blood.
Then bind it from above with bandages, and leave it, and do not loosen it as long as it remains, so that it does not fester. For the medicine is not removed from it until it is healed.
And sometimes lime alone does it, when you do not have frankincense and dragon's blood present; it also does the same work in many large wounds, when it is appropriate to put it on them and bandage them, and are fresh wounds with their own blood, which the air has not altered.
But if the air has already altered them with some alteration, especially when it is in the time of summer, then the intention of this powder is not in position: therefore treat it with what we have said, so that you put on it some digestive ointments; or put on it poultices made of barley flour, made with water and honey, until they bring out pus. Then treat them with the rest of the treatment, until they are healed.
However, if a fracture in the bone occurs with the wound, and it is small, then remove it with the forceps; and I will now say the treatment of head fractures in what is to come.
Concerning wounds of the neck.
If, however, a wound occurs in the neck, then there is no difference between it in treatment, and between a wound of the head when it is simple.
If, however, the nerve of the neck or artery has already been cut: then if it is a nerve, there is no skill in it, except that its treatment should be avoided with anything that produces a strong astringency, such as ziniar, and zegi, and similar things. Because they harm the nerves and contract them.
And do not place anything cold on the wound at all: because the substance of the nerves is cold, and its continuation is with the brain, which is higher than the limbs: and let that with which it is treated be mild from medicines, such as lime washed with sweet water, sometimes infused with oil or with rose oil, and tutty, when washed also with sweet water: and the other mineral stones, in this manner, and moist ointments.
If however the wound is large, then administer a suture: or constrict its lips with plasters. Then treat it until it is healed.
If however the wound has depth, or a sinus occurs in its lower part, in which pus is already collected, then pierce it in the more depressed place in it.
If indeed an artery has been incised in the wound, and its blood flow has followed, then separate or cut it, and tie it: or cauterize it, if necessity prompts it.
If however the windpipe has been incised, or the whole of it, and the soft tissues have been saved, then join the lips of the skin with sutures over the windpipe, and do not touch the windpipe itself, but rather level it and return it to its natural shape. Then bind it with a suitable ligature and leave it for a few days. Then treat it with ointments containing astringency and desiccation, until it is healed.
If however the wound has already made an impression on the bone, then investigate it; then if there are fragments of bone in it, remove all of them, or as many as you can; and what cannot be removed, leave it until the wound rots, and its exit is separated and eased.
If the wound is recent, then powder over it the powder I have described. If, however, bone is visible in the wound and it becomes a fistula, then treat it with what is mentioned in its chapter.
Concerning a wound in the shoulder blades.
If there is a blow from a lance or a knife, and you see its depth, then consider. If, therefore, wind emerges from the wound when the sick person gasps, then know that the wound is mortal.
But if its depth is not great, and it is fresh with its own blood, then do not apply powder to it from the first hour, nor bind it, so that the blood is not retained in its depth, and returns to the heart, and kills the sick person: but apply attractive ointment to it, and loosen it twice daily.
If, however, the ointment is not present for you, then place old cloth in the wound's opening, so that it sucks what emerges from it of the moistures; and cause the sick person to sleep over the wound, so that what gathers in it may run out.
However, if three days or more have already passed since the wound, and no spasm occurs in the sick person, nor bad tremor of the heart, nor constriction in breathing, and you see the rest of his conditions good, then know that the wound is safe. Therefore, treat him with bandages and the rest of the treatment until he is healed.
If, however, his healing is excused, and it always opens up again, then know that it has now become a fistula. Therefore, treat it according to its chapter.
And if the wound is from the cut of a sword or knife, and is simple on the surface of the chest or back, then treat it with what preceded concerning sutures, if it is large; or with powder, if it is small.
But if it has already made an impression on the bone, and chipped fragments from it: then investigate the wound, and hasten to remove those fragments, if they are separate. If however, they are not separate, then leave them until the wound putrefies, for the extraction of them will thus be eased.
However, for the treatment of other accidental wounds in the other limbs, the judgment is that which we have said.
Concerning wounds of the abdomen, and the exit of the intestines, and their expulsion.
The rupture which occurs in the abdomen is great, medium, and small.
From a great one, therefore, sometimes the intestine, or a number of intestines, emerges.
Therefore, their intrusion occurs, and their suture is more violent and difficult.
And a small rupture also occurs difficult in a different way. Therefore it follows that their reduction is more difficult if you do not hasten their intrusion into their place immediately, because it is inflated, opened, and enlarged, which is why its entry becomes difficult.
Therefore, a middle rupture is better, because when it occurs, the reduction of the intestine is not difficult, as it is difficult in these two kinds.
Know that the suture of the belly is according to four ways.
Two common sutures are appropriate for the suture of the belly, and for the suture of the other wounds of the body; and two other sutures are appropriate for the other wounds. However, they are more appropriate when suturing the belly, and I will tell you each one with an explanation and declaration.
Therefore, I say that when the wound is small, and something from the intestine emerges from it, and it is difficult to reduce it, then it is due to one of two methods. Either due to the smallness of the rupture, as we said, or because there is inflation in the intestine due to the cold air.
Therefore, when this is so, then it is necessary to warm it, by soaking a sponge, or a damp cloth, in merely lukewarm water. Or boil it in water with squinanthus, cyperus, and spica, and apply it to the intestine with that, until the inflation resolves.
Sometimes, wine containing astringency does it, and is better than water alone in resolving the inflation.
When you see the inflation already resolved, then moisten the intestine with water in which mallow or mallow has been boiled. For through it the entrance of it is eased with a small thing.
If, however, its return is excused after this treatment, then cut slightly into the rupture with the instrument with which hemorrhoids are cut.
And the first treatment is better when it is prepared, than the incision; and no compulsion to cut is made after necessity and failure of the instrument.
Let one part of it be twisted, sharp, and the other part not sharp, and the slender extremity not be with the slenderness of a spatula, but rather somewhat blunter: and it is that which is likened to a caul, as you see.
Therefore, when the wound is enlarged and the intestine enters, it is necessary that its reduction be according to its natural form in its own proper place, if possible above it; because it is better.
When, however, the rupture is large in the lower part of the abdomen, then it is necessary that you make the sick person lie on his back, and place his legs higher than his head. And if it is in the upper part of the abdomen, then place his head and chest higher than his lower parts.
And similarly, if the rupture is in one of the two parts of the abdomen, then place your intention and your consideration always in this, that the part in which the wound is be higher than the other part.
And thus it is necessary that you administer it in large wounds and in medium-sized wounds.
In small wounds, however, place it according to what is prepared for you.
Then have a pious attendant present, who should hold the entire rupture with his hand, and bring together its edges. Then uncover for the one who follows the suture something after something; and this is the common manner of a single suture.
And it is that you take a needle, or a number of needles, according to the quantity of the wound's width. Then leave from the extremity of the rupture a quantity of the ring finger, that is, of the physician's: and fix one needle beyond that, into which you shall insert thread at both ends of the skin, with both ends of the thread that is under the skin from the inside, until you make it penetrate from the other side, and you have now brought together the two ends of the skin, and the two ends of the thread: and four rows are made.
Then tie with the double thread in a circle around the needle several times from both sides together, until the two edges of the wound are brought together in a proper closure.
Then leave the thickness of a finger also, and fix another needle in them; afterwards pass it with the thread, as you did with the first needle. Therefore do not cease to do that with as many needles as you need, until you complete the closure of the whole wound.
And let the needles be of medium thickness, between thick and thin; because very thin needles readily pierce the flesh, and thick needles are difficult to enter the skin; therefore it is necessary that they be of medium thinness and thickness.
And it is not necessary that the needle be fixed too deeply in the two close edges of the skin, lest the flesh be pierced quickly and the wound opened before it has healed, and neither should they be pulled too far with the suture, lest the wound be prevented from healing.
Then cut the ends of the needles so that they do not injure the sick person during their sleep; and place pads of cloth around them on all sides to hold the needle ends; and leave them until you know that the wound is now healed.
And this type of suture with needles is indeed more suitable for small wounds. Because a single needle, or two, or approximately that number, is sufficient for their suture.
However, a second common suture is to bring together four edges: two edges of skin and two edges of the wound, in a single operation, with a needle containing a thread equal in thinness and thickness.
Then, when you have pierced these four edges with the needle, draw the needle back from the side from which you began, so that the thread is drawn taut from the upper part of the wound; so that the suture is like the suture of a cloth where the cloths are drawn together, and place between every suture a quantity of padding the size of a little finger.
However, men know this suture.
And with this suture I treated a wound that had happened to a man in his belly, who had been wounded with a knife, and the rupture of the wound was greater than one palm: and about two palms of the middle intestine had protruded, and there was a rupture in the middle of the belly.
Therefore I returned it after his intestine had remained outside the wound for 24 hours.
Therefore the wound was consolidated in a space of about fifteen days: and I treated him, and he was healed: and he lived after that for many years, engaged in his affairs.
And the physicians had judged that he would not be healed.
And it was a miracle that I did not treat it with ointment. Because I was in a place where no ointment, nor any medicine, was to be found. Therefore, I placed old linen over the wound twice a day, and diligently washed it with honey water, until it was healed.
I will now describe two specific types of sutures, according to the account of the text.
And it is, that you begin with a sharp suture, and introduce the needle from the exterior to the interior part.
When, therefore, the needle pierces the skin, and going into the muscle along the length and width of the entire body, leave the end of the thread on the side on which you introduced the needle, and have it penetrate into the other end from the interior to the exterior at the other end miraculously.
When you have caused it to penetrate, then have it penetrate according to this in the same end miraculously from the exterior to the interior; and leave the end of the thread, which is on this side, and cause the needle to penetrate the other end from the interior to the exterior; and have it penetrate with your penetration by which you cause it to penetrate in the miraculously ending thread, which is on its part, until it penetrates the whole of it.
Then begin also from this same side, and sew it with the end that is from the thread on the other side, and withdraw the needle from the skin which it pierced.
Then return it to that skin, and sew the end of the thread, which is on the other side, with this miraculous end; and withdraw it from the skin that is on its part, and do it once, and again; and do it turn by turn, until you have sewed the whole exterior according to that example.
And the sum of this suture is that it should sew the skin like the way leather workers sew skins: so that you should sew the thread once from one side of one end of the skin; and that you should leave it another time from another side, until you complete it.
The second type of proper suture, which Galen said.
His speech recounted that you should sew it according to the way some physicians sew them, so that they join each part to its naturally corresponding partner; and they apply the thread end to its other end, and the needle end to its other end. And that is done according to what I tell you.
You should insert the needle into the nearest needle point on the exterior side, and make it penetrate into the inside of that alone; and leave the two thread ends. Then revolve the needle, and make it penetrate from the outside to the inside in the two thread ends, that is, on both sides.
Then revolve both, also making them penetrate from the inside to the outside, into the two other needle ends, which are on the opposite side.
And this method of stitching is better than the common, easy stitching, and is a stitch whose recall has preceded.
This is therefore also the speech of Galen.
And some said. The stitching of the belly is rectified with two needles. That is, that you introduce one thread in both of them: and begin with the insertion of the needle, from where it is at your hand, and make it penetrate to the other side, according to the stitching of those who stitch hides evenly.
And know that when there is a rupture in the middle of the belly, then its stitching is more difficult than the other parts of the belly.
However, the judgment of the treatment of a wound is like the judgment of other wounds.
That is, because when you get a fresh wound with its own blood, before the air alters it, and you reduce the intestine and stitch it up and fit it in place, then place the flesh-forming powder on it.
If the air has already altered the wound, then place upon it certain ointments that are present to you, until they cause pus to form, and the threads fall away, and the wound consolidates by sifac and mirach. Then treat it as you would treat other wounds until it is healed.
However, if the medicines are not present for you, then immediately place upon it, as soon as it begins to discharge pus, old linen: and change it twice a day, as I taught you, until it is healed.
For you do not need another medicine in many dispositions, when the wounds are simple.
If, however, you fear that the wound might communicate with the principal members in the injury, then it is necessary that you soak soft wool in oil of moderate warmth, or in rose oil, and place it around the parts between the groin and under the armpits.
If, however, he feels pain, or prickings, or putrefaction in his intestines (for this often happens), then enema him with astringent, black, lukewarm wine: especially if putrefaction has already reached the intestine, and a penetrating exit has been made to its concavity.
And know that that which is from the large intestine is of easier healing, and that which is from the small intestine is of more difficult healing.
However, the intestine called the Jejunum does not receive healing from a wound that occurs in it at all: and this is because of its multitude of veins, and their size, and because of the subtlety of its body, and the proximity of its nervous nature.
If, however, that which comes forth from the wound is a zirbum, and you obtain it fresh, reduce it according to your reduction, by which you reduce the intestines equally.
If, however, it has passed a while and has already become green, or black, then it is necessary that you tie it with thread over the spot that has become blackened by it, so that blood flow does not occur. For in a zirbum are veins and arteries.
Then cut that which is beyond the ligament, and place the two ends of the thread hanging from the lower part of the wound outside it: so that the pulling of it might be lightened upon you, and its extraction in the case of the zirbum, and the emission of the wound's pus.
A recollection of the wound that occurred in the Intestine.
When a rupture occurs in the Intestine, and it is small, then it is possible that it can be restored in some people.  Because I have seen someone who was wounded in their belly by a lance blow, and the wound was on the right side of the stomach, and the wound was old; and it became a fistula, and expulsion of waste matter and wind came from it.
Therefore, I applied a treatment, until I was not confident in its healing; nor did I cease to refine it, until he was healed and the place consolidated.
After I saw the place already consolidated, I feared for the sick person, lest some ill accident befall him in his belly; and no such ill accident befell him from that. And he remained in better condition, eating and drinking, and engaging in sexual activity, and entered a bath, and exercised in his daily routines.
And now some experienced persons have said that when a wound occurs in the Intestine, and it is small, then it should be tied in this manner. And it is, that you should take ants of large heads. Then bring together the two edges of the wound, and place one ant of those that have an open mouth upon the two edges of the wound. When therefore it bites upon them, and tightens its mouth, cut off its head, for it adheres and does not loosen.
Then place another ant near the first, and do not cease to do this with ant after ant according to the quantity of the entire wound.
Then bring it back, and sew the wound. For those heads remain attached in the intestine, until the intestine is fed, and healed, and no harm befalls the sick person at all.
And now it is possible that the intestine be tied even with a fine thread: which is cleaned from the animal's intestine, attached to it after it is inserted into the needle.
And it is that you take the end of this thread from the intestine, and it is to be extensively scraped. Then tie a fine linen thread wrapped around its end. Then insert that thread into the needle, and sew with it the intestine. Then return it to the rupture.
And this method of sewing with ants and intestine is only according to the path of trust and hope.
