Know that a wound which is large, wide, especially if it is in one of the small intestines, then there is no remedy except refinement, nor is its healing at all.
Concerning the treatment of ulcers and fistulas.
Know that any wound or abscess, when it grows old and becomes an ulcer, and does not heal, and always discharges pus and does not close: then it is universally named a fistula, wherever in the body it may be, and we call it Zuchemen.
And a fistula, truly, is a filtered, hard, white knot, with which there is no pain, having the hollow of a bird's feather: and because of that, some call it Risati, that is, feather.
And in some instances, it is moist, discharging pus. And perhaps the moisture is incised in some instances. And sometimes this moisture is abundant, and sometimes scant; and sometimes thick, and sometimes thin.
And I have already mentioned in the division, when I spoke of wounds, that any ulcer that does not heal, nor flesh grows upon it, is not so except for one of nine causes.
Of which one is either due to a deficiency of blood in the body; or because of its bad quality; or because there is hard flesh between it and the edges of the ulcer, preventing the growth of flesh; or because there is much uncleanness and dirt; or because the ulcer itself is putrid, and the matter discharged to it is of bad quality; or because the medicine used to treat it is unsuitable; or because the corruption present in the region is a form of pestilence, or due to a property of the region, as happens in Caesaraugusta, where there is difficulty in the digestion of illnesses and the healing of abscesses by nature is delayed; or because there is a single bone, or a number of bones, present in it.
And I have already demonstrated all these causes and their signs, and their treatments with medicines in the division; and it is necessary here to make you aware of the treatment of Fistula and Zuken, which is accomplished through surgical intervention using the hand.
Therefore, know that Fistula occurs in all the members of the body. For some of them are fistulas which reach large veins and arteries, or nerves, or sinews, or the intestine, or the bladder, or to one of the ribs; or to one of the vertebrae of the back, or to one of the joints of composed parts, such as the hand and foot; and of them are some which extend to the vicinity of the principal member and similar ones.
Therefore, whatever of these fistulas is of this kind, is from illnesses of difficult healing, and does not receive treatment, and dealing with it is laborious and ignorant.
If, however, there is any fistula whose depth does not reach these places which I have mentioned, and you hope and trust in its cure, and wish to arrive at its knowledge, then take a probe of air or iron, if the fistula tends in a straight line, and investigate it with it.
If, however, there is a winding in the fistula, then investigate it with a probe of fine lead. Since lead, with the gentleness of its body, is lightened and bends around the winding.
If the fistula has many openings, it is not possible for you to point to it with a probe. Therefore, irrigate from among its openings.
For the moisture with which you irrigate it goes toward the other openings and runs out through them.
Then inquire carefully in what way it is possible for you to ascertain whether there is a bone, or a nerve, and whether the fistula cavity is distant or close, or whether there is one fistula with many openings.  Stand over the whole of it with all your strength, questioning the patient, and finding the pain from pressure with your hand on the area, and similar signs and causes of accidents and abscesses.
When, therefore, you have stood over all that place properly, then proceed to the treatment with confidence, and consider whether the fistula is a nearby apparent one or in a safe place distant from the joint or nerve or artery or vein or one of the locations I have told you.  Then cut the fistula as previously stated in my account, and remove what is in it through filtration and corrupted flesh, that which grows from the flesh or puss, or similar things. And treat it until it heals.
If however the fistula is of distant depth, and is straight, then you ought to cut it as deeply as possible. Then cleanse it of all its corrupted flesh.
Then apply bandages soaked in sharp medicines, and insert them into the cavity of the fistula which you have grasped with the iron implement.  Do this several times, until the sharp medicine has consumed everything remaining in the fistula cavity from the corruption.
Then restore it with ointments that generate healthy flesh, until it heals.
If however it does not heal by that, then cauterize it as we have described.
If therefore the cause of the fistula is bone, and this is confirmed to you, then locate it, and cut it as I told you: if nothing from a vein, or nerve, or main limb prevents you, as we have said.
If therefore bone is found by you, and there is something corrupt and black in it, then scrape it, until all its corruption is removed. Then treat it with that which causes it to flesh over, until it heals.
If it does not heal, and emits pus as it did, then know that you have not reached the complete eradication of the corruption.
Therefore, expose it again, and diligently do its scraping and cleansing with your utmost strength.
Then restore it. If it heals, well and good; and if not, then know that the corruption is in a deep, distant part of the body, which you have not reached. Therefore, you have no skill in it, unless you leave it to nature.
If a small bone is corrupted, and it is possible for you, draw it out with fine forceps suitable for it.
If, however, there are many bones, then carefully draw them all out, and do not leave any of them behind with your strength.
If, however, something from the humours resists you, and there is no fear or error in cutting them: then cut through those humours and flesh, if any are there, and free the bones.
If, however, you do not have the preparation to remove the bones from your time, then place over the wound something that will rot it, and leave it until that which is around those bones from the flesh and humours rots.
And guard the wound so that it does not heal. And constrict the section that you cut in your operation. Indeed, place over it, if you fear, a poultice steeped in crushed sulfur mixed with oil, or a poultice steeped in Egyptian ointment, or some other green ointment.
For when those humours, namely, rot, and the bones are separated from them, the removal and drawing of them is eased.
If however there is one large bone, such as the bone of the leg or the bone of the hip, and similar to them, and that which is corrupt from it is a large part: then scrape it with the utmost scraping until that blackness and corruption is removed. Then restore the wound.
If however that which is corrupt in it is a large part, and corruption has already reached the marrow of the bone, then it is necessary to saw it and cut it completely to the place where the corruption has reached; then treat it until it is consolidated.
And I tell you a case, which happened to a man in his leg: that you may put it as an example and aid to your operation.
There was a young man about thirty years of age, to whom pain occurred in his leg, from a cause that arose within his body, that is, from the interior parts, until the matter reached the leg, and it became a large abscess: and there was no external cause.
So, time passed with the physicians' error, until the abscess was opened, and many substances flowed out with it. And his treatment was delayed until the leg became fistulous, and many openings were made in it; which all emitted pus and bodily humors.
Therefore, a multitude of physicians treated him for about two years, and there was no wise man of the art of surgery among them, until I was called, and saw his leg and the matter that flowed from those openings with great force; indeed, the entire body of the man was already putrefying, and his complexion was yellowish.
Therefore, I inserted a probe into one of the openings, and the probe reached the bone.
Then I examined all the openings, and found that they connected to each other from all parts of the leg.
Therefore, I hastened and cut over one of those openings until I exposed a portion of the bone; and I found it corrupted, already corroded, blackened, and decayed, and perforated until it penetrated to the marrow.
Therefore, I removed what was revealed to me, and prepared it from the corrupted bone.
And I determined that there was nothing in the bone except that corruption which I cut away and removed. And I had already eradicated it.
Then I applied a restoration of the wound with consolidating medicines for a long time. And it was not consolidated.
Then I attempted, and I discovered a second layer above the first discovery on the bone: and I found continuous corruption with the bone: and I cut away what appeared to me also from that corruption.
Then I attempted its restoration. And it was not restored.
Therefore, I again discovered upon it, and did not cease to cut the bone piece by piece, and to attempt its restoration: and it was not restored, until I cut away about one palm's breadth from the bone, and removed it with its marrow.
Then I restored it with medicines, and it was quickly consolidated and healed: and this repetition in its operation and section was not necessary, except on account of the weakness of the patient's condition, and his small tolerance, and my fear for him regarding death: because it happened to him at all hours, from a superfluity of evacuations, bad syncopes.
Therefore, he was healed with complete healing: and hard flesh grew in the place of the bone, and a good condition was established in his body, and his strength returned: and he was restored to his former condition: and no harm at all was caused to him in walking.
If, however, a prominent bone in the body has already been fractured, then it is necessary to saw it, in this manner: and thus you should take a bandage and tie it to the end of the prominent bone; and instruct someone to pull it upwards; and place another bandage, thicker than the first, made of wool. Then tie it over the flesh that is beneath the bone; and pull its extremity, so that you draw the flesh downwards; and uncover the flesh over the place you wish to saw, so that the saw does not injure the flesh.
Place a piece of wood, or a board, beneath the bone properly from below; because when you do this, it does not prevent the cutting of corrupted flesh.
And it is necessary that the sawing be over the corrupted place for a short time, so that there is no corruption in the hollow of the bone; and that its appearance does not appear to the senses, and require another sawing of its place.
If the bone is corrupt, and not prominent, but rather its part is continuous with another part; and the corruption is in its middle, or in a part of it: then uncover the flesh from all sides completely. Then place the wood below.
Afterwards saw it from the first part, where the corruption is, until the sawing continues to the other part; and let the sawing be along the length of the corruption for a short time, as we have said.
If the corruption is in the joint, cut the corrupted joint itself, and saw the bone where it continues to the other part.
If, however, the corruption is in the continuation of two joints, then there is no ingenuity in it except abrasion.
If the corruption is in the palm of the hand, or in the sole of the foot, then the matter is very difficult in it. But you should inquire how the corruption appears to you. And abrade it, and cleanse it according to whatever arrangement is possible for you, and with whatever ingenuity it is rectified for you, when the vein or nerve does not cause you opposition.
And know that there are many incisions and saws for cutting these bones, according to the position of the bones, and their preparation, and their thickness and thinness, and their size and smallness, and their hardness and their rarity. Therefore, it is necessary to prepare a suitable instrument for every type of operation.
And know that these operations indicate to you the type of instrument you need, when you have long study with them. And the knowledge of the methods of this art bears witness to the methods of illnesses; for then you will find for yourself what is suitable for it from the instruments for every illness.
And I am firmly establishing for you in the final part of this chapter the number of instruments, which you will place as examples upon which you will cut, and the test by which you will try upon others.

And it is not fitting, unless the heads of the joints are rubbed with it, when they are corrupted, or the bone is large and great.

Its end is triangular, with sharp edges.
It is made of steel. And similarly, it is necessary that all that we have said be made of razors, and knives, and saws.
Concerning the incision of extremities, and the sawing of bones.
Extremities putrefy, either from an external cause, or from an internal one.
Therefore, when you have treated that corruption with medicinal remedies, and the treatment does not help; and you see corruption seeping into the limb, from which nothing is repelled; then it is necessary that you cut the limb as far as the corruption extends, so that the infirmity escapes from death, or from the greater harm that is in the loss of the limb.
And the sign of this happening is that the limb darkens until it rots; that fire has burned it, or that it rots after the blackness; until that rot is cut away to the point that follows that limb, and it spreads to the entire body.
Therefore, hurry with its incision, and similarly if the cause of the corruption is from the bite of certain poisonous creatures, such as the sea scorpion, and the viper, and the adder, and the like.
If, therefore, the corruption or injury is in the extremity of the finger, then cut off the finger, and do not give space to the corruption to proceed to the rest of the hand.
And similarly, if it happens in the hand, then cut it from the root of the forearm, and do not give space to the corruption until it reaches the upper part of the arm.
And if it invades it, then cut the arm at the elbow in its joint.
If, however, the corruption passes, and you see it approximately at the elbow: then in that is the death of the weak one. And to administer beyond that from the cure, is the measure of virtue.
And similarly do with the foot, when corruption invades the toe, then cut it at one of the joints; and if it reaches the sole of the foot, then cut the entire foot. If however it ascends to the knee, then cut the leg at the joint of the knee.
If however the corruption has already reached above the knee, then there is no skill in it, except for dismissal, and leaving the weak one to death.
And the manner of cutting or sawing a limb is that you tighten the ligament below the one you wish to cut, and tighten another ligament above the place. And let the servant extend one ligament downwards, and let the servant extend the other ligament upwards. And you should separate the flesh that is between the two ligaments, with a broad Spatumili, until the entire flesh is exposed. Then it is to be cut or sawn.
And it is necessary that you place linen cloth from all sides, and that the saw does not touch healthy tissue; and that additional injury and a hot abscess befall the sick person.
If, however, an internal flow of blood occurs while you are working, then cauterize the place quickly, or place some blood-stopping powders on it. Then return to your medication until it is complete.
Then bind the wounded limb with a suitable bandage, and care for it until it heals.
And I tell you that this same event happened to a man in his foot, the same event which I describe to you.
Because it happened in his foot with a blackness, like a burning that resembles fire: and that corruption first happened in his toe, until it began the whole foot.
Therefore, the man hastened himself, after seeing the corruption strike the limb with its intensity, which he found from its pain and burning: and it struck him at the joint, and he was healed.
And after a long time passed, the same corruption happened to him in the index finger of his hand.
So he came to me, and I tried to remove that excess with what I placed on his hand from medicines after cleansing his body: and the excess was not repelled, and it began to move to another finger: until the corruption began to spread to his hand.
Therefore, he called me to cut his hand: and I did not want that, hoping from me on the repulsion of that excess: and I also feared for him death from the incision of his hand. Because the man's strength was above the case.
Therefore, after he despaired of me, he returned to his land: and it came to me about him, that he hurried, and cut his whole hand: and he was healed.
And I have not brought forth this account, except that it may be a help for that which falls under this kind of illness; and that it may be a signification by which to be signified, and according to which to be done.
On the treatment of Sinuses, and the quality of enemas with medicines.
When an abscess occurs in certain fleshy limbs, and the space of the abscess extends until it forms a collection of pus; then it bursts, or is perforated, and that which is within it of pus emerges, and an empty space remains as if it were a vessel; and the skin that is over it is like a cloth that has already become thin; and it has not reached the final stage of corruption, nor does it affect the mouth, nor the nerve, nor the ligament: it deserves to be called a Sinus; and it is not called a Fistula; however, if its space extends until the corruption affects any of these parts, then it is called a Fistula, or zuken.
And its treatment is to cut away that entire skin as it turns; and especially when it is thin, and made like a cloth; and you are confident that it does not adhere to the area, due to its corruption.
However, if you hope that the skin adheres, and that corruption does not result from that ultimate consequence; and if there is a thickening of flesh in the skin, then treat it with an enema; and it is to be considered as a Sinus.
Then, if it is large, and the pus that is emitted from it has a foul odor, then irrigate it with an Egyptian medicine, which is vinegar, oil, and ziniar, taking equal parts, to be combined in a vessel; and they should be boiled over fire until the medicine turns red; and it comes to the consistency of honey.
Then take from it as much as you need; and dissolve it with water and honey, and irrigate the Sinus with it; and constrict its opening, and leave the medicine in it for a period of two hours.
Then extract it with pressure; do this for so many days, until the Sinus is cleansed and the foul odor is removed.
And sometimes it is irrigated with ash water, when this medicine is not present to you; and it is as if you take the ash of vine wood, or ash from the wood of holm oak; and pour water over it. Then strain it, and irrigate with it, until you are confident that the Sinus is now washed clean.
If the patient does not tolerate the Egyptian medicine, nor the ash water, then irrigate it with water and honey, to which some crushed ziniar has been added. Or irrigate it with honey and wine mixed, since the property of honey is to cleanse and wash away; and wine causes the sinus to adhere, especially when there is an excess of astringency and dryness in the wine.
When you have done this several times, and are confident that the corruption of the sinus is now removed, then irrigate it with that which causes flesh to grow there.
And it is to take from the diafinicon plaster, and dissolve it with rose oil and astringent wine; or irrigate it with certain medicines and other ointments, whose properties I have written in the treatise on ointments.
If the opening of the sinus is narrow, so that the clyster tube does not enter, then enlarge it slightly with iron; or place a linen cloth wrapped in Egyptian ointment, or in basil ointment, upon it, until it is enlarged.
And similarly, if the opening is even wide, then bring together its lips with a suture, and leave the quantity in which the clyster enters without addition.
And similarly, if the opening from which pus runs is elevated upward, then cut it in the lower place within it, so that the pus runs downward; because when pus is constricted in the depth of the sinus, it prevents flesh from growing there.
If it is not possible for you to cut the sinus approximately below it as you wish, then try to prepare the member in a way that the pus runs out of it with ease as you have prepared it. Try to keep nothing of the pus in it at all, and take the consolidating medicines that are placed on the sign, suitable for what you wish. And spread them out over a linen cloth. And let the cloth be of such a size that it covers the entire sinus. Then cut with scissors a hole in the direction of the sinus opening, a little larger than the sinus opening. Then spread mild ointments on another cloth, according to the size of the hole, and place it over the first, some distance from the sinus opening, so that the discharge of pus from it is relieved; and do not remove the large cloth, unless after a few days at a time.
However, the one over the sinus opening is the one you remove every hour, so that what accumulates in it from pus may come out; and so that you may know whether the matter flowing from it, the pus, is much or little, or digested or undigested.
And with this, again consider the location of the sinus itself, whether its owner feels pain there, or if there is no pain there, and whether there is an abscess there, or if there is no abscess there.
When, therefore, your treatment proceeds thus, and you see the matter which emerges from the sinus opening, then know that the sinus is now adhering, or is approaching adherence; then, therefore, loosen it in the morning, or on the third day; and when you loosen the wound, then change the cloth and renew the ointment.
If, however, thin matter emerges from the sinus after a long time, then do not despair of its adherence, for its healing is approaching, because there are men who delay the generation of flesh in their wounds, and there are those who act contrary to that.
If, however, undigested pus emerges from the sinus after many days, then know that the sinus does not adhere.
If, therefore, the adherence of the sinus is delayed and the matter prolonged: then add to the drying of the medicines, but let the physicians make them such that the medicines are moist in their substance and dry in their power; such as the ointment dyafinicon, when superfluity of addition is added to it from zegi.
And from the most ultimate in its treatment, is that you take the ointment dyafinicon and dissolve it with rose oil, and sprinkle upon it old wine equal in its substance.
Then prepare it with that, and use it.
And similarly to this action, when the ointment dyafinicon is not present for you, use honey when you boil it until it thickens, and use it. Or take myrrh, and aloe, and frankincense: grind the whole, and powder it over honey while it is on the fire. Then line a cloth over it, and bind it over the sinus: and line with the honey that you have boiled until it has thickened: and powder spices over it, and place it over the cloth, and bind it.
And sometimes long aristolochia, and the roots of the heavenly lily, and the lily, and the herb's flour, and centaury, are administered in it. Use them, simple or compounded, grind them, and sieve them: and powder them over the honey that I have mentioned, and use it.
If the sinus persists and is not healed by this treatment, then know that there is corruption deep within it: or it is already making an impression on the bones, or on the other hard members, or ligaments: then treat it with the treatment of Fistulas, as was previously mentioned.
Concerning the treatment of whitlow and a worn nail, and the incision of an added finger, and section.
A whitlow is an abscess that arises under the nail of the thumb of the hand, or foot: and perhaps arises on the other fingers.
When therefore its condition is prolonged and its treatment neglected, and it becomes an abscess with a hot abscess: or it is corrupted, and emits pus, until it corrodes the root of the nail: and perhaps corrupts it entirely, and perhaps the corruption reaches the bone, until there is a foul odor from it: and the end of the finger becomes wide, and its color becomes dark.
Therefore, when you treat it, with what we have said in the division, and your treatment does not help: then it is necessary that you cut with an iron all the superfluity that remains from the nail. Then cauterize the wound after that. For cautery is very helpful.
If however the bone is healthy, and the nail also healthy, and the useless additions of the nail now press the flesh inwards, and begin to prick it and injure it: then it is necessary that you place a thin Radius under the angle of the nail which pricks the flesh, and push it upwards: and cut the flesh with ease, and place upon that which remains of the flesh burning, corrosive medicines, until the whole of it is removed. Then treat it with ointments until it is healed.
If however corruption has already penetrated into the bone, then it is necessary to cut that bone and extract it: for it will never be healed while corrupted bone remains within it.
If, however, you see corrosion and corruption entering the finger, then cut it at one of its joints, according to its previous description. Then treat it until it is healed.
If, however, a nail suffers attrition, or impact, and a severe pain results, then it is necessary to first bleed the afflicted person. Then cut the nail with a sharp Spatumil in a transverse cut from above, as you see.
And guard that the cut does not reach the flesh beneath the nail, for you will cause intense pain to the afflicted person through that, and it will be the cause for the generation of added flesh in that place.
Then treat the place with that which soothes pain.
The extra finger that is born in some human hands, perhaps is entirely fleshy; and perhaps there are bones in some of them, and perhaps a nail is in it; and some of them are born at the root of the joint, or its birth is in some nodes of the finger. Therefore, the one that is born in some nodes of the finger, does not move; and the one that is born near the joint of the finger, perhaps moves.
Therefore, the incision of the one that is fleshy among them is easy. That is, cut it at its root with a broad spatula.
However, the treatment of the one whose birth is at the root of the joint is difficult. Therefore, abstain from its incision.
In that one, however, which is born on the finger at the last joints, then it is necessary to cut first its flesh with a round incision, down to the bone. Then saw the bone with one of those saws suitable for it. Then treat it until it is healed.
However, the consolidation that happens between fingers often occurs either from the birth of a person, that is, it is born with it, or from the consolidation of a wound, or the burning of fire, and similar things.
Therefore, it is necessary to separate that consolidation until the fingers return to their natural shape. Then place between them bandages, or cloths soaked in rose oil, so that they do not consolidate quickly, and separate between them; or place a thin sheet of lead between them, until the place consolidates as it should.
And similarly, if consolidation occurs between certain fingers and the palm, then cut that consolidation, as is prepared and suitable for the shape of the limb.
Concerning the incision of a defect.
Ulcers are enveloped, thick veins, full of melancholic superfluities which occur in many members of the body; and their effect is most often in the legs, especially in the legs of those who travel, work, and carry burdens.
Therefore, it is necessary that you administer a purging of the body from black bile, occasionally with a strong purge.
Then, bleed the patient from the basilic vein.
However, their treatment with iron is according to two methods. One of them is that they should be cut, and the black blood extracted. And the other method is that the vein should be raised and the whole thing extracted.
However, its incision is according to this method. First, soak the leg in much hot water until the thick, foul blood is dissolved. Then bind the leg of the sick person from the upper part of his thigh, up to the lower part of his knee with a bandage. Then cut the vein in one, or two, or three places with a wide incision.
Then elevate, that is, draw the blood in your hand from the lower part of the leg up, and from the upper part it is necessary that the phlebotomist first treats the sick person. Then cut the nail further down: until an amount of blood comes out that you see sufficient, and which the patient's strength can tolerate.
Afterwards, tie it off; and order him to abstain from foods that produce black bile; and repeat the evacuation and phlebotomy when the veins are filled and that harms the patient.
However, its elevation is in this manner. Shave the patient's leg if there are many hairs on it. Then immerse him in a bath; or apply a poultice of warm water to his leg until it reddens, and the veins swell; or have him exercise strenuously, if a bath is not available, until the limb is warmed.
Then cut the skin in a straight line along the vein: either at its end near the knee, or at its lower part near the heel.
Then open the skin with hooks, and strip the vein from all sides until it appears to the senses: and at its appearance, you see it sloping from red to black. When it emerges from the skin, you see it white, like a string.
Then introduce a probe under it, until when it is raised and emerges from the skin: suspend it with a blunt, gentle hook.
Then make another incision near that incision, by three fingers. Then strip the skin from the upper part of the vein, until it appears. Then raise it with probes as you did, and suspend it with another hook, as you did at first.
Then make another incision, or several incisions, if you need them. Then raise, and cut it at the very last incision, near the heel.
Then pull it out to the incision that is above it.
And do that until you pull it out from the incision that is higher, or from all the higher incisions, until the whole of it emerges; then cut it.
If however it does not yield to you in pulling and lifting it, then insert a needle with a strong double thread, and tie it, and pull it out; or insert a rod under it, and turn your hand in every direction until it comes out. And be careful not to cut it. For if it is cut, it becomes very difficult for you to lift it, and enters upon the weak one from it harm.
When therefore you have pulled it out entirely, then place on the wound site wool soaked in wine and rose oil, or oil, and treat it until it is healed.
If the vine is contracted, having a twisting to which there is a wrapping to parts, and is not as we said according to straightness, then it is necessary that you cut it on every part of its twisting and its wrapping places.
Then suspend it with hooks, until you lift it entirely: and be careful at your cut on it not to cut a vein, or wound it, for it will be difficult for you to lift or extract it. Therefore, preserve your prudence.
Form of the incident by which the vine is healed.
Form of the blind hook.
It does not have the curvature like the other hooks, nor is its extremity sharp, so as not to wound a vein: and it is of a thick, flexible nature, smooth; because if it were thin, it would cut a vein by its thinness: indeed, let its thickness be superfluous, as we said.
Of the extraction of the vein of the leg.
This vein is generated in legs in hot lands, such as Al-Jazirah and the land of the Arabs, and in arid, hot lands, of little tenderness, that is, in those in which no grass nor tree grows.
And perhaps it is generated in other parts of the body besides the legs.
And indeed its generation is from the putrefaction that occurs under the skin, just as happens within the bodies of snakes and worms, ascarids, and worms generated between the skin and flesh.
And the sign of the beginning of the occurrence of this vein is that a violent inflammation occurs in the leg. Then the place becomes blistered. Then the vein begins to emerge from the place of that blistering, as if it were the root of a plant or animal.
Therefore, when its extremity appears from it, then it is necessary to wrap a piece of lead around it, whose weight is from one drachma keil up to two drachmas, and tie it; and leave the lead hanging from the leg.
Whenever anything emerges from it to the outside, wrap it in lead and tie it. If it extends a great deal, then cut part of it and wrap the remainder. And do not cut it at the root before the whole of it emerges. Because if you cut it, it shrinks, and enters into the flesh; and it causes an abscess, and putrefaction in the place, and a bad ulcer.
Therefore it is necessary that it be gently handled and drawn out gradually until it emerges from its last part, and nothing of it remains in the body; and from this vein indeed, in some men, that which is in its length emerges five palms and ten. And now it has reached me, that it emerged from a man of twenty palms.
If however it is incised upon your time of medication, then insert a rod into its opening and pierce it with a long opening, along with the body, until the entirety that is in it is evacuated from the material; and administer treatment for the putrefaction of the place with medicaments for some days. Then treat it with the treatment for abscesses.
And this vein sometimes has many branches, especially when it appears in the joint of the foot or in the foot itself; and many openings occur to it, and a branch emerges from every opening.
Therefore treat it, as we said in the division, and with what was previously mentioned.
Concerning the incision upon worms generated under the skin, and it is called the Disease of cattle.
This illness is named in a certain land among us the Disease of cattle; because it frequently happens to oxen, and is a small single worm that is born between the skin and the flesh.
And it travels throughout the body, ascending and descending; and its passage from limb to limb is perceptible to the senses, until it breaks through wherever it breaks through the skin, and emerges.
And its generation is from the putrefaction of certain humors. As happen worms and snakes, that is, roundworms and threadworms, in the belly.
And it is feared because of its malice. Because when it travels within the body, and ascends to the head, and reaches a place: then perhaps it opens there, and emerges, and destroys the eye: and this happens many times.
Therefore, when one wishes to treat it, and to extract it, then this is not done except by its movement, and its appearance to the sense.
Therefore, it is necessary that you tie that which is above it, and that which is below it with a strong constricting ligature. Then cut above it, and extract it.
If it penetrates deep into the flesh, and you do not find it: then place a cautery with fire on the area, until it dies.
And the greater fear is its corruption, by which it corrupts the eye, as we have said.
If, however, you see it already having reached the head near the eye, then tie below it the eyebrow with a good ligature. Then cut above it, and extract it.
And it is necessary that the sick person use cleansing of his body, with medicines that dissolve bad, putrid humours.
And let him beware of foods that generate putrefaction.
Concerning the incision above the ailment of Nakir.
This ailment is called Nakir in our land: and it is a pain that occurs in certain limbs; then it changes from limb to limb.
And indeed I myself have already seen it, as I tell you. I was called to a woman infirm in certain villages. And she uncovered her arm, and I saw a small swelling in the vein of the arm's cord.
And after I remained one hour, I saw one swelling moving with the pulse, as a worm moves, ascending to her shoulder more quickly than possible; like quicksilver running from place to place.
Therefore, the pain receded from that place and settled in the shoulder.
Then I sat for one hour, and it ran to the rest of the body, until it reached the other arm.
Then she told me that it was rolling throughout her whole body, as I had seen.
Therefore, I was amazed at the speed of its movement from limb to limb. And I myself, before that, had not seen this illness with my own eyes in such a way, in this manner. However, I have seen many who find pain shifting from limb to limb, and I have not seen it with my own eyes, as I saw it in the woman.
And I do not think it is otherwise than because the woman was from among those whose bodies are dry, with detected veins; therefore, this windiness appears to the senses, shifting.
And it is necessary that they not appear in this manner in those who are delicate, and with moist bodies, with hidden veins.
When therefore you wish to cure it, and the patient feels that pain: then if it is apparent to you with the eye, as we have said, then pinch above it, and below it quickly, and cut above it, until that confined windiness emerges, and cauterize the place.
If however you do not see it with your eyes, then cure it with shaking the body, and with that which expels windinesses, and mitigates them with foul pills, and pills of serapion, and similar ones.
Concerning the extraction of Arrows.
Arrows are diversified according to their kinds, and according to the places where they fall from the body.
Their diversity is, however, because some have large points, and small ones: and concave, and blunt. And some of them have three angles. And some of them have tongues. And some of them have stars.
Those, however, which are according to the members on which they fall, are according to two ways. For either the members are from the principal concave members, such as the brain, and heart, and liver, and lung, and kidneys, and intestines, and bladder, and similar to them. Therefore, when it falls into any of these members, and signs of death appear to you, which I relate to you afterward, then it is necessary that you avoid the extraction of that arrow from them, for death follows him in whom it is, in many dispositions. And when these bad signs do not appear to you, and the arrow is not hidden deep within the member, then extract it, and treat the wound.
For the signs of the brain when an arrow falls into it, and pierces the bone, and exits through the thin membrane that is over the brain, are: because intense headache, and blindness, and dizziness, and redness in the eyes, and swelling, and redness of the tongue, and spasm, and confusion of intellect, and vomiting of cholera, occur from it. And perhaps blood exits from the nostrils, or ears. And perhaps speech is cut off, and the voice is destroyed. And a white moisture like gruel comes out of the wound location. And from it comes out something similar to the watery part of flesh.
If therefore these signs appear to you, then abstain from treatment, and remove the arrow if it does not come out.
The signs of an arrow striking the heart are: that it is near the left breast; and that one feels as though it were fixed in a hard substance, not in an empty one. And perhaps the arrow's movement is similar to the pulse's movement; and black blood runs from the wound. And this is followed by coldness of the extremities, and cold sweat, and fainting. Know therefore, that death descends without a doubt.
And the sign of an arrow wounding the lung is the outflow of frothy blood from the wound; and the vessels that follow the neck become inflamed, and the sick person's color changes. And he exhales a large, deep breath; and he seeks the inhalation of cold air.
If an arrow should strike the covering, which is in the chest, then it is near the small ribs; and a great breath is taken with violent pain, with a gasp, and all the shoulder limbs move.
And if an arrow strikes the stomach, then perhaps undigested food will issue from the wound.
And if an arrow strikes the belly and is fixed in it, something of the excrement, or a zirb, or an already ruptured intestine, will emerge from the wound. Therefore, there is no hope of curing him, nor of extracting the arrow.
If an arrow strikes the bladder, and urine emerges, and something issues forth from it to the exterior, and the pain is more severe upon the sick person, then know that it is mortal.
However, the remaining members, such as the face, neck, throat, shoulder blade, arm, spine, ribs, hip, leg, and similar members are saved in various ways, when the arrow does not strike an artery or nerve, and the arrow is not poisonous.
And I tell you what I have seen regarding these arrows, so that you may understand your treatment through it.
Because an arrow struck a man's tear duct, at the root of his nose. Therefore, I extracted it from the other side, beneath the fat of his ear, and he was healed, and no harm came to his eye.
And I extracted another arrow from a Jew, which struck him in the fat of his eye under the lower eyelid.  The arrow was already hidden, and I only retrieved a small tip of it, with which it was attached to its shaft.  And it was a large arrow from the Turkish bows, with soft iron, on which there were no ears.
Therefore, the Jew was healed, and no misfortune happened to his eye.
And I extracted another arrow from the throat of a Christian.
And it was an Arab arrow, and it is of the kind which has ears. Therefore, I cut above it between the two gullets: and it was already submerged in his throat. Therefore, I worked delicately with it, until I extracted it: and the Christian was healed.
And I extracted another arrow from the man which had fallen into his belly, and we thought that he would die from it.
Therefore, after thirty days had passed over him, and nothing had changed in his condition, I cut above the arrow, and I worked upon it, and extracted it; and he was healed, and no harm befell him.
And I saw a man upon whose back an arrow had fallen, and the wound above it had healed. Therefore, after seven years, an arrow emerged at the root of his thigh.
And I saw a woman upon whose belly an arrow had fallen, and the wound above it had healed, and the arrow remained inside her; and nothing changed in her condition, and the woman experienced no harm in any of her natural functions.
And I saw another man, upon whose face an arrow had fallen; and the wound healed, and it remained, causing him not much pain; and many similar cases to these.
And I extracted an arrow from the King's chief men, which had struck him in the middle of his nose, and had already slightly inclined to the right side; and the entire arrow was hidden.
Therefore, I was summoned for his treatment after the arrow's impact for up to three days. Therefore, I found the arrow wound very tight.
Therefore, I searched for it with a fine probe, and did not feel it; and he himself found a prick and pain under his right ear. Therefore, I hoped that the prick was from the arrow's tip.
Therefore, I plastered the area with a plaster containing attractive and digestive properties, confident that the area would form an abscess, and that the arrowhead would appear, so that I could cut over it; and no sign occurred at the location to indicate that the arrow had impacted the place.
Therefore, I applied the plaster to him for many days; and no sign occurred, and the wound meanwhile healed; and he remained weak from the extraction for a space of days, until he felt the arrow one day inside his nose. And he told me that.
And he told me that. Therefore, I applied a sharp, corrosive medicine to that wound for many days, until it opened; and when I probed it, I felt a fine arrowhead adhering to the wood.
Then I added to the opening of the wound through that sharp medicine, until the arrowhead appeared to us visibly; and I spent approximately four months with him during this time.
Then, after the wound had widened, and it was possible for me to insert forceps into it and move it, and it did not yield to removal, I did not cease to refine and devise methods for it, until I began one day, using specially crafted forceps, in the form described at the end of the chapter, until I extracted it. Then I repaired the wound.
And indeed, physicians judge that the cartilage of the nose is not restored. Therefore I restored it, and the wound was consolidated, and the sick man was healed with complete health; and nothing at all harmed him.
And I tell you the nature of the extraction of certain arrows: so that you may establish a rule and meaning for what I have not said.
Since the parts of this art and their distinctions are not encompassed within the narrative, nor does the book encompass it, and a skillful craftsman compares the little with the much, and with what is present with what is absent, and finds a new operation and a new instrument among the descent of foreign things of this art when they happen.
Therefore, I say that arrows do not emerge from the limbs in which they are fixed except in two ways. Or by traction from the place from which it entered. Or by the contrary of the other part.
And that which emerges from where it entered is either an arrow proceeding in a fleshy place, and is pulled out, and emerges.
If it does not yield to exit from the hour in which it falls, then it is necessary to leave it for a number of days, until the flesh that is around it rots, and its traction is eased, and its exit.
And similarly if it is fixed in the bone, and does not yield to you for exit, then leave it also for a number of days, and repeatedly with traction and motion every day, until it emerges.
If it does not yield to you for exit after days, then it is necessary to pierce around the arrow in the bone itself from every side with a fine drill, until the arrow is enlarged. Then pull it, and extract it.
If however the arrow is now hidden in a place of the body, and is concealed and hidden from sense, then seek it with probes. If however you feel it, then pull it out with certain instruments that are suitable for pulling it out.
If however you cannot grasp it, due to the constriction of the wound, and because of the length of the arrow deep within; and there is no bone, nor nerve, nor vein there: then widen around it, until the wound is enlarged, and it is possible to extract the arrow.
If however there are ears by which it is held, then free the flesh fixed in them from every side, by every means you can, and try, if it is not possible to remove the flesh from the fractured or twisted ears, until it escapes.
And when you administer the extraction of the arrow, wherever it is, then use the twisting of your hand, with forceps, to all sides, until you make it escape: and be subtle in the utmost subtlety, so that the arrow is not broken, and its extraction becomes difficult for you.
If you cannot remove it within one hour, then leave it for several days, until what is around it rots. Then try again: for it will be easier then.  But if a flow of blood gives you trouble, then administer what we have said regarding treatment in its chapter: and guard your skill from cutting a vein, or nerve, or tendon.
And use every means possible to extract the arrow: and let it be with ease and calmness, or with slowness and firmness, as I have told you.
And it is necessary, when you pull out the arrow, that it be weakened according to the shape in which the arrow was lodged. Because it is more helpful. If however it is not possible for you, then administer what is possible for you from the figures.
However, an arrow that emerges from the opposite side, either already protrudes from it outward, or causes a swelling on the arrowhead, by feeling, on the surface of the nearby skin, and you see it protruding; therefore, cut over it, and let the incision be according to the size that the forceps can hold.
Then pull it out; for it becomes easy to remove.
However, if it is retained in the bone, then turn your hand in a circular motion, until the arrow imprints itself in the bone, and enlarges itself, then pull it out.
And if not, leave it for a few days; then try again until it comes out.
If the arrow's shaft is in it, then push it out with it.
If however the shaft has fallen away, and you wish to administer a push, then insert a concave instrument into it, so that its concavity enters the arrow's tail; then push it out with it.
If however the arrow is concave, then push it out with an instrument entering it. For the arrow is eased by it.
If the arrow is poisoned, then it is necessary that you cauterize all the flesh where the poison is, if it is possible for you. Then treat it with whatever is suitable for it.
If the arrow is lodged in the chest, or in the belly, or in the bladder, or in the side; and is close to that which you touch with the probe, and it is possible for you to cut above it: then cut, and be careful of cutting a vein or nerve, and extract it.
Then sew up the wound, if you see fit, and treat it until it is healed.
And this is the form of the forceps with which arrows are extracted.
Let the extremities be similar to the beak of a bird, made of iron; when they grasp the arrow, they do not release it.
And many types of them are made, large, small, and medium, according to the quantity, size, and smallness of the arrow, and the breadth and constriction of the wound.
The shape of the impelling concave.
And the shape of the impelling blunt.
This is a blunt extremity, like a ray, so that its entrance into the concave arrow may be eased, and it may be impelled with it.
Concerning the phlebotomy of veins.
There are thirty veins to be opened, whose course is customary in the body.
Of these, sixteen are in the head: two pulsating veins, which are behind the ears, called two Herbs; and two arteries, which are apparent in the two temples; and two veins which are in the two tear ducts of the two eyes, which are called Looking; and the prepared vein in the middle of the forehead; and the vein which is at the tip of the nose. And two Guidegi, which are in the neck. And two veins which are in the upper lip of the mouth. And two veins which are in the lower lip. And these are veins called Algecherich. And two veins which are under the tongue.
The veins, however, that are bled in the arms, are five veins in each arm.
One of them is the Cephalic, and it is from the wild side: and the common people call it the vein of the head. And Fusca. And it is the middle vein, and it is composed of a branch of the Basilic and a branch of the Cephalic: and the common people call it the vein of the body. And Basilic, and it is situated on the domestic side: and it is also called Titillicaris. And the common people call it the vein of the belly. And the arm's cord, and it is situated above the elbow: and it is that in which phlebotomy is performed, and it is that which appears above the thumb with manifest appearance.
And Saluatella: and it is the vein which is between the ring finger and the little finger: and it has two branches.
And in the leg and foot there are three veins. One of them is that which is under the curve of the thumb from the wild side. And the second is Saphena: and its place is at the heel from the domestic side. And the vein Nesse: and its place is at the heel from the wild side.
And in the other leg are three veins similar to those.
Concerning the phlebotomy of two veins behind the ears.
Phlebotomies of the two veins behind the ears are beneficial for old colds, and for hemiplegia, and for sahafati, and for old, bad head ulcers.
And the quality of the phlebotomy is as I describe. And it is as if the sick person's head is shaved, and his occiput is rubbed in the place of the two veins with a rough cloth by good rubbing. Then let the sick person tighten his neck with a bandage until the two veins appear. And their locations are behind the ears in two depressed places of the head. Therefore, seek them with your finger, and where you feel their pulse under your finger, mark that place with ink.
Then take a phlebotomous knife, and it is called the incisor. Then insert it under the vein in the skin until the phlebotomous reaches the opening. Then lift your hand with the vein and skin upward, and cut the vein with the skin with an incisive incision. And let the length of the incision be the amount of two joined fingers, or approximately that: and make as much blood come out as you wish.
Then bind it with bandages, and leave it until it heals.
And already in the beginning of the book was the incision of those [things] and their cauterization.
The phlebotomy of the two arteries, which are in the two temples, is beneficial for ancient hemiplegia; and for crooked speech, and constant ophthalmia; and for a flow of excessive acute discharges to the eyes.
And the quality of phlebotomizing them is according to what I tell you. Let the sick person stretch his neck greatly, until two veins appear clearly to the senses, and their pulse is shown under your finger. Then mark them with ink.
Then lift the skin above the vein upwards with your index finger, and insert the lancet underneath, and lift the vein upwards. And cut it, as you did in the other two veins: and let the blood flow according to your need.
Then release the constriction of the sick person, and place your finger on the vein for one hour. Afterwards place a bandage and pads on it, and bind it tightly above the constriction: and release it until it is healed.
And already the recollection of them, and their incision, and their extraction have gone before in the beginning of the book.
Indeed, the benefit of phlebotomy of the forehead vein is after phlebotomy of the Cephalic vein, for the old ailments of the face, and for hemicrania, and redness, and foul ulcers.
And the quality of its phlebotomy is, according to what I tell you. The sick man binds his neck with a bandage until the vein appears. Then take the instrument which is called the Fossorium.
Place the prominent point which is on the head of the Fossorium over the vein itself, and strike it with a scraper, or with something else in its manner; and let the blood run according to the quantity that you wish. Then loosen the wound of the sick person, and bind it until it is healed.
And sometimes it is opened with a wide phlebotom. However, it should not be a phlebotom with a sharp end, like the other phlebotoms, but rather with a broad end. And lance it across, because the bone is close by, and perhaps the phlebotom will break in it when it is slender.
The relief of the two veins that are in the two tear ducts of the two eyes is for the eye from scabies and redness, and for a speck in the eye, and for ailments of the face.
And the quality of their lancing is such that the weak man tightens his neck with a bandage. Then lance them, and you be standing over his head.
And let the lancing be across, a little lengthwise, with a small lancet, somewhat broad, for there is no flesh in the place. For if the lancet is of a slender tip, it might perhaps break.
Then let the blood run as much as you need. And place a bandage over them, and bind them one night, and unbind them.
The lancing of the veins of the Nose is helpful in acute fevers, and violent headaches, and illnesses of the face, such as migraine, and redness that occurs on the face, especially when it is longstanding.
And the quality of its bloodletting is such that the weak man tightens his own neck. Then hold his nose with your left hand, and take a slender, long lancet, and stick it into the middle of his nostril, between that which separates the nose according to its straightness. Because the vein is not apparent to the senses there, and because blood begins to flow immediately.
And it is necessary that you hold your hand with the lancet for a little while, and cause the blood to flow according to your need. Then tie it with a bandage. For it (the wound) will recover quickly.
Indeed, the benefit of the phlebotomy of Guidegi's two veins is for the constriction of breathing, and the onset of leprosy, and melancholic illnesses, and impetigo and evil ulcers, and those that corrode, that is, shingles.
And the quality of their phlebotomy is such that the weak man should be restrained beneath both (veins) on his neck with a bandage: and the craftsman should stand over the sick person's head, and the sick person should be seated on a seat.
Then the phlebotomist should lance the vein to a length, the incision wide for a short time. Then extract a moderate quantity of blood, or according to what you see as necessary for that. Then do likewise with the other vein.
Afterwards, loosen the bandage, and tie the two veins with a middle-level constriction, so that the sick person is not choked. And leave it until morning. For the wound is healed.
Concerning the phlebotomy of veins, Algeberich. Veins, however, in Algeberich's phlebotomy are helpful: because they contribute to ulcers in the mouth, and gum corruption; and bad ulcers; and lip fissures, and bad ulcers, even those in the nose and around it.
And the quality of their phlebotomy is, that you should make the infirm person sit before you; and tie his neck with a bandage. Then turn back his lip, and look at the two veins: of which one is to the right of the lip, and the second to its left; and the demonstration of each is by the blackness of both. And that is true, because around them are small, black veins. Therefore, cut each with an incision proceeding.
However, if there is doubt upon you, and you do not know which of them it is, then aim at the incision of the larger of them, and the more evident of them.
And similarly do with the two veins which are in the upper lip: and indeed most custom is with it: and cut the two veins which are in the lower lip.
Of phlebotomy Of the vein under the tongue after the phlebotomy of the cephalic vein. However, the benefit of the phlebotomy of the two veins that are under the tongue is that the phlebotomy of the Cephalic vein is for the suffocations that are in the throat, and the ailments of the uvula, and the ailments of the mouth.
And the quality of phlebotomizing them is such that the sick person should sit between your hands before the sun; and lift up his tongue, and look under his tongue on one side of it a vein, and on the other side of it a vein; and the colour of both of them should slope towards blackness.
Therefore, phlebotomize each of them; and beware lest you press your hand too hard when phlebotomizing them. For under them are arteries, and perhaps a flow of blood will come from those arteries.
The three veins, however, which are phlebotomized in the elbow, are those whose phlebotomy has become customary in all people.
And their phlebotomy is done according to two methods. Either by pricking with a broad myrtle phlebotome, or a sharp olive-shaped one, or by cutting with a knife phlebotome, and it is Alnesil. And this is its form.
And from them are broad and narrow types according to the width and narrowness of the veins. The form of the myrtle phlebotome should be broad, as you see. And it is suitable for opening concave, full, proceeding, apparent, large veins, and those which contain thick, turbid blood.
And this phlebotome is of smaller width and finer extremity.
And it is suitable for the venesection of slender veins, which contain thin, yellow blood.
The Basilic vein, which is one of these three veins, is an aid in venesection. Because it draws blood from illnesses that are beneath the throat and neck, from what follows the chest, and the abdomen; and it is fitting for the phlebotomist, when performing the venesection on it, to be cautious and be wary of it, for there is an artery beneath it. If, therefore, he errs and adds to the venesection puncture, he will cut that artery, and a flow of blood will result. Therefore, it is necessary not to venesect it with a venesection lancet; rather, one should cut it with a scalpel.
If the Basilic vein is not clearly visible, then one should seek another vein, and switch to another, or look for one of its branches; or venesect the Brachial vein instead. For it is indeed evident, and cut it with the scalpel, as we said.
If, however, you wish to venesect it (the same vein), then it is necessary, before constricting the arm, to touch the area until you know the location of the pulse. Then mark the spot with a marking ink, afterward the arm, and cut the vein with a concave incision using a scalpel, as we said. And be careful that the blow does not fall over the space from the artery location.
And when you see, as you tighten the ligature, inflation in the spot you marked with the marking ink, then that inflation is the inflation of the artery: therefore avoid it.
If you see blood at the venesection leap, like a child's urine, and it is thin, red blood: then know that it is from the artery's blood. Then quickly place your finger over it a long space, then remove your finger. If, therefore, blood is cut (for it is often cut), then tighten the arm, and release it: and let the sick person beware of its movement; and let it be upon their fear.
And let him not move it for some days, until it is healed.
If, however, the blood is not cut, and overcomes you, and there is no medicine present at your moment, then cut the artery, if it appears to you. For its extremities contract, and the blood is cut.
Or take the bark of the fig-tree, and split it: take one half, and tie it over the place of the vein with a decent tightening, with a bandage and lint until another day.
Then if the blood is cut, it is good. And if not, then treat it with that whose prior recollection was from the place of the powders cutting the flow of blood: and the cutting of its blood is not difficult in many dispositions, because of the small place of the wound, and the possibility of a bandage from the arm.
The phlebotomy of the Fusca vein is beneficial, because it draws blood from the upper part of the head and the lower part of the body; because it is composed of a branch of the Basilic vein and a branch of the Cephalic vein, as we said.
And it befits the phlebotomist that it be above the fear from its phlebotomy, for a nerve is beneath it. Therefore, if the phlebotomist adds to the puncture, and finds a nerve, there occurs in it a stupor, and its healing becomes difficult, and perhaps it is not completely healed.
And this nerve often appears to the senses. However, if it is hidden in some people, and is subtle, and not shown, then it is necessary that you place your phlebotomy in such a way as to phlebotomize it, a section with Alnesil, and avoid the nerve with your strength.
If the vein is between two nerves, then divide the vein lengthwise.
Concerning the cephalic vein. Phlebotomy of the Cephalic vein is helpful, because it draws blood from the head, and contributes to ailments of the eyes.
And it is necessary in this vein properly, if you wish, that you phlebotomize it by puncturing with an olive-wood phlebotome, or with a myrtle-wood phlebotome, because it is the safest of all veins, because it is not beneath an artery, nor a nerve.
However, it is fitting for you in phlebotomy, that you avoid with the phlebotome the head of the muscle only, and seek a gentler place; and it does not harm it if it is not found with the first puncture, so that it may be repeated upon it with phlebotomy several times.
However, perhaps it becomes suppurated in some people when it is not phlebotomized on the first puncture, but that suppuration does not harm it in any way.
Concerning the usefulness of Phlebotomy, and the precautions to be observed in it. However, the quality of phlebotomy, and its resulting accidents, are matters on which you should reflect.
First, you should know that phlebotomy is not administered except in the preservation of health, and its persistence, and precaution against the arrival of illnesses.
And so that phlebotomy is done on one of the three veins in the elbow, namely the Cephalic, and the Fusca, and the Basilic. And that phlebotomy be done in the beginning of Spring on an empty stomach, in Spring and Summer; and in Autumn and Winter after eating, when signs of fullness appear.
And phlebotomy should be performed on the first, or third, day, after three hours have passed since the previous day.
However, phlebotomy administered in illnesses does not have a determined time; but when the appropriateness and necessity for it arises, in the night, or day, and at any hour, and at any time.
And it is not necessary that children be bled until they reach fourteen years of age. Nor should a pregnant woman be bled, except from the fourth to the seventh month. Nor should the elderly be bled, who have already passed sixty years.
Therefore, when someone wishes to be bled, for whatever reason, it is necessary that they cleanse their bowels before the phlebotomy with a gentle clyster, if there is much retained feces in them, so that the veins do not draw putrid superfluities harmful to the main members from the intestines during the phlebotomy.
Nor should a patient be bled who is suffering from nausea, or who is surfeited, or escaping from intoxication, until that is removed from them. Nor should the sick with dropsy.
And phlebotomy should be avoided again after colic, vomiting, diarrhea, and an abundance of sexual intercourse, labor, exercise, wakefulness, fasting, and everything that dissolves strength from the bodily or animal constitution.
Then consider the subtlety of the humours before that, if the blood is thick with foods and drinks, and medicines: if it is possible for him.
Then he should enter a bath, if nothing prevents him: or he should exercise with some exercise, so that the blood is refined.
And place his phlebotomy on his chest, around the third day, as we said: and endeavor that his chest be emptied that day from all bad animal accidents: such as anguish, and anger, and fear: and from all bodily accidents, such as labor, and punishment, and excessive intercourse, and those similar to them.
And let him have present things, with whose use the custom of men has run, from aromatics, and scents, and festivities, and things similar to them, in as much quantity as possible.
Then the phlebotomist should sit on a cushion, which is higher than the phlebotomist's cushion: afterwards, he should pull out his arm, and the phlebotomist should rub it with his hand twice, or thrice. Then he should tie the ligature with a strap, and wrap it twice.
And let the constriction be moderate, because when the constriction is unequal, or by excess in the constriction, then it hinders the flow of blood: or in its loosening, then it also hinders the flow of blood.
Then, after the constriction, the phlebotomist should rub his hands together until the veins swell and are apparent to the senses.
Then the phlebotomist should wipe the phlebotomized area with a little old oil, properly.
Then he should place his left index finger slightly over the vein itself, under the place where he wishes to phlebotomize, so that the vein does not flee and the blow is not missed. Because there are veins which are like a fleeing chord when phlebotomizing, and there are those which are full of windiness, and when you place the phlebotome upon them, they contract beneath the phlebotome, and the phlebotomist is deceived, and does not open the vein; and if he opens it, it is constricted.  Therefore it is necessary that the phlebotomist be firm and careful in all these things.
Then he should lay down the phlebotome. If therefore the vein opens at once, then it is good. And if not, then repeat it another time under that place slightly, or above it with haste, if the place is not ulcerated.
If, however, it becomes ulcerated, or the patient becomes weak, then leave him for a day or two, and do not tighten the bandage. For perhaps it will attract a hot ulcer. Nor shall he enter a bath.
Then he should repeat the bloodletting, if he desires.
If the opening is small, or the blood flows subtly, and you fear that the desired quantity will not drain from the blood, then repeat the bloodletting at the same spot, following the natural line, and add a little to the operation.
And do it quickly, before the place becomes ulcerated, for a small incision will result in an ulceration in many people.
If therefore you see it already ulcerated, then do not repeat the procedure on it at all, for it will not benefit you in any way. And place something of olive-oil dregs upon it, because it alleviates the flow of blood. And it is better in this place than the oil itself, and than other oils.
And similarly, administer olive-oil dregs in all your phlebotomies, when you want to prevent the flow of blood: and the Tyrian, alfarach, and sagzenea do this, when one of them is placed over the site.
For blood is made thinner and dissolved when it is thick.
If a large abscess occurs at the phlebotomy site, for this frequently happens, especially to someone who is not a phlebotomist except on that occasion, or if the vein opening is small, then quickly place a sponge soaked in warm water and salt over the area, and bind it for one hour, because it will resolve.
And it is necessary that you do this after the blood has flowed from the vein itself, when it is full, or from another vein.
If some blackness or greenness remains in the area after a few days, that does not harm it; and if you wish, place some dissolved aloe or myrrh over it, or something from the juice of mint, that is, balm, or something similar.
And frequently abscesses and swellings do occur at the Basilica phlebotomy.
Therefore, place your hand over it; then, if you find softness upon your compression over it, that swelling is bad. Therefore, be careful to place something of what we have said upon it. For perhaps blood will flow from it, of the artery.
But make a plaster on it with that in which is astringency, so that the place may be hardened. Then treat it with the remaining treatment, until it is healed.
And it is necessary that you extract from every man's blood according to the quantity of its virtue. And that which appears from the color prevailing over the blood.
For if the blood is black, then let it exit until it turns red; and likewise if you see it thick, then let it exit until it becomes thin; and likewise if it is sharp, until its sharpness is removed.
And it is necessary for one who is full, strong, and needs bloodletting at once, that the vein's incision be widened, and the phlebotomist be broad-minded; and in one who is weak, it be contrary to this.
And it is necessary that he extract it in stages, and that the opening be narrow; and better that which is administered in the bloodletting of the vein is that it be a transverse incision, not a puncture.
And this method of bloodletting is safe from blood flow, and from nerve incision; and it is more praiseworthy and safer than bloodletting by breadth and length.
And to whome it is accustomed to come to bloodletting syncope, it is necessary that he eat beforehand a moderate portion of bread soaked in pomegranate juice water; or secaniabin, if it is heated; and extract from him blood in three stages, or four.
And if he is of a cold complexion, then let him take, before bloodletting, bread soaked in quince syrup, or in syrup of aromatized honey with spices, or in fragrant aromatized syrup.
If syncope occurs during bloodletting, and its cause is the outflow of much blood, then it is necessary that he drink water from meat, and a subtle fragrant syrup; and let him use aromatics with allay, and let his chest be lined with it; and let him use the rest that we have said in the division in the chapter on syncope from evacuation.
However, whoever wishes to evacuate their arm and release their blood a second time, it is necessary for him whose bloodletting is for much evacuation, and whose strength is weak, to release the blood gradually, according to the amount of strength, over consecutive days.
However, whoever wishes to evacuate their arm and release their blood a second time, and their body is strong, then let them do it after seven hours, or nine, from their first bloodletting.
Whoever wishes to draw blood from their body to the opposite part to which it inclines, it is necessary that they be evacuated on the second or third day.
In whose body, however, there is much blood already heated, and sharp, and causing fever, it is necessary that blood exit from it in one turn, and a large quantity be drawn out of it; and the opening be increased until syncope occurs; after you have observed all the conditions of bloodletting; and that you place your hand upon his pulse near the flow of blood, so that death does not occur in place of syncope. For often that happens, when the bloodletter is ignorant and negligence occurs.
And it is not fitting, when you wish to release the arm and release blood a second time, when the opening of the vein has already closed and the exit of blood becomes difficult, that you press upon it with vehemence and rapidly stretch or bend it. For that is very harmful; indeed, leave it until it is bled a second time.
Or remove with sharpness the blood clot from the opening of the vein, and place upon it some dissolved salt in water. And place upon it something of theriac alfaroch, or sagzenea, and apply gentle pressure until the blood emerges.
If, however, the vein has already become suppurated, then leave it and do not touch it until the abscess has subsided.
If, however, necessity compels the drawing of blood a second time, and there is no excuse, then either bleed it above that spot, or in another arm in another vein.
However, the arm's vein is to be bled at the place of the Cephalic and Basilic veins, when these are not found and are hidden, since it is composed of them.
And the quality of the bloodletting is such that the infirm person should immerse their hand in warm water until the skin reddens, and the vein becomes clearly visible.
Then it should be tied above it with a ligature of moderate constriction. Then bleed it according to a transverse incision gradually, not laterally, nor lengthwise; and let the opening be wide, and your incision should be over the joint of the hand for a short while.
If the flow of blood is obstructed, then immerse the hand again in warm water, and allow the blood to flow into the water until you achieve your goal.
However, if you are in the days of summer, then you are excused from re-immersion of the hand in warm water. And indeed, the course of blood is mostly placed in warm water in the time of winter.
And the incision of this vein is safer than all veins, because there is no pulsating vein nor nerve beneath it.
Phlebotomy of the Saluatella vein of the right hand is beneficial for liver ailments.
And the quality of its phlebotomy is such that you should constrict the mahason hand with a bandage, or with your hand, after you immerse it in warm water, until the vein swells, and is very clearly apparent to the senses. Then incise it transversely. And if you cut it completely, that will not harm it at all.
And be careful not to cut your hand with the lancet. For beneath it are the nerves of the fingers laid bare of flesh.
Then apply the hand to warm water, and let the blood flow into it. For if you do not apply it to warm water, the blood clots in the vein's opening and is prevented from flowing.
When you have extracted the necessary quantity of blood, then put oil and salt over the vein: so that it does not clot quickly.
And similarly, it is necessary to do so with every constricted branch.
Indeed, the benefit of phlebotomy from the left hand is: because it is beneficial for illnesses of the spleen; and similarly do in its phlebotomy, as you did in the second equally.
However, the benefit of saphenous phlebotomy is for illnesses that are in the lower part of the body, such as illnesses of the womb, and retention of menses, and illnesses of the kidneys, and old ulcers of the thighs and legs, and similar illnesses.
And the method of his phlebotomy is that the sick person should put his foot in warm water, and make friction upon it until the veins swell, and tie above the joint of the foot with a strap. And indeed the vein's location is at the heel, appearing towards the big toe; and branches from it fan out into many branches on the foot's surface.
Therefore, phlebotomize it in its larger branch, or at the heel in its gathering; because it is better and safer.
If one phlebotomizes it on the foot's surface, then beware of the nerves which are beneath it upon the foot's surface.
And place your phlebotomy so that you phlebotomize it across, as if you wish to cut it; and let the phlebotomist be careful.
If, however, the flow of blood is obstructed, then repeatedly immerse his foot in warm water; and let the blood flow into it, until it is evacuated.
If the phlebotomist errs in the first instance, then let him repeat the phlebotomy a little higher up, for the place is safe, and you do not fear harm from it, when you beware of the nerves, as we have said.
And similarly with the saphenous vein of the other foot equally.
The Sciatic vein's location, as we have said, is at the heel on the wild side.
And phlebotomy is a help for the pain of the hip, when it is due to hot blood.
And the quality of its phlebotomy is that the sick person should enter a bath and hurry; and let him tighten his leg from the side of the hip up to the heel by four fingers, with a long thin bandage; for it does not appear except on account of that.
Therefore, when it appears, then the phlebotomist should cut it according to whatever ability is possible for you, or according to the transverse method; and it is better either that you cut it with an incision or that you sever it with a section. For its location is safer, and it is very hidden in many people.
For its location is safer, and it is very hidden in many people. If, however, you do not find it, and it does not appear to the senses at all, then the phlebotomist should cut any branch of it, and that is the one that appears on the back of the foot and toward the ankle; and beware of the nerves, and make the blood run in whatever quantity you wish.
Then release the constriction, and place a skin over the phlebotomy site, and tighten the location. For it quickly heals.
Concerning Cupping, and the quality of its use.
Cupping instruments are made from horns, and from air, and from glass.
And cupping indeed is done according to two methods. With scarification, and extraction of blood; and the other is cupping without scarification.
And this cupping which is without scarification, is according to two methods. Or indeed it is with fire. Or it is without fire.
And cuppings that are administered with scarification and extraction of blood are fourteen locations on the body. Of which one is, cupping Alnocrati, and it is the back of the head; and Alkehel, and it is the middle of the neck; and cuppings Alhagdahim, and they are two blades of the neck at both sides together; and cuppings menti, and it is under the jaw in the lower mouth; and cuppings of the two shoulder blades; and cuppings Alachosos at the end of the tail; and cuppings of the two armpits, and they are in the middle of the two arms; and cuppings of the two thighs; and cuppings of the two calves.
