And cupping indeed does not extract blood from you from small veins scattered in the flesh, and because of that no power is lost from them, just as is the case in phlebotomy. Nor should cupping, whether with fire or without fire, be administered in any illness that arises from fullness until the whole body is emptied.
If necessity prompts cupping from illness or for custom, we administer it at any hour at the beginning of the month, at the end of it, and in the middle of it, and at whatever time it may be.
This is because there are people in whom when blood multiplies in it, until it needs its extraction by cupping, finds heaviness and throbbing in their head.
And among them are those who find fullness and redness in their face, head, and neck. And among them are those who find itching in their face and forehead, and darkness and dullness in their eyes.
And among them is one who scratches the site of their cupping. And among them is one whose laughter multiplies. And among them is one who finds the taste of blood in their mouth: or the tongue becomes suppurated, and spits out blood. And among them is one whose sleep multiplies. And among them is one who sees in their sleep blood and redness, and slain ones, and wounds, and things similar to those.
Therefore, when we see any of these things, and especially if it is in the third middle of the month, we then order venesection to be performed, after two or three hours have passed of the day.
The benefit of venesection of the nape of the neck is: because it contributes to heaviness in the head, and that which flows to the eyes. However, it is necessary that it be after evacuation of the whole body.
And this venesection is in the place of a cephalic phlebotomy.
And it is not permitted for one of cold complexion, or in whom there is a cold, to use it. For it brings him great harm.
And because of that, old people and those with cold ailments in their heads should not use it.
But whoever is habitually over it, forgetfulness is generated in him.
And because of that, you should instruct the wind-dresser to lay his hand a little lower with the wind-pipe, fearing the generation of forgetfulness.
Wind-dressing, or Alkehel, is in the place of dark phlebotomy and basilica phlebotomy, and because of that it benefits asthma, and constriction of breathing, and fissure of the breathing instrument, and cough, and repletion.
And it is necessary that the Alkehel cupping be moved a little, because if it is placed lower, it generates weakness in the heart and stomach.
Wind-dressing of the hagdahm benefits accidental pains in the head, and ophthalmia, and hemicrania, and perforation, and pain in the roots of the teeth: and it is in place of basilica phlebotomy.
And it is necessary to instruct the wind-dresser not to press his hand with the scarification so that he does not cut an artery and cause a flow of blood.
Wind-dressing under the chin benefits sores in the mouth, and corruption of the gums, and similar diseases that are in the mouth: and it stands in the place of other phlebotomies that are on the two lips.
Wind-dressing of the shoulder blades benefits alchafacham, which results from flexion and heat.
Wind-dressing of the inner parts of the two flanks benefits the relief of phlebotomy of the three veins, Basilic, and Fusca, and Cephalic: because it draws blood from all the small veins that are in the flesh; and those small veins draw from other larger veins, until the drawing reaches the three veins.
And it is necessary that you instruct the wind-dresser not to proceed too far in the scarification, because the place is deprived of flesh, and beneath it are nerves and arteries.
A cupping applied over the alchazos benefits hemorrhoids of the anus and lower ulcers.
And it is necessary that you instruct the wind-dresser that the cupping be large and that it be made of air, because the place needs strong suction, and perhaps the delicate cuppings break; and it should be scarified with a large scarification.
Cupping of the legs reduces swelling noticeably, because they draw blood from the entire body and benefit old pains in the kidneys, uterus, and bladder; they provoke menstruation, and benefit botor, and ademenul; and they take the place of phlebotomy of the two saphenous veins and alhorcobeni.
However, they weaken the body greatly, and cause fainting in many people.
And the benefit of alhorcobeni cupping is close to the benefit of cupping the two legs.
The quality of placing cupping is that you should place the cupping initially empty, and suck with moderate suction; and do not prolong the placement of the cupping, but place them quickly and remove them quickly, so that the humors come to the place with equal arrival; and do not cease to repeat this and continue, until you see the place already red and swollen, and the redness of the blood appears; then, therefore, scarify, and repeat the suction gradually. Then consider the disposition of the bodies.
For those who are of tender flesh, of rare pores, it is necessary to scarify him once, no more: so that the place is not ulcerated.
And instruct the cupper to enlarge the scarification a little, and to deepen it slightly; and to moderate the suction with ease, speed, and gentle movement.
If there is thickness in the blood, then it is necessary to scarify him twice. Indeed, in the first instance, the way is opened to the thinning of the blood and its becoming watery; and in the second instance, to the searching out of the exquisite extraction of thick blood.
If, however, the blood is very foul, then let the scarification be done a third time, so that the final result may be achieved.
And finally, when we wish to extract blood gradually, or a little bit, one scarification is enough for us. If we wish to extract much blood, we scarify with many scarifications.
And if we measure that the blood is thick, then it is necessary that it be scarified with a deep scarification; and the equal limit in the scarification is the depth of the skin.
What is that which ought to be administered from the oils at the application of the Cupping-glasses, and what should the cupper beware of.
In that one whose skin is thick, hard, dark, and whose pores are narrow, it is necessary that the places to be cupped be anointed with oils that are opening, softening, and dissolving: if it is in the time of summer, such as the oil of alkeiri, or bruised oil, or oil of sweet almonds, or oil of pumpkin seed.
And if it is in the time of winter, then the oil of narcissus, or the oil of lily, or the oil of camomile, or sarbatic oil, and similar to these.
If however the superfluity is thick, cold: then the oil should be amaranth oil, or oil of balsam, or oil of alben, or oil of dill, and similar to these.
If however the cupper is of wide pores, tender flesh: then it is necessary that he abstain from oil, and it is necessary that they wash the places of the cuppings after cupping with rose water, or with cold water, or with water of solatri, or with pumpkin water, or with purslane water, and similar to these.
However, he whose blood is of much moisture, should wash the cuppings with vinegar, or with myrtle water, and sumac, and similar to these.
However, whose superfluities are thick, let him wash his cuppings with old wine, or with water of samsuci, or with a decoction of dill, or camomile, and similar to them.
And it is necessary that cupping be avoided in a bath, or in the vicinity of a bath; rather, it is necessary that it be administered after exiting the bath by the hour, or by two hours.
And it is not necessary that someone sleep after cupping.
What is it that should govern a Ventosandus, or Phlebotomist, before cupping?
And after it, it is necessary to consider first: if the cupper, or phlebotomist, is choleric, and sharpness and inflammation overcome his blood: then it is necessary that he take refrigerants, such as pomegranate juice, and also endive with vinegar and lettuce, and secaniabin, and iuleb, and similar to them.
And put his food as chickens, and the flesh of sheep sekebiget, and foods seasoned with agrimony; and similar to them.
And whose complexion is cold, it is necessary that he take in drink syrup of honey, or of almiua, or alsecaniabin made from seeds; and he should take medium fragrant wine, which is between old and new; and it is prescribed that he use a small amount of food. And put his food as chickens, and canary birds, and sparrows, that is, small birds, and young doves issiadebet.
And it is necessary that the drink on the day of cupping and bloodletting be more than the food; and it is necessary that in some men, in the hours of tyriaca alfaroch, or a medicine of musk, that is, musk, or alesselithe, be given in the drink before the cupping; and before or after bloodletting, so that the principal members are strengthened, and the blood is refined. And it is not necessary that they take it in heated drink.
Of cupping without scarification. However, cupping that is without scarification, is cupping that is placed upon the liver, and the spleen, and gout, and upon the belly, the navel, and the places of the kidneys, and the chest of the lungs; because these members do not endure scarification upon themselves.
And they are not sought for unless there is either an adhesion of blood from one limb to another limb; like when we place cupping on the nipples in the sickness of nosebleeds. And we use them to loosen cold, viscous wind from a limb; like when we place cupping on the belly and the navel. For it rarefies the limb and heats it, and removes pain because it dissolved that wind.
It is also placed upon the kidneys when obstruction or stone occurs in them. For by its power of attraction perhaps it opens the obstruction or uproots the stone from its place.
And similarly it does when placed upon the liver and spleen, in the case of wind to be crushed in either.
And these cupping vessels are administered empty, with only incision. And sometimes they are administered with fire.
And sometimes they are administered full of lukewarm water in cases of pleurisy; and that is, that the cupping vessel be filled, and be large, with only warm water, or water in which herbs suitable to the illness have been boiled.
Form of cupping vessel administered with fire.
Let its opening be the width of two open fingers, according to what we have shaped: and let its depth be half a palm: and let there be a small hole in its side, about the middle of it, according to the size that a needle may enter it: let it be made of brass, or tin, of thick, smooth, even, and polished edges, so that it does not injure the limb in its position on it: and let a transverse rod of brass or iron be in its middle, where the candle with fire is placed.
And now this cupping vessel is made large, larger than the one we have described, and a little smaller than that, according to the illnesses and the age of him who uses it.
For the cupping vessels of children and thin people are different from the cupping vessels of men and those with large bodies.
The method of placing this cupping vessel with fire on the limb is as follows: light a wick with fire made of linen suitably, or a small candle made of wax, and place it on the middle of the transverse rod that is in the middle of the cupping vessel, so that the fire is burning approximately at the lower part of the cupping vessel, so that the sick person's body is not burned.
Then it is placed on the limb, and a finger is placed over the hole I mentioned, as long as we hold the cupping vessel: when we need to, we remove the finger, and vapor will emerge through that hole, and the cupping vessel will immediately be released.
Then light the wick in its own way, and repeat it, if you need it.
However, in the cupping vessel administered in the illness of pleurisy with water, there is no transverse rod, nor hole; and it is not administered unless it is filled with water and placed on the limb only.
And this is its form.
And the larger this cupping vessel is, so that it can take much water, the better it is.
Concerning the suspension of leeches.
Leeches are not applied in many places, except in those limbs where cupping is not possible, due to their small size, such as the lip, and gums, and similar parts. Or on a limb stripped of flesh, such as a finger, and nose, and similar parts.
And the quality of using them is: that you obtain leeches which are in fresh, clean waters, free from corruption.
Then let them be kept in fresh water by day and night, until they are hungry, and nothing remains in their bellies. Then the body should be emptied primarily with bloodletting and cupping. Then wipe the sick limb until it turns red. Afterwards, place them upon it.
When, therefore, they are filled, and fall, and suction of the place with a cupping glass is possible, then it is more effective in helping: and if not, then wash the place with vinegar. Then with much water, and it is rubbed, and squeezed out.
If the flow of blood after the falling of the Sanguisug continues, and it is oozing: then soak a linen cloth in cold water, and place it on top, until the oozing is stopped.
If the blood multiplies, then powder on it ground zegi, or galls, and similar astringents, until the blood stops.
Or place on the area the halves of shelled beans: and leave them, until the beans adhere to the place, for the blood is stopped.
And if a repetition of the Sanguisug is necessary, when possible, change them.
If, however, the leeches are prevented from being suspended, then the place should be wiped with fresh blood. Or pierce the place with a needle, until something emerges from the blood. Then place them, for when they feel something of the blood, they immediately adhere.
Therefore, when you want them to fall off, sprinkle something of crushed aloe, or salt, or ash on them: and they will fall off immediately.
This chapter is also very necessary in the art of medicine, and it is the restoration of fractures and dislocations of accidents in bones.
Know, O children, that the foolish physicians and the common people boast about this chapter, and those who have never examined the books of the ancients, nor read a letter from them.
And for this reason, this method is private among the sciences in our land. And I have never encountered any good in it at all, and I have not attained that which I have attained in it unless because of the length of reading the books of the ancients, and the study upon understanding them, until I extracted the knowledge of it from them.
Then I adhered to experimentation and study for the length of my life.
And now I have described to you from it in this chapter everything that contains my knowledge: and upon which my experimentation has passed, after I have abbreviated it for you, and delivered it from the occupation of elongation, and abbreviated it by the end of abbreviation, and shown it by the end of declination: and I have formed in it many forms from the forms of instruments which are administered in it, since it is by addition of declination, as I did in the two preceding chapters.
Chapter II.
It is necessary, as we say in the prologue of this chapter, to sum up the discourse and the chapters, so that the artisan is first compelled to understand their truth: namely, so that you, and those who are ready, may know, or learn this noble art, and others from you.
I say, therefore, that when a fracture, or dislocation, or twisting, or fall, occurs to someone: then it is necessary, first of all, to bleed him, and to loosen him, or both simultaneously: if nothing prohibits it, such as weakness of strength: or if he to whom something of those things has occurred is a child, or an aged decrepit person: or if the time is of vehement heat, or of vehement cold very much.
Then let him be content in his food with cool vegetables, and the flesh of birds, and kids: and forbid wine, and coarse meats, and repletion from foods and every food that fills the veins with blood. Until when you are secure from a hot abscess, and do not fear the effusion of matter to the limb: then let the sick man return to his former regimen with which his habit ran.
When, therefore, the fractured bone begins to be restored, then it is necessary that the sick person be fed with nourishing foods, with much, thick, heavy nourishment, in which there is viscosity: such as foods made from cooked wheat with water, and rice, and heads, and feet, and bellies of cows, and eggs, and fresh fish: and thick wine, and similar things to them. For with this regimen, the coagulation of the fracture is made quicker and better.
And know that fractured bones, when they are in men who are in the first old age and decrepit, are not possible to be continued and consolidated according to their own nature always, because of the drying out of their limbs: and those that are from them, at the end of gentleness, are consolidated and continued, like the bones of young children.
However, nature attaches to the fractured bone from every side something similar to glue, in which there is thickness, by which it adheres to and binds it, until they are glued together and bound together, until it comes to the end of strength and firmness, as it was at first, until nothing hinders it from its operations: and for this reason it is necessary that you provide the sick person with nourishment of foods that possess weight, viscosity, and thickness, as we have said.
And know that the types of fractures are diverse, according to the diversity of the limbs: for the fracture of the thigh is different from the fracture of the skull; and the fracture of the breastbone is different from the fracture of the spine; and likewise all the other limbs are different from each other: and I have come with a reminder of every type of fracture, set out in its chapter, distinct from another. And the types of bone fractures are diversified even within themselves.
Because a fracture of it occurs in an arched shape, besides which splinters happen in it. And sometimes its fracture occurs along the length of the bone: and there are its fractures that are splinters, that is, splinters, and proceeding additions, as are in a reed when the knife is fitted for writing. And not proceeding: and there is a fracture with a wound and disruption at the head; and there is a fracture with a small fissure.
And every type has its own nature or instrument in its restoration, according to which its mending will come, distinct in its own places.
And from that by which the fracture of the bone is known, there is its twisting, and its own prominence, and its appearance to the sense, and its sound when you compress it with your hand.
Therefore, when there is no apparent twisting in place, nor sound upon touch when you touch the bone with agitation, nor does the sufferer experience great pain: then there is no fracture there; rather, it is possible that there is twisting, or a small fissure; therefore, you should not move it with the hand and pressure entirely, but rather place upon it from the medicines, of which mending comes, after the time that is suitable for the place; then a thin bandage should be tied.
And know that when a place is curved and divided into two, besides what exertions happen in it, yet each part is removed from its counterpart; then it is necessary for you to begin from your time for its straightening, making it even before a hot abscess happens to it.
However, if a hot abscess happens to it, then leave it for a few days, until the abscess subsides. Then make it even according to whatever manner is possible by gentleness and skill.
And know that the restoration and straightening of it is easier than of a bone, in which exertions occur.
Then bind it, according to what memory of it will come to you.
If, however, there are exertions in the bone, then the extension of this fractured limb is necessary, from two parts, if it is a hand or a foot: or with your hand, if the limb is small; or with two ropes, or with a rope and a hand.
And let your position be such that you place the limb upon an even surface, according to its natural shape, until, when the fractured parts of the limb are extended, then endeavor to restore those additions to their proper places with all your skill and in whatever way you can; and be careful, according to your strength, that no pain or harm comes upon the weak through your operation; and endeavor, according to your strength, to apply one of the two bones with its companion according to the best forms.
And it is necessary at that time to try and touch it with your hand. If, therefore, you see anything different there, correct it and equalize it by the amount of your strength.
And beware of excessive extension and strong compression, as many foolish people do. For very often they cause a hot abscess or contraction in the limb through their operation, as we have already seen in their operations many times.
Then, after the equalization, and proper operation, and constriction of that limb, and rest, and tranquility, advise the sick person concerning it. And make him beware lest he move it during his waking hours, and his sleep, at its turning, and its agitation, and at its evacuation, and all his movements with his utmost strength. And that he be careful that the position of the limb is not one that will follow with pain. Because, when he feels pain or injury in the position of the limb, then he must change it to another position in which he does not feel pain.
And be careful with him that his position is equal, straight, so that twisting does not occur in the limb when it is restored.
And the quality of the fracture of the limb is as I describe.
Know that fractured limbs are diversified in their smallness and their size and their shape.
Therefore, those of them that are small, such as the arm, and the finger, and the foot, and similar to them, then it is necessary that there be a wrapping of soft, thin cloth. However, those of them that are large, such as the thigh and the leg, it is necessary that there be a wrapping that is wide, hard.
Because a wide ligament connects a large limb and binds it from every side with equal tightness, so that no blemish enters it.
Then, after your evacuation, begin to apply a suitable liniment to it, in soft linen over the place of the fracture itself. Then begin to wrap the ligament over the place of the fracture itself with three or four wraps, according to what the limb deserves; and gently tighten your hand with the bandage.
Then proceed with it towards the upper part from the place of the fracture, and tighten less than was your tightening, with which you tightened the fractured place. Then draw away the bandage a little from the fracture site, and gradually loosen the tightness until you obtain a large amount from a healthy place.
Then take another strip, and wrap it again over the fractured place with wraps. Then proceed with the bandage towards the lower part from the fracture. And let your procedure be in the tightening of the bandage, and its loosening, according to what we said in the initial upper bandage.
Then place between the bandages soft wool or a cloth, with which the twisting of the fracture is equalized, if there is twisting in it: and if not, do not place anything in it.
Then wrap another strip over it. Afterwards, place over these bandages properly made strips immediately, if there is no swelling in the area, nor a hot abscess.
However, if there is swelling or an abscess in it, then place upon it whatever subsides the abscess and removes the swelling, and leave it for several days. Then tie strips over it.
And let the strips be from the middle parts of broad reeds, shaved and cleverly shaped; or let them be from wood from which sieves are made, which are made from pine, or gerard, that is, palm branches, or calings; and let the width of each strip, according to this shape and this same form, be equal.
However, it is necessary that the strip placed over the wound itself be somewhat thicker and wider than the other strips; and the length of the strip shall be according to what is permissible for the member, in terms of its size and smallness.
Then tie another strip over the strips, according to the first tightening. Then bind from above with threads neatly, according to what we said about tightening; and it is so that your tightening should be greater over the place of the fracture; and the further it is stretched from the fracture, the tighter it should be.
And it is necessary that the middle threads be of medium thickness and thinness, and that they be of soft flax.
Because if the threads are thick, as I have seen from the operation of foolish men who place their threads from twisted flax cords, it is a great error; because the tightening falls with them outside the proper quality.
And thin threads again are not suitable, because you will not achieve through tightening with them what you want.
And it is not necessary that there be less than a finger between splint and splint.
If the patient is injured by the ends of the splints after tightening in healthy areas, then place under them soft linen or dyed wool until nothing is damaged by it.
When, however, there is a fracture with a wound, and a rupture occurs in the skin, I will come with a reminder of it in the second chapter according to its singularity.
And know that not every broken limb needs to be tightened with splints from the first day. That is because when a limb is large, you should not place splints upon it unless after five days, or seven, or more, according to your safety from the event of a hot abscess.
And this is the method of plasters, by which the ancients restored those which are placed over a fracture and dislocation.
Method of an abbreviated common plaster, restoring a fracture, suitable for many complexions, especially women and children, because it is from that which neither heat nor cold overcomes.
And it is that you take from the mill's dust, and it is the flour's pith which hangs on the mill's walls by the grinding of the millstone: and make it as it is, except that you sift it with the white of an egg: and put its confection not thick, nor thin. Then use it.
Method of another plaster, restoring a fracture, and dislocation, and twisting. Take gum arabic, and laudanum, and acacia, and enula, and mugath, and juice of all, each 10 scruples; myrrh and aloes, both 5 scruples; and alethil 20 scruples.  Grind all, sift, and mix with alethil water; or with the white of an egg, if the patient's complexion is hot. Then use this plaster. For it is of good composition, quickly restores broken bones, and benefits many people.
Method of another plaster, which also benefits fracture and twisting. Take mugath, and mess, and white mallow, of all 10 scruples; myrrh and aloes, both 5 scruples; acacia 6 scruples; Armenian bole 20 scruples.  Grind all thoroughly, sift, and make into a paste with water, or the white of an egg; and administer.
Method of plaster for joints and bones separated from their places; and it relieves the pain that comes upon them, and the cracking and fracture of the bone. Take wool, soak it in vinegar and boiled oil, and place it over the affected area.
And in this plaster there is no virtue of restoration, but it is good in calming a hot abscess; and it expels pain properly.
Method of plaster, which restores a broken bone. Take leaves of the wild fig and leaves of the wild poppy; and grind them all together, and make a plaster from both.
Method of a shortened plaster, administered in the restoration of a fractured bone; and when you wish for the resolution of the remaining abscess. Take root of marshmallow, and chamomile, and violet flowers, and herb flour, of each one part: let all be ground, and mixed with wine, if the limb is not inflamed.
If however it is inflamed, then mix it with water of moist coriander, or with water, and use it.
Method of another stronger plaster in resolution, than this first one, when a hard abscess occurs in the restoration of a limb. Take root of marshmallow, and linseed, and pomegranate bark, and melilot, and sorrel, and violet flowers, and chamomile, of each one part; let all be ground, and mixed with willow water, or with fresh water, or with wine. All of that according to the warmth of the limb, and the quiet of its heat.
Hippocrates however did not say in his book that something be placed upon a fractured limb in its restoration, except for a cerote made from wax and oil: nothing else; and he related that it should be intermediate between thickness and thinness.
Galen, however, deemed that things that contain the drying of heat, such as myrrh, and aloes and frankincense, and similar to those that we have mentioned, should be placed on a fractured limb in its restoration.
Method of a plaster which is beneficial for injury and pain. Take equal parts of mugwort, chickpeas, cut human hair (or bird's feathers), marshmallow root, and salt: grind, sift, and mix them together; and make a plaster from it.
The amount of time, after which the bandage should be removed, is as follows: if the patient experiences no pain, no itching, and the fractured bone remains in place, then do not remove it for many days. If the patient experiences intense itching, or troublesome pain, or swelling at the site, then quickly remove the bandage immediately, and take the plaster off: take a soft cloth or soft sea sponge, moisten it in lukewarm water, and wash the area with it, until the itching subsides, and the pain is settled.
If the patient experiences intense itching, or troublesome pain, or swelling at the site, then quickly remove the bandage immediately, and take the plaster off: take a soft cloth or soft sea sponge, moisten it in lukewarm water, and wash the area with it, until the itching subsides, and the pain is settled. Then allow the limb to rest for one hour. Then place upon it a piece of finely cut wool soaked in vinegar and oil, or rose oil, and bind it upon it for one night, until you are sure of the absence of a hot abscess, and the swelling of the limb subsides, and its pain is removed.
Then apply a thin bandage and a small plaster, and do not tighten your initial bandage and the subsequent ones too tightly until it heals.
If you see that the abscess, redness, pain, swelling, and all the accompanying symptoms have completely disappeared, and you no longer need a plaster and bandage, then treat it again as you did at first equally.
If none of the things we have described happens to the limb, then do not loosen it until after three, or four, or five, or seven days. And when twenty days have passed, and the entire area, as you see from the loosening of the limb, as we said, is such that the fracture is adhered to, and the flesh has coagulated over it, then add more bandage than your first bandage altogether; and also add in the thickening of the weak substance, according to the remembrance of that which preceded.
However, if you see the fracture site already drying out and weakening more than necessary, then know that nourishment is prevented from reaching it. Therefore, embrocate it with lukewarm water every time you loosen it, and let it be there for all three days, and slightly loosen the bandage.
Through this operation, nourishment flows to the limb, and it heals quickly.
However, what foolish restorers do with a fractured limb a second time, when it is not restored at first as it should be, and is restored according to twists and turns, is an error of their operation, and great fear; and if it were correct, the ancients would have said it in their books, and operated with it.
And I have seen no trace of it anywhere in that at all, and it is better that it not be done.
Concerning the accidental injury to the head.
The species of fractures are diverse, and its forms numerous; and its causes are distinct.
For there is a fracture that occurs from a blow with a sword; and the blow removes the bone entirely, as far as it reaches the siphac, which is beneath the bone, just as an axe does in wood; and because of that, this species of fracture is called Axialis.
Or the sword cuts a portion of the bone, and cuts its surface, and the incision does not penetrate to its utmost extent; and this species of fracture is called Kalaan, that is, a lifting up absolutely.
And it is a wound of two fractures, either great or small.
And there is a fracture that is a crushing or grinding; and its cause is a blow with a stone, or a fall upon a stone, or similar things.
And this fracture is also either penetrating, which now approaches the soft tissue that is beneath the bone, or it is on the surface of the bone; and the wound of these two fractures is also either small or large.
And there is a fracture that is hidden within the bone, in the subtlety of a hair; and it is a small fissure, and because of that this species of fracture is called Capillaris.
And of them is a fracture that happens from a fall, or from a blow of a stone, and things like them, that cause a lamina of bone to enter its interior, and a cavity is formed; just as happens with copper kettles, when a blow hits them, and part of them enters the interior.
And this happens most often in the heads of moist bones, such as the heads of children.
And in all these types of fractures, separate efforts are made, and I will come with a reminder of the whole of it in its place.
And we recognize all these types of fracture by probing over them, and investigating them with probes, and removing the corrupted flesh that is over them.
The capillary type, however, is recognized by this, that it is detected over the bone, and scraped off, and smeared over it with ink: for the fracture appears black.
In the treatment of a fracture, first consider the patient's symptoms: then if you see from the symptoms that it signifies clearly a fear, such as vomiting, cholera, extension, loss of understanding, loss of voice, fainting, acute fever, protrusion of the eyes, redness of them, and similar symptoms in many dispositions, undoubtedly it will not be cured: and if you see symptoms not alarming you, and hope for recovery: then begin his treatment.
If a wounded person comes to you immediately after being wounded, and it is during the winter days, then it is necessary to strive for the removal of the bone before the fourteenth day according to every disposition.
And if it is during the summer days, then it is necessary to hasten to remove the bone before the seventh day, before the tissue beneath the bone becomes corrupted, and those symptoms which we have described occur.
If the fracture of the bone has already reached the tissue touching the brain, and is accompanied by pieces and pulling: then it is necessary to cut the broken and crushed part, as I describe to you.
And it is to shave the head of the wounded man and uncover the bone by any means possible to you, according to the shape of the wound, and what is easy for the patient.
If during the uncovering of the bone, a flow of blood or a hot abscess occurs, then apply to it what is necessary. And it is to fill the place with cloths soaked in wine and rose-water, and to keep the wound still until the abscess subsides and you are secure from the flow of blood. Then begin the treatment of the bone and its removal.
And that is done according to one of two methods.
One method is to cut the bone with a scalpel of a narrow edge, and this is its form.
Then administer another scalpel after the first scalpel, somewhat wider; and this is its form.
Then administer yet another scalpel, wider still than the second one.
And the second method is that you should have a number of different scalpels; some of which are wider than others, and some shorter than others; and let the ends of their blades be sharp, and let them be of tempered steel, or iron; and use ease in striking on the scalpel, so that it does not strike the head, and injure it.
However, if the bone is strong, hard: then it is necessary that you pierce around it, before you administer the scalpels, with drills that are called drills, not penetrating deeply; and they are not called so, except because they do not pass through the boundary of the fleshy bone to that which is behind it; because the drill has a rounded end on that which is beneath its sharp, pointed head, similar to a margin, and a small circle prevents it from sinking in, and passing through the thickness of the bone.
And it is fitting for you to take from these drills a multitude, each fitting the thickness of the bone, until you have a drill for every skull; whose sharp end in length and shortness should be according to the thickness of that skull.
And this is the form of three types of drills, or trepans.
The method of piercing around the fractured bone is: that you place the drill upon the bone, and revolve it with your fingers, until you know that the bone has now been pierced. Then change the drill to another place, and put what is between every hole the thickness of a radius, or approximately that.
Then cut with the chisels what is between each hole out of the bone, and make it finer than you can above it from the end of lightness, as we have said, until you remove the bone, either with your hand or with any other of the instruments you have prepared for it: such as pliers, and fine forceps.
And you must take every precaution that the drill, or the chisel touches nothing of the flesh.
Therefore, when you have encircled the bone by piercing, and it has been separated from the flesh, if it has been continuous with it, and you have extracted it, you must smooth and equalize the end of it that remains in the bone with another similar instrument, a chisel. However, it must be thinner and more delicate than other chisels.
If any small, stubborn pieces of bone remain, then remove them easily with the instruments you have. Then treat the wound after that with the dressings and ointments that we have mentioned.
And another method of operation is easy, entirely free from fear: which Galen said, and praised greatly; and this is his statement. First, you must begin by exposing the part of the bone where the fracture is more severe and obvious; until, when you have exposed that part, and have placed the end of this lenticular chisel beneath it, whose shape is this.
And the lenticular part, smooth, does not cut anything; and its sharp part running lengthwise on its two sides, as you see; so that the lenticular part rests on the bone, its sharp face meeting the bone.
Then strike the chisel from one side with a small mallet until you cut through the entire bone with ease, just as it revolves; and you be sure, from the bandage, that no accident happens to it, which you fear completely.
And if the physician were not more foolish than men, and more ignorant of it, nor were he sleeping, then if anything remained attached to the bone from the bandage in some places, he would cut it away from it with the end of his lenticular chisel, and free it from it with ease. It is freed from it without harm, and without fear.
And if the fracture of the bone does not penetrate to the bandage, and that which is cut away from the bone is its surface, and roughnesses and fine irregularities remain on it: then it is necessary to smooth away that roughness and scrape away those irregularities with fine scrapers of various sizes which you have prepared, so that it may be possible for you to apply those that are most suitable and most correct in each place, according to that to which the operation leads you, and the fractured bone itself, and its shape. And it is necessary that you apply, in your judgment, the wider scrapers first for scraping the bone. Then use a finer one after it.
And it is necessary that you apply, in your judgment, the wider scrapers first for scraping the bone. Then use a finer one after it. And do not cease to do this in a continuous manner until you reach the point of applying the thinnest and finest of them all.
In each of the remaining small fissures, and fine fractures, it is necessary to apply care, according to that which leads to its straightening.
And it is a matter not hidden from anyone who has any concern in this art, and who has stood upon what we have written and shown in the case of large fractures.
If, however, a flap is found and retained when you remove the bone, then it is necessary to take a linen cloth, according to the size of the wound, and soak it in rose oil, and place it over the wound opening. Then take another cloth, doubled or tripled, and soak it in wine and rose oil, and place it over the first cloth; and do it as easily as you can over it, so that it is not burdened by this.
Then apply a broad bandage over it; and do not tighten it, unless by a quantity that only holds the wound together; and leave it with this treatment for one day, or two days, until you are sure of being free from a hot abscess. Then loosen it, and apply after that medicines with which superfluous dryness is overcome: such as lily roots, and herb flour, and frankincense powder, and aristolochia, and similar to them; and it is that you make from these medicines a powder, which is powdered over the wound as they are dry.
And universally apply to it every medicine of whose property it is to cleanse, and not to bite. And beware according to your ability, that the wound in your treatment be clean; neither be there dirt of ointment, nor filth.
And beware according to your ability, that the wound in your treatment be clean; neither be there dirt of ointment, nor filth. Nor allow any pus to collect there at all; for when pus collects on the brain membrane, it corrupts it and putrefies it, and great harm comes upon the sick person after that; and this happens in the scalp, when the bone is exposed, especially when its treatment is neglected, a blackness on its surface.
Therefore consider, if the sick person experiences the accidents that we have mentioned, know that it is undoubtedly dangerous.
And if blackness does not occur, unless from the medicine placed upon it, and it is in the power of that medicine to produce that blackness: then it is necessary to take one part of honey, three parts of rose oil, and to line a cloth with them. Then place it upon the scalp; afterwards treat it with the kinds of treatment that are appropriate, until it is healed.
Concerning the restoration of the Nose when it is broken.
Know that only the two upper parts of the nose are broken simultaneously, or one of them; because it has two bones.  Since the lower part is cartilaginous, it is not broken, and only abrasion, twisting, and distortion befall it.
If one of the two parts is broken, then it is necessary to insert the little finger into the nostril, and to straighten that fracture with your index finger and thumb from the outside, until the nose returns to its natural shape; and do this skillfully and easily. And beware lest your operation cause pain to the sick person.
If the fracture is in the upper part of the nose, and the finger does not reach it, then it is necessary to straighten it with the end of a radius bone, where there is a small thickness.
If the fracture is in two parts, then do likewise; and hasten its restoration on the first day of the fracture, if possible. And if not, then after the seventh or tenth day, at rest from a hot abscess.
Then insert into the end of the nose a bandage of linen cloth, if the fracture is in one part; or insert two bandages if the fracture is in two parts; and let the bandage have a thickness such that it fills the nasal opening.
And some of the ancient restorers said that the bandages should be soaked in butter, and changed every day. However, that does not seem to me appropriate; rather, the bandage should be soaked in egg white, mixed with mill dust. Then leave the bandage until the bone is strengthened and the cartilage hardened.
And sometimes a goose feather is inserted into the nose in place of a bandage, after a soft cloth is wrapped around it; and it is done so that it holds the fracture of the nose more firmly and that the weak person is not prevented from breathing in.
And this is not necessary. If you wish, do it; and if you wish, do the bandages.
If it happens again while treating a hot abscess, plaster the nose with cerote, or with cot infused in rose oil, or a little diaquilon.
If however a hot abscess does not occur, then it is necessary that you plaster it with sesame flour and frankincense powder mixed with egg white. Then place soft linen upon it, and do not bind the nose at all.
If, however, the bones of the nose are broken, or fractured slightly, then it is necessary that you divide them, and extract them with instruments suitable for them. Then sew the wound, and treat it with whatever causes flesh to grow and consolidates it with suitable plasters or ointments.
If a wound occurs between the nostrils, then it is necessary that you treat it with bandages and administer a lead cannula until it heals.
Concerning the restoration of the lower jaw, when it is fractured.
When the lower jaw is fractured, and the fracture is not accompanied by a wound, then look if the fracture is only on the outside and does not break into two pieces, but bends inward: then its recognition is easy.
Therefore, if the fracture is on the right side, you should insert the index finger of your left hand into the patient's mouth; similarly, if the fracture is on the left jaw, then insert the index finger of your right hand; and push the bend of the fracture from inside towards the outside with your other hand from the inside of the bone properly, and thereby straighten it.
If the fracture of the jaw is already separated into two pieces, then it is necessary to apply traction from both sides, according to the straightness, until its alignment is possible.
If, however, movement or breakage has already occurred in the teeth, then tie those which you hope will remain with gold, silver, or silk thread. Then place a poultice over the fractured jaw. Then place a doubled cloth over it, and put a large, properly made pledget on the cloth; or a piece of leather sole of equal length to the jaw.
Then bind it from above, according to the preparation for its ligature and its application is suitable, until it is not broken; and instruct the sick person to use quiet and rest; and give him food that is easily swallowed.
If, however, you estimate that something of the shape has already been altered in some way, then hurry and loosen it on the third day. Then rectify what has been altered from it, and plaster it with mill dust, with egg white, or with sesame flour, after you have removed the poultice; and place soft wool over the plaster.
While therefore that plaster remains attached to it, and the arrangement of the bone is not altered, leave it, do not loosen it, until it is healed; or tighten the fracture. For this fracture is often tightened in three weeks.
If, however, a hot abscess occurs in the meantime, then administer that which we have said many times for its subsidence, until that abscess is removed.
And if the fracture is with a wound, consider. For if a piece is already separated from the bone, or from the splinters, then be meticulous in this, so that you remove those splinters with whatever suits you in removing them from the instruments.
If truly the wound opening is constricted, then enlarge it with a phlebotomum according to your necessary quantity.
Then, when you have removed those splinters, and nothing remains from them, close then the wound opening, if it is large; and if not, place over it from plasters which are appropriate for it, and cause it to heal, until it is healed.
Concerning the restoration of the Furcula, when it is broken.
Indeed, the Furcula is most often broken from the anterior part, at the shoulder, and its fracture occurs in one of three ways.
For it is either broken and separated into two, besides which splinters may occur in it; and it is easier to restore it.
Or, so that splinters occur in the bone: and it is more difficult to restore it.
Or so that it is a fracture with a wound.
And the operation in it, when the fracture is without a wound, is that you should have two assistants present, so that one of them holds the support that follows the fractured Furcula, and the other extends the neck towards the part.
Then equalize the fracture with your fingers, until its form is as it should be: and there should be neither a prominence nor a concavity in it.
If you need more extension, then you should place under the patient's armpit a sphere of cloth or wool: and its size should be according to your need: and extend and lift the Furcula, and narrow the sphere with your hand, until you equalize the fracture as it should be.
If you cannot pull the extremity of the Furcula outwards, because it has reached a depth, then it is necessary that the patient be laid on their back and you place a pillow of medium size under their shoulder, and let the assistant press on their shoulder downwards until the bone of the Furcula that is in the depth is lifted upwards; and then straighten the fracture and equalize it with your fingers.
If, however, you feel a splinter from the Furcula and it moves, then you should cut over it and extract the splinter with ease.
If it is however retained in the bone, then make an incision on it with one of the scalpels you have prepared for it, after you place an instrument that preserves the sifach beneath the Furcula: and it is an instrument of wood or iron. And this is its form.
It resembles a spoon that has no cavity.
And let its width be according to that of which you have need, based on the size and smallness of the bone. Its length, however, also according to what is possible for you in the operation.
And let it have two ends, as you see: and one end be wide, and the other narrow.
If however the rupture of the wound that you made when you extracted the bone splinter is wide, and you are secure from a hot abscess: then bring together the two edges of the wound with stitches.
And if the rupture is small, or you fear a hot abscess: then fill the wound with cloths and lint according to the size of the wound's tear.
If a hot abscess should occur, then immerse a cloth in rose oil, vinegar, and wine, and place it upon it.
A constriction of the bone, however, when it is without a wound, and without an incision, is to place upon the bone a plaster made from mill flour and egg white. And place soft lint upon it. Then place a sphere under its projection, if you need that. Then take a himematem, that is, a wheel which the Saracens hold, which is very long and whose width is about one palm. Then place a doubled pad over the lint and plaster. Then take a thin piece of wood, whose width is three fingers and likewise in length. Then wrap it in cloth. Then wrap that cloth with the stick in the place of the alhimemati which falls upon the fracture and the location. Then place alhimemati over the fracture as it revolves, and wrap it over its neck, and under its healthy and under its infirm titillico: and reduce it over every part, and as you see that constriction holds the fracture in proper constriction: and that is, that it is not hidden from you, and all, so that the stick does not remove itself from over the fractured bone. And therefore it is necessary that you watch over the infirm person every day. Therefore, whenever the ligament softens, and you see the stick already removing itself, then straighten it and tighten the ligament.
Then place the patient's sleep on his back, and place a small pillow under his arm at his sleep at night, so that with it his assistance might be lifted from his side. Therefore the fracture of the forearm is lifted by the lifting of the humerus.
Or bind his arm to his neck, and do not loosen the ligament if it does not happen in a place that happens from itching, or from an abscess, until twelve days.
Then renew the plaster, if you see its face to it. And add in constriction, and leave him until he is restored and coagulated.
And indeed a fracture of the forearm is tightened and strengthened most of all in twenty-eight days, and sometimes it happens in some people in a shorter time.
Concerning the restoration of a fractured shoulder blade.
The shoulder blade is rarely fractured, and at its broad part; and only its edges are fractured from it.
Therefore, when a part of it is fractured, and it is broken in its middle, and that is not recognized except by touch, then according to the nature of the fracture, attempt its preparation. And reduce it to its natural shape by every means of skill that is possible for you.
Then place flour of mill with the white of an egg, and soft linen, and put a little cushion of folded cloth on top. Then place upon it a broad splint of thin wood, according to the size of the whole shoulder blade, or a little larger.
If however there is a hollow under the splint in any part of the shoulder blade, then equalize that hollow with soft linen until the splint comes down to a level.
Then bind over it with a long bandage, with suitable constriction, and firmness of the splint, until it does not move from its place.
And consider the bandage every day. Whenever therefore the bandage softens, tighten it, and adjust the splint if it moves from its place; and let the sick person lie on their healthy side.
And the shoulder blade indeed is restored in twenty days, or in twenty-five days.
When therefore this number is completed, then loosen the bandage; and you will be in safety. For it is from bones whose union is not feared, nor their separation.
If however a piece of bone protrudes, and pierces beneath the skin: then cut over it, and remove it. And do what we said in the fracture of the collarbone from the resolution of a hot abscess, if anything happens from it.
Concerning the restoration of a fractured breastbone.
The breastbone sometimes breaks in its middle, and that rarely happens. Its extremities, indeed, are broken and crushed most often from its accidents.
When its middle breaks, which inclines downward, and a violent pain, and difficulty in breathing, and coughing occur to him; and perhaps he vomits blood, and a concavity is revealed in the fractured bone, and not hidden from the senses, its restoration is that the sick person be laid supine on his back, and a pillow be placed between his scapulae.
Then press his shoulders, and gather the ribs from both sides with your hands, and make them equal in their alignment, according to every manner in which it is possible and appropriate, until the shape of the bone returns as it should.
Then place a plaster and linen bandage over it, and place a thin, light board made of willow or similar material, after you wrap it in cloth, on top of that. Then adjust the binding over the fractured bone so that it does not move, and go around the back with the bandage several times, and bind it with a suitable tightening.
Then check the bandage every hour, and whenever it softens, tighten it; and if its necessity calls for its removal due to itching or the corrosion that occurs in the place, or pain, or an abscess, then quickly remove it and take off the plaster, and correct whatever has happened with the manner of its treatment.
Then replace the plaster, if you see a need for it: and apply a bandage to it until it heals.
Concerning the restoration of ribs when they are broken.
Know that a fracture of the ribs does not occur except in the thick areas following the back, and from their extremities at the front. And attrition does not occur to them unless because they are cartilaginous. And its knowledge is not hidden from the sense in examination with the fingers.
And its restoration is done so that one aligns the fracture with the fingers as much as possible, until the shape is equal, as is fitting. Then apply plasters, and bind the fractured bone with splints, if it needs them.
And if the fracture of the ribs is sloping inward, then a great pain and a stabbing occur to the sick person, like the stabbing that occurs to one who has pleurisy: because the bone stabs the lining, and difficulty in breathing, and coughing, and vomiting of much blood also occur to him; and this is a difficult cure.
And the ancients were ingenious in it with many devices: and among them are those who say that you should give the sick person foods that generate inflammation and windiness, so that the belly will become inflamed and distended, and that the fracture will be driven outward.
But we abhor this, lest a rapid cause for a hot abscess be made, if it does not occur. If however it has already occurred, then add to it.
And some of them said: Let a windy substance be placed over the spot, then it should be sucked with force, and this is a similar reasoning. However, it is feared that the windy substance will attract superfluous fluids to the spot, due to the weakness of the condition.
And some of them said: It is necessary that the spot be covered with wool that has already been immersed in hot oil; and cushions should be placed between the ribs, until it is filled; so that there is an even binding when you wrap it around the rounded shape.
Then the sick person should be treated with the treatment for pleurisy, using food and medicine.
If a violent matter, which the patient cannot tolerate, constricts the patient, and the mouth pricks or the covering hurts with a piercing wound, and we fear for the patient, then it is necessary to cut over the spot and expose the broken rib. Then we shall place upon it the instrument that preserves the sifac, the description of which preceded; and we shall cut the bone with ease, and cut it.
Then we shall bring together the two edges of the wound, if it is large, with sutures; and we shall treat it with plasters, until it is healed.
If meanwhile a hot abscess occurs, then hasten, and infuse lint into rose oil, and place it over the spot; and let the patient be treated with that which suits the abscess.
Meanwhile, let him lie on his side on which he sleeps, until he is healed.
Concerning the restoration of a fractured spine of the back and neck.
A fracture sometimes occurs in the bones of the neck, and attrition rarely happens to them; and similarly with the vertebrae of the back. Therefore, when this happens to someone, and you wish to know whether it will heal or not, then consider. For if you see his hands already softened, and numb, and dead; and he cannot move them, nor extend them, nor contract them; and when you contract them with your fingers, and prick them with a needle, he does not feel it, and does not feel pain in them: then know that it will not heal, and in most cases is mortal.
But if he moves them, and feels the contraction with the fingers and the prick, then know that the neck bone is sound, and that the sick person will be cured with treatment.
If the same happens to the vertebrae of the back, and you wish to know whether it will heal or not, then consider his feet.  If you see that they are softened, and that the same happens to them as to the hands; then when he lies on his back, wind and excrement are expelled involuntarily; and when he lies on his stomach, excrement is expelled involuntarily; and when he lies on his back and wishes to urinate, and cannot: then know that it is mortal. Therefore, do not labor in his treatment.
If, however, nothing of that happens to him, the condition is less serious.
And the treatment for what happens from that is, to attempt to soothe a hot abscess: thus, to place on the vertebra crushed rose oil alone, or with the yolks of roasted eggs.
Place that on it several times a day, until when the hot abscess is soothed; then place a strengthening, drying plaster on the area and bind it with a bandage; and prescribe to the sick person rest and tranquility. Nor should he sleep on the part where he finds pain until he is healed.
If it happens at a point where the bone is fractured, or something already separated from it: then it is necessary to dissect above the skin and remove it. Then bring together the two edges of the wound, if it is large, with sutures. Then treat it with some kind of healing ointment until it is healed.
If, however, the last bone of the tail is fractured, and it is the end of the tail: then it is necessary to insert the thumb of the left hand into the anus, and to align the fractured bone with the other hand, as far as possible, with a second adjustment.
Then place it over it, and remove it: and treat the wound with the treatment that preceded, until it is healed.
Concerning the restoration of the Hip.
Chapter XI.
The bones of the hips are rarely broken. If they are broken, then their fracture is not unless they are shattered at the extremities: and they are split lengthwise, and are deflected inward: and the patient experiences pain and a puncture in the area, and the leg that is fractured becomes numb.
And its restoration is to extend your hand over it, until you stand over the fracture as its shape is.
If the fracture is only at its extremities, then equalize that fracture according to what is prepared for you by equalizing, until its shape is made similar to its natural shape.
If however the fracture is lengthwise, or already inclines inward, then make the patient lie on their stomach, until the restoration of that fracture is prepared for you. When therefore you have equalized it, place a plaster over it; then place a splint of wood, or of leather, over it; and bind it tightly with a bandage so that you fear neither a change in the fracture nor the removal of the splint.
And equalize the concavity of those things with what fills it, until the bandage takes it according to equality; and command the patient to sleep on their back, or on their healthy side.
If however a hot abscess occurs to him, then cease from its extension and its restoration until the hot abscess subsides; and place upon it that which settles it, according to what has gone before, then return to its restoration; and bind it as is proper.
If however it occurs in the bone and is shattered or crushed, remove it.
On the restoration of the fracture of the arm.
The arm is that which is between the elbow and the shoulder blade.
If therefore it is broken, then its restoration is according to one of two methods.
One of which is: that you should take a piece of wood of medium thickness, according to this form, which follows.
And two bandages are tied at the two extremities.
Then it should be suspended from a raised place, and the sick person should sit on a seat. Then let him place his broken arm over the wood, until it reaches the tickle of it, in the middle of which the curvature of the wood is attached.
Then suspend something heavy above it, so that the minister extends it downwards. Then the physician should align the fracture with his hands together, until the fracture returns to the way it should be.
And another method is that the sick person should lie on their back on their neck; and suspend his hand from his neck with a bandage. Then command two ministers, that one of them should hold that which is above the fracture with his hands, and that the other should hold below it, and that each of them should extend to their side.
And if you wish the extension to be stronger, then bind below the fracture with a bandage, and above it with another bandage, and let each of the two ministers extend them to their own sides.
If therefore the fracture is near the extremity of the humerus, then it is necessary that one bandage be placed below the armpit, and another below the fracture towards the elbow.
And similarly if the fracture is near the elbow, then it is necessary that a bandage be placed over that place, and over the elbow itself.
Then equalize the fracture with ease without labor, until when the fracture is equalized as it ought to be, and a good application is applied: then bind it, if a hot abscess does not happen.
If however a hot abscess occurs, then leave the binding until the seventh day. And place over it cut wool soaked in vinegar and rose oil, until when the abscess subsides: then bind it: and the manner of binding it is that you place a plaster over the fracture.
Then place a bandage of new cloth over the plaster. Then let the arm be supported by that support, and let him place his open hand on his shoulder.
And place a cloth, and bind the arm over the support, so that the arm stands in the position of stars: if nothing hinders you from that, nor is anything of the broken bone disturbed on you.
If you fear that anything of the broken bone may be disturbed on you, then use splints: and it is to place a splint over the fracture itself that is wider and stronger than the other splints. And place the width of a finger between each splint; and let the length of the splints be according to the fracture with the addition of three fingers on every side.
Then bind over the splints the binding that I have spoken of at the beginning of the chapter.
And it is to be that your binding be tighter over the place of the fracture. And the more the fracture is lengthened, the less the binding should be.
If therefore you see the position of the splints and the binding, as we have said, in the hour in which you restore the limb immediately, then do so; and if you fear a hot abscess, then leave the binding and the splints until the seventh day, as we have said.
Then consider the ligament for all three days, so that itching or swelling does not occur in the place, or that food is prevented from reaching the limb, because of the disposition of excess fluid from the binding. Therefore, rectify it all, according to what we have said.
If however you are confident that nothing will happen from it, then do not loosen the binding, unless after three days, or more.
And let the patient's sleep be on his back, and his hands on his stomach; and place under him a mattress filled with wool evenly.
And consider at every hour of night or day, that the fractured limb's shape is not dislodged, or that the binding is loosened. Therefore, rectify it all with your skill.
And give the patient food according to the order we have prescribed, so that initially the food be subtle, until the mouth begins to tighten; then it is necessary to increase his food.
For it is the custom of the wound and leg that it tighten in forty days; then, therefore, it is necessary that it be loosened, and a bath be administered, and that it be treated with ointments which rectify it.
If the fracture is foul, bruised, then do not loosen the bandages and splints from it until fifty days, and up to two months.
Concerning the restoration of a fractured arm.
The arm is composed of two bones, which are called two forearms.
One of which is small and it is that which follows the thumb. And the other is large, and it is that which is situated beneath the small one at its lower part.
Perhaps therefore only the larger forearm is broken, or the smaller one; and perhaps they are broken simultaneously.
When therefore the smaller forearm is broken above, then its restoration is easy, and its healing is easier.
And when the forearm is broken below, its fracture is bad, and its healing is difficult.
And worse than that is when both bones are broken simultaneously.
If therefore the bone that is broken is the smaller forearm above, then it is fitting for the physician, at its restoration, to apply a small extension with ease, until it is made even.
If however the large forearm is that which is broken: then it is necessary that he apply a much stronger constriction.
And it is necessary that he place a figure of the hand at its restoration, and its extended extension upon a cushion. And let the thumb of the hand be higher than all the fingers: and let the lower joint be below all the fingers, or the rest: and the patient sitting square upon himself, and let the cushion be before him, or in line with him, in height, so that the patient does not incur hardship.
Then let the attendant extend the arm from the lower part, either with his hand, or with a bandage. And another attendant should extend it from above in the same way.
Then the physician should adjust the bone until he restores it to the best possible form.
If in the fracture of the bone there are fragments, then endeavor to return every fragment to its place by your skill.
If however a separated fragment appears in it, and it pierces the skin, and there is no hope for its restoration for you: then dissect over it, and remove it according to the manner which we have said in those which are previously mentioned.
If however the fracture is with a wound, I have already made a special chapter on it, therefore take the treatment from there.
If however a hot abscess occurs at the beginning of your restoration, then line a cloth with a cerate made from rose oil and white wax, and let it be intermediate between thickness and thinness; and bind the cloth over it with a subtle constriction, until when the abscess is calmed: then remove the cerate, and apply a plaster prepared from mill powder with egg white. Then place splints, and let the splint placed over the fracture itself be slightly wider and stronger.
And know that the number of splints for the arm is six in many arrangements, whether the fracture is in one bone, or in two bones together.
Then apply your bandage to the site of the fracture more strongly and forcefully, and somewhat more with the bandage towards the upper or lower part of the fracture; or apply a gentler bandage, a bit less constricting than the one previously mentioned in the beginning of this chapter.
And let the cloths wrapped around the fracture be soft, moist; and let them not be hard, rough. And let the thread with which it is tied be of linen, properly, of a medium thickness between fine and coarse, as we have described.
And consider the limb and the ligament after days. If anything requiring rectification occurs, such as itching in the limb, then it is necessary to bathe the limb in warm water until the itching subsides, and to leave the limb unbandaged until it subsides, then reapply the bandage.
If the bandage is now softened, and the bone is now removed, and similar things to these: then rectify it all with your skill, and consider again.
For if nourishment is prevented from reaching the limb, due to the excessive tightness of the bandage, then it is necessary to soften it a little, and to leave it for a few days, until nourishment flows to it, then bandage it again.
If, however, nothing of what we have said happens to the sick person, then it is not necessary to loosen it, unless after twenty days, or approximately this many.
Then the sick person's hand should be suspended from his neck. And his neck should be level, and be protected by his own skill from violent movements. And make him sleep on his back.
And know that this fracture is restored in thirty days, or in thirty-two days: and perhaps it is restored in twenty-eight days. All that is according to the dispositions of the complexions, and the dispositions of the strength.
Concerning the restoration of fractures of the Hand.
Know that fracture of the hand's knuckles and the nodes of the fingers rarely occurs. And it does not occur to it unless by very frequent friction.
Therefore, when a fracture or abrasion of the sole occurs, then it is necessary that the sick person sit square; and before him be a seat according to equality. Then place his hand upon it extended.
Then let the attendant extend the broken bones, and let the physician adjust them, until when they are joined by a good union.
Then it is necessary that he apply a plaster and linen bandage, if a hot abscess does not occur. Then place a pad over it according to the amount of the area; and you have already wrapped it in linen cloth.
If however the fracture is towards the lower part of the sole of the foot, then make something like a sphere out of cloth; and command the sick person to compress it with their fractured foot; then bind it with a long cloth.
And let the pad be of leather, in which there is softness, so that the leather is applied to the sole of the foot; and bind it, according to what is necessary.
If however the fracture is to the outside, then it is necessary: that you place a pad on top, and another pad from below on the sole; and let the hand be straight and open. Then place a bandage, as the hand is rotated; and stretch it between the fingers with a ligature.
If, however, a fracture occurs in any of the finger joints, then if it is the thumb, it should be straightened as necessary. Then bind it to the sole; and if you wish, place a small straight pad on it so that the fracture is elevated and does not move.
If, however, the fracture is in the remaining fingers, such as the middle, and index, or ring, and little finger, then it should be straightened, and bound to the healthy finger that follows it; or all should be bound in sequence; and this is better. Or place a small pad on it, as we said for the thumb.
And consider in your treatment, and after the resolution of the hot abscess: and apply what is necessary, when anything from it happens, according to the repetition of its description.
Concerning the restoration of a fractured hip.
The hip bone is often fractured, and it is revealed to the senses, because it is turned inward and backward.
And its restoration is done thus, that a ligament is tightened above the fracture, and another ligament below the fracture. And the sick person lies prostrate on their face.
Then let the attendant stretch every ligament on his side equally; and this, when the fracture is in the middle of the bone.
However, if the fracture is near the root of the hip, then let one ligament be on the side of the crest, so that the extension falls upward; and another ligament below the fracture.
And similarly, if the fracture is near the knee, then let the ligament be near the knee, so that the extension is downwards.
Then the physician shall equalize the bone with both of his hands, until he restores it to the likeness of its natural shape; and the bone is joined with good union.
Then it is necessary that you apply a plaster and a bandage, if a festering sore does not occur to the limb. If a festering sore occurs there, then leave it for several days until the hot festering sore subsides, then return to your treatment.
However, its bandage should be like this. It is necessary that you wrap a hard bandage over the fracture, that is, a bandage, and double it twice or thrice, and let there remain surplus of it. Then wrap the leg until the heel reaches the root of the buttocks.
And insert a long thread between the thigh and the leg near the knee to the lower parts, and let the ends of the thread come from the upper part of the two parts. Then revolve over the leg and thigh what is in excess of the bandage.
Then place on the thigh, at the site of the fracture itself, splints, and place one splint upon the bone of the leg.
Then fill the spaces between the leg and thigh with soft cloths, so that the tightness is equalized.
Then begin with a strong bandage from the middle over the fracture site in three or four turns. And the further you move away from the fracture site with the bandage, the less tight and softer and gentler your bandage should be.
Then pull on the two ends of the thread, which you have inserted between the thigh and leg. Tie together the three splints that are on top. Then go with the two ends of the thread to the lower parts, until you reach the ankle of the foot. And with both ends tighten also the ends of the splints on the other side, so that the bandage does not shift from its place.
Then loosen the tightening on it, while it remains, so that no ulceration, or abscess, or swelling, and similar things, befall the limb. If any of those things happen. Therefore, hasten with its solution, and rectify whatever has happened from that, entirely, according to what we have said many times.
If however there is a piece of bone from the bone that pricks: then it is necessary, that you make it even, if it is possible for you. And if not, split over it, and remove it: and treat it with what preceded its mention, until it is healed.
And sometimes this fracture of the hip is bound, besides which splints are added to it, as we said in the assistance and the arm.
However, in the restoration of it, thus fractured, lameness does not happen to the sick person. And if it is restored alone, besides which the other leg is applied to it, then it is necessary that the one to whom it belongs should always limp.
And know that the hip is bound in fifty days, or a little is added, or a little is lessened: all that is according to the diversity of complexions and the other dispositions.
Concerning the restoration of a fractured kneecap.
Know that a fractured kneecap rarely happens, but it often suffers from attrition.
If however a fracture does happen to it, then it is only either a fissure or a hole in its parts. And it is with a wound, and without a wound. And it is supported by all of it with sensation.
And its restoration is such that what is broken from its parts is equalized with fingers, until it is brought together and joined as much as possible by equalization and ease and fabrication.
Then apply a plaster of sesame flour, or mill flour, with egg white: and place a round pad on top of it, if you need it: and bind it above with suitable bandages to it.
Then consider all the dispositions that we have narrated in the other fractures: such as a hot abscess, and similar to it: so that you oppose every accident with what is appropriate to it, until it is healed.
Concerning the restoration of a fractured leg.
Know that the leg has two bones, one of which is thick and is called the Cruris bone, and the other thin, and is called the Focile bone.
And both kinds of fractures occur in the same way as in the two bones of the arm; and on account of that, its restoration is done, just as the restoration of the arm, equally, and the operation is the same.
If both bones are broken at the same time, the leg is turned in all directions.
And if the thinner bone is broken, the leg is turned towards the front.
And if the thicker bone is broken, you will find it lower; and it is from among those that are not hidden from you.
Then administer extension, and equalisation, and the binding of splints according to that equally.
However, if the fracture of the leg is a foul fracture, having much pus, your extension should be less and your easing gentler.
And in the leg there is something more in the operation than in the arm; and it is that, when you equalize the splints and complete all your operation, then take two strips of pinewood, which are used in the making of beams which are placed under planks, and are made from the palm's leaflets; and choose those with a small thickness, and let them not be thin; and let their length be according to the length of the leg from the knee downwards.
Then wrap each one in two folds along its length. And place one part of the leg on one side, and the other on the other side; and let them be from the knee down to the foot.
Then tie the two strips in three places from the two ends and the middle. For when constriction is forbidden, the leg bends to the right and left, and stands in a good position.
And now indeed let a cradle of wood be administered along the length of the leg, and let the leg be placed in it, so that it may preserve it from movement.
And it is very necessary, that you do this in a fracture when there is a wound properly with it.
