Then consider the leg each day, and strive with that final care from the abscess, or inflammation, or other remaining ailments. If therefore anything of those happens, then apply to it with that which is necessary, until it is healed, if God wills.
Of the fracture of the bones of the Foot and Toes.
A fracture of the heel bone indeed does not happen completely.
However, a fracture sometimes happens to the bones of the foot, and a fracture also rarely happens to the toes; and it happens to them only by attrition in many dispositions.
If a fracture of the bones of the foot happens, and you see those bones rising up against each other, then the infirm person should place their foot on prepared ground, as if walking.
Then stand, and place your foot on that which is raised from those bones; afterward press, or tread upon it, and straighten it until it returns to its place.
Then place a plaster, and linen on top; and put a small board beneath the sole of the foot, having two flat ends.
Then bind it with a suitable bandage, after you have wrapped it with cloths, and other things it needs.
When therefore three or four days have passed, then loosen the bandage. For you will find the bones straightened, whether they were broken or dislocated. Therefore, the operation is on them, as you see.
If however some fingers are broken, then restore them and straighten them according to what I have told you in the restoration of the fingers of the hand. Then place a splint along the length of the finger, and let it be slightly wider than it; then place under the foot this board which I have told you, and bind it with a suitable bandage.
If however what is broken of the bones is two, or three, or more, then place on each finger a splint of its own size, wrapped in linen cloth; and bind the board at the sole of the foot; and let it stand upon one end of the board outside the sole of the foot, so that it holds with good retention.
And it befits you, that you not forget, to consider all that I have said to you in the remaining fractures from accidents that we have spoken of; and apply to every accident that which is appropriate.
Concerning the fracture of the vulva of a woman, and the breastbone, and the penis of a man.
When the vulva of a woman is broken, then make her sit square. Then incline her a little towards her back, and let her be held back.
Then let the midwife fill her vulva with wool, until it fills it; and let it become like a sphere in the vulva from it.
Then let the woman be shaken, and raise her back gradually. For that wool will come out, until it becomes like a sphere before the gate of the vulva, and the broken opening returns.
Then place a cushion upon her back. Therefore, when she wishes to urinate, remove the wool with ease, then let her urinate; and return it according to the manner in which you first introduced it; then let her return to her initial cushion.
Let her do this for seven days, or thereabouts, for the opening itself is restored.
And if you wish, take the bladder of a sheep, and tie a reed cannula over its opening, and introduce the entire bladder into her vulva. Then blow into the cannula with force, until the bladder inflates within the vulva, for the fracture returns.
Then let it be filled with wool, and let it stand for some days, according to what we have described, until it is healed.
And when the bone of the pubic region of a man or woman breaks, then administer in its restoration, and its equalization, that which we have described in the case of the sacral bone.
And the rectum is not hidden in these nearby fractures, which rarely occur, especially to one who has some skill or experience.
And understand my book well, for the fracture itself signifies to you in many situations the right way in its restoration, and its tightening: understand therefore.
In the penis of a man, when it breaks, take a goose's throat and insert it into it. Then place upon it a bandage of cloth, and let it be wrapped, and let it be left for three days, or about that time, and it will heal, if God wills.
Concerning the restoration of a fractured bone, when it is with a wound.
It is fitting for one who has a fracture with a wound, especially if it is large, such as the hip bone, or the femur, or similar to it, that you hasten and immediately phlebotomize him: if the conditions for phlebotomy are evident to you, as we have already stated.
If the wound is flowing blood, then it is necessary that you hasten to cauterize it, so that you powder it with crushed zegi: if another is not present for you.
Then begin to restore the fracture on that same day: and do not postpone it, if an abscess does not arise.
If, however, a hot abscess arises, then postpone its restoration until the ninth day, until the abscess subsides. And do not approach him on the third and fourth days at all, for you would cause him to suffer ill accidents.
If however the broken bone is prominent above the skin, then it is necessary that you try to reduce it, and to align it with your hands with ease and slight extension.
If, however, its extension and alignment with your hands are not prepared, then reduce it with this instrument. And the instrument is made of iron, whose length is the quantity of seven or eight fingers, and its width is according to the quantity of the wound. And for that, it is necessary for the physician to take three or four of them, according to the quantity needed for the treatment of every type of fracture.
And let it be round, and let it have a small thickness so that it does not double upon compression upon it during the operation, and let it have sharp ends, with a margin at its extremities. And let its upper part be of a thickness from the middle to the lower part.
And this is its form.
In this operation, its pointed curved end must be placed upon the prominent bone end, and drive it with it in a single motion, until the bone returns and becomes aligned.
Therefore, strive to align the fractured ends together.
If its fractured ends are delicate and the instrument cannot properly grasp them, then it is necessary to cut the end of that bone until the instrument can grasp it.
However, if you cannot reduce them at all with what we have described, then cut it with one of the cutting instruments we mentioned, or saw it with a saw in any way possible for you.
Then scrape away whatever remains on the bone of roughness and small pieces.
Therefore, when the bone is reduced, and the weak person after its reduction finds severe, painful injury, then know that the bone has not returned to its natural place.
So do that. For you confer great help to the sick person, when your restoration of the bone is completed.
Then immerse the cloth in astringent, black wine. And properly if it is in summer. And do not place on the wound a cauterized thing, nor anything in which there is oil, so that it may not cause putrefaction and corruption in it.
Then administer the splints at the hour of completion of your restoration of the bone. And leave the wound uncovered, so that you make an incision with scissors in the wrappings according to the size of the wound.
And beware with every precaution not to bind the wound with the fracture. For many times foolish physicians have done this: and they have caused to befall upon their sick either death, or corruption, or zuchem, that is, pus. And let your ligature be soft, gentle, different from the ligatures of the other fractures.
If the wound is bad, or if the wound is large, and you fear some of the bad accidents which we have mentioned, and you find pain in the suspended place: then you should not place splints upon it. And make for them wrappings from hard cloths in the place of splints, and bind it with them.
When, however, it is after one day, or two days, and you see the wound already beginning to generate pus in it: then remove from above it the cloth which you have placed upon it with wine. Then administer bandages and ointments with which it is customary for us to treat wounds: such as the tetrapharmacon ointment, and similar ones.
And it is fitting for you to loosen the bandage. And consider the wound every day morning and evening, until it is consolidated and healed.
And it is necessary that the limb be prepared in such a way that pus runs from it downwards with ease.
If many days pass over a wound, and it is not consolidated, nor is pus separated from it: then know that there are small bone splinters there. Therefore it is necessary that you investigate the wound with probes. As many of those splinters as are separated, remove them and extract them. And those of them that are not separated, and pierce the limb, and cause pain to occur, attempt to cut them, and remove them in every way possible to you.
If a wound suffers from a zch, or ekileten, that is, festering, or another sort of corruption and putrefaction, then it is necessary that you apply to each of them with what is suitable for it from the treatment, of which the account preceded in its chapter.
And in that which my discourse must take hold, and to which you should apply your attention when a bone is broken and protrudes from a limb, such as the bone of the hip, and the kneecap, and similar ones from the large limbs, it is that you should not presume to pull it back, for often death arises from that, but rather leave it alone until it putrefies.
For perhaps it will fall away by itself after twenty days, or thirty.
Then therefore treat the wound, if it seems good to you; and if not, leave it alone.
Concerning the treatment of a swelling at the site of a fracture.
This swelling often happens at the site of a fracture's healing. Especially those near the joints; and the shape of the limb is disfigured by it. And perhaps it prevents the limb from its natural function.
Therefore, if the swelling is recent, administer medicines that have astringent properties, such as aloe, frankincense, myrrh, sarcocolla, acacia, and similar ones.
So, take some of these, or all of them: and mix them with astringent wine, or with egg white, or with vinegar; and place them on the swelling in linen, and bind them tightly over it.
And leave the binding on, and do not loosen it for many days. Then loosen it and apply another, until the swelling is removed.
Or bind a lead sheet firmly over it. For lead has the property of removing all that is feared from the limbs.
However, if the swelling has already become hard, and is tight, and necessity quickly compels one to remove it: then split it from above, and cut off the protruding excess: or shave it with some razors until it is removed: and treat it until it is healed.
Concerning the treatment when it is restored, and the limb remains after it, in a state not natural to it.
When a broken bone is restored, and the limb remains afterward thin, weak: then it is not so except for many reasons. Some of which are: a multitude of loosening and binding the ligament not as it should; or due to excessive tightening of the ligaments, until the nourishment is prevented from reaching the limb; or due to excessive application of fomentation at an inappropriate time; or due to a lack of blood in the body of the weak person, and their weakness.
And the treatment for this is to nourish the weak person, and to make his body tender, until blood multiplies in him. And administer a bath. And introduce joy and gladness upon him.
Then apply pitch to the limb; so that the pitch attracts much nourishment to it. And diligently apply a poultice of it with lukewarm water, until the nourishment flows, and it returns to its natural form.
Concerning the treatment of fractured bones when they are restored crookedly.
When, after the healing of a limb that has already been restored, crookedness, and a prominence of the fractured bone, or a knotting, occurs, and the form of the limb is disfigured thereby, yet the limb is not hindered from its natural function, then you should not heed the opinion of one who thinks that the limb should be broken again.
And already there have been many foolish physicians and bone-setters who did that in our land.
And indeed this operation is very reprehensible, leading to death, or great fear, so that it is mortal.
But if the lameness and knotting are recent, then it is necessary to bathe it in lukewarm water in which softening herbs have been boiled, such as mallow leaves and its root, and melilot, and similar things. And a plaster should be made with a softening plaster, such as a well-made diachylon.
Or take the mucilage of mallow root, and mix it with chicken fat, or sesame oil: and plaster it with that.
Or take fat figs, and rub them with pigeon dung, and similar things to them called from among the medicines that lessen consolidation.
And sometimes the knotting is loosened with diligent, old, and gentle rubbing, which is done with wet hands. And movement of the limb should be administered to every part at all hours.
However, if twisting has already occurred, and become strong, and turned into stone, and the need has arisen to treat it with iron: then it is necessary that the upper part of it be cut, and the continuity of the bone be loosened, and that what is superfluous from the knotting or the bone be cut with thin knives; and ease be given to it with care and diligence.
Concerning the discourse on Dislocation.
Dislocation is the departure of any joint from its place. Therefore it is prevented from its movement, and the figure of the limb is disfigured. And it causes grievous pains and severe injuries to the afflicted.
Therefore, when any dislocation occurs, one must hasten to its restoration; nor should one delay it at all.
For if it is delayed, the place will become inflamed, and perhaps the reduction of the dislocation will be lost with it.
Therefore, it should not be moved or extended during its inflammation. Because it often causes spasms and damaging pains.
But when that happens, then it is necessary to hasten to bleed the patient. Then let it be left until the inflammation subsides.
Then the limb should be bathed with warm water and oil. Then it should be reduced gently.
And every limb should be treated with whatever will cause its return to its proper place.
I have indeed already arranged the chapters on dislocation, even as it preceded in fractures, from the upper part of the body down to its lower part.
Concerning the treatment of dislocation of the lower jaw.
The two jaws are rarely dislocated unless infrequently.
And dislocation of both occurs according to one of two ways. For either they are moved from their places by a small removal, and are softened a little. Or they are dislocated by a complete dislocation, until they are softened to the chest area, until the sick person's saliva runs, and he is unable to hold it, nor able to close his mouth, and his tongue is involved with speech.
When, however, its dislocation is small, then it returns in several positions by itself with few efforts.
If however it is a complete, entire dislocation, then it is necessary that its reduction be administered quickly, and not delayed at all: and it is so that the attendant holds the sick person's head; and the physician inserts the thumb of one hand into the root of the dislocated jaw between his mouth, if the dislocation is on one side. Or he inserts two of his thumbs simultaneously, if the dislocation is from two sides. And the remaining fingers of his hands should be outside, with which he equalizes.
And command the sick person to cleanse his jaws, and to make them free to eat on every side. And the physician should adjust the jaw, and push the jaw until it returns to its place.
If its reduction is difficult, especially if both jaws are simultaneously affected: then apply vaporization with hot water and oil, until the reduction of both becomes easy: and do not delay the reduction of both at all, as we have said.
When therefore they return and are equalized, and the sick person's mouth is closed, and they are not softened: then place on them pads of cloth, with a cerate made from wax and rose oil.
Then it is to be bound with ease with soft bandages.
And let the sick person's sleep be on their back, and let their head be supported between two pillows, so that it does not move to the right or left, nor attempt, or be forced to chew anything: indeed, let their food be administered by sipping, gently, until when the pains are removed, and the jaw is bound: then let them eat what they wish, and let it be administered easily.
Let them not exert themselves over the operation of their mouth in eating and drinking, and yawning, until the jaw is bound, and healed.
If the reduction of the two jaws becomes difficult, when they are dislocated at some time, and do not return to their places: then fevers and continual sweating often result from this, and perhaps the patient's bowels are loosened, and perhaps pure bile is vomited.
Therefore, when you see this, then know that it is destruction, and often the one to whom this happens dies within ten days.
Concerning the reduction of the dislocation of the collarbone and the extremity of the humerus.
The collarbone indeed does not become dislocated from the inner side, because of its continuation with the chest. And it sometimes dislocates outwards, and this is evident to the senses.
And its restoration is that the sick person lies on their back, and extends their arms. Then grasp the place firmly with your hand, for it will return.
Then place a plaster and cushions over it, and bind it tightly.
However, its extremities that follow the elbow and continue with it do not dislocate, except rarely. If it dislocates on any day, then it must be reduced and straightened as we have said, and as is prepared for you.
Then place a plaster and cushions over it, and a bandage. And command the sick person to adhere to tranquility and rest until he is healed.
And with this same treatment, the humerus extremity is reduced when it also separates from its place.
Concerning the reduction of the dislocation of the Humerus.
Know that the Humerus is not dislocated except in three ways.
One of which is that it is dislocated to the side of the armpit towards the lower part. The second is that it is dislocated towards the side of the chest. And perhaps it is dislocated to the upper part of the Humerus: and that happens rarely.
And it is not dislocated backwards, due to the scapula. And it is not dislocated forwards, due to the nerves.
And very often it is dislocated and comes out downwards towards the titillicum: especially in those whose flesh is scarce. Because it emerges in them quickly, and enters quickly.
However, in those whose flesh is abundant, it is the opposite, namely because it emerges with difficulty, and enters with difficulty.
And perhaps it happens to some men through a blow or fall, and the humerus becomes inflamed with a hot abscess, and it is thought to be due to that, because it has already been dislocated.
Therefore, it is necessary that you test it, until you stand on the certainty of it, and then proceed in its treatment.
And dislocation is recognized when it is turned downwards towards the tickle: and distinguish between a dislocated humerus and a healthy humerus. For you find a clear difference among them, and you find a concavity in the head of the humerus, and below the tickle, at the touch, the head of the humerus is as if it were an egg: and the sick person cannot bring their hand up to their ear, nor move it in all ways.
And similarly, if it is dislocated towards the chest, or upwards: then you find a clear touch, nor is it hidden.
And indeed, a simple reduction of this dislocation is possible when it is recent, or if the patient is a child.
And its reduction is that the attendant should raise his hand upwards. Then place your thumbs under its tickle, and raise the joint with strength upwards to its place: and let the attendant raise his hand and extend it upwards. Then let him place it below, for it returns quickly indeed.
However, if it does not return by what we have said, and many days have passed since the dislocation occurred, then it is necessary that the patient be bathed in warm water, and an embrocation that softens and soothes be administered, such as if the roots of marshmallow, fenugreek, and melilot are boiled in water, and administered.
Then let the patient be laid on his back: and let a sphere and wool be placed under its tickle, which should be equal between gentleness and constriction.
Then let the physician place his heel on the sphere, and lift the head of the shoulder with force, and pull the patient's hand downwards.
And if you wish, reduce it according to this method: and it is that you should have present a man longer than the patient, and let him stand on the side, and let him insert his shoulder under the patient's tickle: and lift the tickle upwards, until the patient is suspended in the air. And another attendant should pull the patient's hand downwards toward his belly. However, if the patient is light, then it is necessary that something else be hung with him to weigh him down; for the dislocation returns immediately.
And it is also reduced according to another method: and it is that a long piece of wood should be fixed in the ground, whose head should be round in shape, like the sleeve of a mortar: which should not be thick, nor thin. Then let soft cloths be placed over the head of the wood beneath the patient, and let the patient stand along the length of the wood.
Then let his hand be extended downwards on one side, and let his body be extended on the other side with strength; for the joint returns to its place quickly.
If his reduction is difficult by all that we have said, then use this treatment: and it is that you should take wood whose length is the size of two arms, and its width the size of four fingers, and its thickness the size of two fingers: which should have a round head, so that its entry into the depth of the joint may be eased, according to this form.
Then soft cloths are tied over the round head, so that the wood does not injure the patient.
Then place the wood under the patient's joint, so that the hand or arm is extended downwards over the wood; and the wood is tied to the support and arm and hand's extremity.
Then place the arm over a step of a ladder in width, and extend the hand downwards, and let the rest of the body be suspended on the other side. The joint enters immediately.
When therefore its entry is completed in whichever manner is possible, then it is necessary that you place a sphere of equal size made of wool under the joint. Then place the prepared plaster from the mill's powder with frankincense and egg white over the entire shoulder, as it revolves from above.
Then bind with a suitable bandage under the joint, and let the ligament be wrapped around the plaster from above, and let his hand be tied to his neck, and let it be released, and let not his hand be moved for seven days.
And it is necessary that little food be given to the sick person until the limb is strengthened, because that is more rapid for its healing.
Then, after seven or five days, a larger one is placed, and it is exercised with movement. If therefore it is strengthened and does not soften, then it is now healed.
If however the joint dislocates repeatedly, due to the moisture that befalls it, or for some other reason, then it is necessary that a cautery with three openings be administered to it, according to what has been stated in the chapter on cautery.
If all this is done, and the ligament is loosened after seven days, and the joint is not strengthened, and the plaster and constriction are applied repeatedly to it, and it is not strengthened, and it falls, and cannot be raised to its higher position, then know that its nerves which are to the head of the humerus are now cut, or stretched, or softened; and know that the joint will never be fixed in its place.
However, the reduction of a dislocation towards the chest and nipple, or to the rear, is done with pressing and stretching with the hands, until it returns, and further constriction and treatment are administered to it until it is healed.
If after healing, heaviness occurs in the limb, and slowness in its movement, then the sick person should use the bath repeatedly until that heaviness is lessened, and it returns to its original nature.
On the treatment of elbow dislocation.
Know that the elbow joint is dislocated with difficulty, and similarly returns with difficulty; and indeed it is dislocated to all sides, and especially to the front, or to the back.
And its dislocation is not hidden from you, because it is readily apparent to the eye and to the touch, regardless of the manner in which it is dislocated. And when you join the dislocated elbow with the healthy one, that is made manifest to you. For the joint is hollowed out, and cannot double the arm, nor touch its shoulder with it.
And it is necessary that you hasten and reduce the dislocation immediately, before a hot abscess occurs to it.
For if a hot abscess occurs to it, its reduction becomes difficult, and perhaps it will not be healed at all, especially when it is to the rear. For it is worse than all kinds of dislocations, and causes more severe pain to them, and often death descends with it.
And its restoration, when it is of those that can be reduced, is that his hand should be extended with two hands, and his arm stretched out, and the physician's hands should be above and below the elbow, and he should push the joint with the thumbs of his hands together, or with the base of his palm, until it returns to its place.
Or indeed the dislocation is to the front: then it returns thus, that he should double his hand with his hand, until he strikes with the base of his palm the elbow that is dislocated.
If the dislocation does not yield to reduction, then administer a very strong, forceful extension: and it is that two assistants should extend the arm; and two assistants should also hold it so that it is not moved during the extension.
Then the arm is to be revolved in every direction after a long doubled cloth, or a broad bandage, has been wrapped around it.
And when the physician touches the joint with his hand, let him anoint it with oil, so that it may be among those things that aid in the lubrication of the joint with ease. Then he should drive the joint with a forceful thrust, until it returns.
And after its reduction, it is necessary to place a plaster upon it, in which there is astringency and desiccation with egg white: and a decent bandage should be applied. And let the arm be suspended from the patient's neck, and leave it for a few days.
Then let it be released. If therefore the joint is strengthened in its place, then loosen the bandage from it and leave it.
And if you see the joint not being properly bound, then reapply the plaster and bandage, and leave it for a few days, until it is bound. Then loosen it.
If however heaviness occurs to it after the reduction of the joint, and the disposition is in motion: then administer moistening in bathing, and gentle friction, and compression, until it is lined, or place upon the joint fat of a ram's buttock. Then it is to be bandaged, and leave it upon it day and night. Then remove it, and immerse it in a bath. When therefore it sweats, then rub the joint with moderate friction. Then repeat upon it the aleliati in one, and second, and third time with the entrance of the bath, until it is soothed.
And if you wish, place upon it a warm, moist cow dung, warmed with butter, and bind over it.
Do that several times. For it is soothed and returns to its proper state.
Concerning the treatment of dislocation of Almahassem.
The joint between the arm and hand, the Mahassem, is often dislocated.
And the reduction of its dislocation is easy. Unlike the other joints. However, speed is necessary in reducing its dislocation in the hour in which it is dislocated, before the place is suppurated; or a hot abscess occurs in it.
And the reduction of its dislocation is to place the weak hand's Mahassem on a board, and let the assistant extend his hand; and let the physician place his palm on the prominence of the joint, and push it until it returns.
However, it is necessary to consider if the palm has already receded to the back of the hand: then the weak man shall place the back of his hand on the board at the extension and reduction; and if the dislocation is proceeding towards the back of the hand, then let the position of his hand be internally on the board, that is, the inner part of his hand be placed on the board, so that the hand of the physician falls upon the very prominence of the joint.
If therefore it returns immediately, it is good; and if not, then bind it with a soothing plaster for the abscess; and leave it, and do not repeat it.
For if it does not tolerate it, nor can you bring about its reduction after days have passed, but the joint remains according to its twisting: and it does not harm the sick person anything except that the hand softens, and cannot grasp anything; then know that the nerves are severed, or worn out.
Therefore there is no skill in it, except that it be bound with a cautery near its fall. And perhaps that does not help anything.
When therefore Almahassem does not return, then place on it the plaster, which we have described. Then it shall be bound, and left for five days: afterwards it shall be unbound and left, and the hand exercised.
If however its movement is deprived, and something serious occurs in it: then gently warm it with heated water, and friction repeatedly, until it is eased.
Concerning the treatment of the dislocation of the fingers.
When a finger is dislocated in any direction, or from the finger to the back of the palm, or to its front: then extend the finger, and drive the dislocation with your thumbs until it returns.
Then support the head of the finger, and suspend it towards the part to which it is dislocated, and let it be for two days.
Then suspend it, and extend it, until it is straightened on that day of its own.
When therefore it is night, bind it according to the account itself: and do not cease to loosen it during the day: and exercise it with movement: and bind it at night.
Do that for several days, until it is strengthened.
And similarly do with it, if it is dislocated towards the palm of the hand: and bind it to that same part, and do your first operation with it, until it is healed.
And similarly do with it when it is dislocated to the other parts.
Concerning the treatment of the dislocation of the spinal column.
When it happens to someone that the spinal column or the neck is completely dislocated, or many vertebrae are removed from their places, then there is no cure for it, because death quickly comes upon the sick person.
And a sign of this is that the sick person's excretion comes out involuntarily, and they cannot hold it; and often some of his limbs become soft, or his two legs, or his two arms, or one of them; or a single vertebra is removed from its place.
For often it is removed, which happens often in the removal of them to four parts.
Therefore, that which is removed backwards is called Gibbosity.
However, the treatment for it is to consider, if the gibbosity occurred from childhood, then there is no cure for it, nor is there any healing from it at all.
However, in those cases which occur from an accident, or a blow, or similar things to these, the ancients have already described methods of treatment in lengthy discourse. Of which much does not return with profit.
I, however, have abbreviated it, from which little excuses much of it, from my proximity to its intention: and by my exposition of it, and I formed its instrument in a different way from that in which they showed it and explained it.
Therefore, I say that in gibbosity which occurs on the anterior part, there is no remedy, nor healing from it.
And similarly in that which happens on both sides as well.
And it is not cured from them, unless that which happens in the back properly, with what I relate: and it is, that the infirm person be stretched out upon their face upon an even platform near a wall: and that moist straw be spread under it, so that their chest is not injured.
Then let a straight piece of wood be fixed in a hole in the ground, at the end of the platform, roughly at the level of his head; and another piece of wood roughly at the level of his feet at the other end of the platform; and let an assistant hold the wood, and let it not be firmly fixed in the hole. And another assistant hold the other.
Then wrap around the head of the sick person, and under their armpits, a soft, strong bandage; and extend the end of the bandage to the wood which is by his head; and tie it to it.
Then tighten another bandage above his armpits, and above his knees, and at his hips. Then gather all the bandages together, and tie them to the other piece of wood that is by his feet.
Then let each assistant pull on the bandage towards the piece of wood, and do not remove the two pieces of wood from their positions in which they are fixed; but let them not be firmly secured, as we have said.
And let the physician place his palms on the vertebra with force, until it returns to its place; or let him place a board upon it. Then he ties his feet to the board, until it returns to its place.
If, however, it does not return with this treatment, then take a board whose length is approximately three arms' lengths. And make a hollow in the wall of which we said was near the sick person, a place for the end of the board to enter. Then place the middle of the board over the vertebra. And let the physician place his feet on the other end. And pull tightly until the vertebra is constricted and returns to its place.
Then if you wish, operate with a turning tool held in your hand: and it is, that you fix in the ground near the head of the sick person, at the furthest point of the footstool, two pieces of wood, whose length is that of one arm: and near his head, at the furthest point of the footstool, two pieces of wood.
And let the length between each piece of wood be one palm: and now a hole has been made in every place, through which the turning tool runs: and let each piece of wood be firmly secured in the ground, so that it does not move at all.
And let a round piece of wood be inserted; and the turning tool is in which the ligament is revolved in the two holes of the two pieces of wood.
And at its extremity is a hole, in which a piece of wood is fixed, whose length is a palm together with that which is revolved: and in the other two pieces of wood it is similarly situated.
Then tighten the ligaments which you have tied on the chest of the sick person with the turning tool which is at his head; and the ligaments which you have tied on his legs with the turning tool which is at his feet.
Then let a servant stand at each turning tool, who may turn his hand with the cord, with which the turning tool is revolved: and the physician shall adjust the Spondyle, according to what we have said.
This is the form of the turning tool, which follows.
Then, after the Spondyle returns and the place is adjusted, it is necessary to apply a drying plaster with egg white. Then, a bandage. Then place a piece of wood, whose width is three fingers, or approximately that, over the plaster. And its length the amount that covers the hump and the healthy part of the Spondyle.
Then tie it with the ligature which we said. And let the sick person use subtle nourishment until he is healed.
If something of eminence remains in the place at the end of the cure, then it is necessary that medicines that soften and soothe be administered, with the administration of the board which we described for a long time. And now a lead plate is administered into it.
And sometimes eminence occurs in the last vertebra of the spine, and it is thought to be caused by dislocation, and that is the bone which is now prominent. Therefore, it is not fitting to presume upon him with this medicine; for perhaps it will cause death.
Concerning the cure of a dislocated shoulder.
Know that dislocation of the shoulder joint, and the humerus joint, happens only as a complete dislocation; nor does anything happen to them that happens to other joints from a slight displacement and concavity.
And indeed the shoulder joint is dislocated according to four parts. That is, because it is dislocated forward and backward.
And it is mostly dislocated inward; and it is little dislocated forward and backward.
And the treatment of its inward dislocation is this: because when you connect the healthy leg of the sick person with the sick leg, it is longer; and the knee of the sick person is more prominent than that of the healthy one, and the sick person cannot bend his foot at the groin; and there is a place which follows the groin, suppurated with a hot abscess, because the head of the thigh has already reached there.
And the sign of him who has a dislocation outward is that the occurrences are opposite to these occurrences.
And the sign of him who has a dislocation forward is that he extends the healthy leg to its limit; however, he does not bend it without pain in the knee; and if he attempts to walk, he cannot move it forward; and his urine is retained, and his groin is suppurated, and upon walking, his heel is striking the ground.
And the sign of him who has a dislocation backward is that he does not extend his knee, nor can he bend it before bending the groin; and his leg is also shorter than the other; and the groin is softened, and the head of the thigh is at the place of the ilium.
The reduction of these kinds of dislocations, however, is that you should consider whether the dislocation is ancient, which has already grown old with the one who has it. Then if you administer its reduction, and it remains according to its position, then there is no cure for it at all. Therefore, it is not necessary to make a complete presumption about it.
However, in the one whose dislocation is recent, and is of one of the four kinds of dislocation, then hasten to this, to wish to join the joint, and to extend it inwardly and outwardly, and to move it to the right and left: for perhaps it returns, and does not need another treatment.
If, however, it does not return in this way, then it is necessary that you prepare a strong assistant, and let him extend his leg from the lower part; either with his hands, or with a bandage that is tied above his leg above the knee; and let another assistant extend his hand from above, so that he inserts his hands under his toes. Then he should tighten with a gentle bandage over the root of the thigh, and hold the end of the other bandage.
And let its extension be either from the anterior part of the groin to the part of the crotch, or from the posterior to the part of the back.
And let the extension of all of them be at one time, until the sick person is raised with his body from the ground, and remains hanging.
And this species of extension is a common species of the four species.
If therefore the dislocation returns with what we have said, it is good; and if not, then it is necessary for every species that I will say about its own cure.
In its own reduction, indeed, when the dislocation is inward, it is necessary that the sick person be laid on his healthy side. Then he should place a bandage over the root of the thigh, in that which is between the head of the thigh, and the place that is below the groin. Then extend the bandage upwards from the groin to the higher part of the body, to the part of the pubis.
Then let another strong servant take his arms, and reduce the thick part of the sick person's thigh, and extend it outward with a violent extension. For now it returns to its place.
And this method is easier than the other methods of treating this limb.
However, if it resists you, and does not submit to you in this treatment at all: then it is necessary that the two feet of the sick person be tied together with a strong, gentle bandage, over the two heels and over the two knees; and let the length of each be from its counterpart in quantity of four fingers; and let the sick leg be extended more than the other by a quantity of two fingers.
Then let the sick person be suspended over a wooden beam, which is in the house; and let its length from the ground be the quantity of two arms.
Then command a strong young man to hold the head of the thigh; and let another young man hold the sick person; and let another young man, holding the thigh with strength, urge it; for the joint returns to its place quickly.
However, in its own return, when the dislocation is outward, it is necessary that the sick person lie upon a raised platform, according to what we have narrated concerning the humpbacked person. And a bandage should be tied around his/her sick leg and across his/her chest.
Then place two pieces of wood, one at his/her feet, and the other at his/her head. Then place a further piece of wood in the middle of the platform, firmly fixed; upon which let moist cloths be wrapped, so that it does not injure the sick person; and let the wood be between his/her thighs, so that he/she is not pulled downwards by extension.
Then let every attendant pull on his/her side, and let the physician equalize the reduction with his/her hand.
If therefore it yields to the return, it will be good.
And if not, place it upon the board itself, and press it, just as we said in the case of a hump, evenly. However, it is necessary that the sick person lie on his/her healthy side.
And when the dislocation is towards the front, then it is necessary that the sick person's leg be pulled quickly; and it is according to this same account upon a raised platform; and let the physician place the palm of his right hand upon the sick person's groin. Then he constricts it with the other hand, and with that constriction places it stretched downwards towards the knee.
And if the dislocation is towards the rear: then it is not necessary that the sick person be stretched downwards and be raised from the ground; rather, it is necessary that he be placed upon a hard object, as is necessary, as is also done if his hip is dislocated outwards, according to what we said about his lying on both hips: and it is upon his face, and the ligaments stretched: according to what we said recently.
And it is necessary that compression be administered with a board also upon the place to which the joint emerges, when the reduction is completed as is necessary.
And the sign of its return is not hidden from you: and it is, that you extend the two legs of the sick person; and when you see them equal, and the sick person tightens his leg and expands it without excuse, then know that the limb has now returned, as is necessary; then join the two thighs and apply a plaster, and bind it with a bandage, that is, a roller; so that the hip is not moved to any parts; and let the sick person adhere to rest for three days or four.
Then loosen the bandage and the plaster, and compare the leg with the other; then if you see them equal in condition, then know that the dislocation is now firm; therefore, release the sick person to walk.
And if you see any softening, then return and plaster it, and bind it according to your first binding; and let it be also for three days.
Then loosen it and make him/her slow in walking on both sides for several days, until he/she is well strengthened.
Concerning the dislocation of the Knee.
The knee is dislocated in three ways. It is dislocated outwards, inwards, and downwards, namely backwards.
And it is not dislocated forwards.
And a sign of its dislocation is that you should instruct the sick person to apply his leg to his thigh. If however it does not adhere to the thigh: then know that the knee is dislocated.
And the restoration of all the ways of its dislocation is that you should make the sick person sit upright, so that he already extends his leg if he can over it, and adheres your back to his face: and place his foot between your feet.
Then make his knee adhere with your palms, and straighten them between your fingers over his knee. Then apply with your palm the two sides of his knee with strength. And another attendant should extend his foot, until the knee returns to its place.
And the sign of its return is that the leg adheres to the thigh with ease, not with effort.
Then bandage it, and make the leg adhere to the thigh. Then tie them together with a bandage for three days, or four. Then loosen them.
And let the infirm person not use walking for some days, until he is healed.
If, however, its reduction is refused by you, then administer a strong extension with bandages, whose description preceded in the treatment of a dislocation, until it returns.
Concerning the treatment of the dislocation of the Calcaneus.
The calcaneus sometimes is removed by a small displacement, and sometimes is dislocated completely. And its dislocation occurs either inward or outward.
And the sign of its dislocation is that you see the Calcaneus swollen, protruding towards the side to which it is dislocated.
The treatment for its displacement is easy repositioning: and it is that it should be extended gently with the hands, and adjusted until it returns.
In treating it, however, when it is dislocated completely, it is necessary that you have the sick person sit upright, and a strong assistant hold him firmly from the back of his back, in the middle of it.
Then, hold his foot with your right hand from its upper part, and with your left hand from the lower part of his foot at the ankle joint.
Then pull the foot towards you with your right hand. Then with the left two times.
Then extend with the left, and push the foot's chest with the right towards the leg without effort.
Do that twice, as we have described.
Then push the foot's chest towards the leg a third time, and you pull the heel.
If therefore it returns in one time, or in two according to this method, and you see the foot is straightened, it is good; and if not, then repeat the operation on it, because it returns.
If however its reduction is prevented by what we have described, then make the sick person lie on his back on the ground, and fix a firmly planted peg in the ground, so that it falls between his thighs; and you have now wrapped a cloth over it, so that it does not harm the sick person.
Then let the attendant take his thigh; afterward let another attendant extend the foot, either with their hands or with a bandage that ties around the ankle. Then let each attendant extend against the extension of their counterpart; and let the peg stand between the sick person's two thighs, holding him so that his body is not pulled downwards during the extension.
Then the physician should straighten the dislocation with his hands, and another attendant should hold the healthy leg downwards. For the dislocation returns with speed.
Therefore, when the dislocation returns, and its return is clear to you, then place a plaster and a bandage, and bind it firmly with bandages, and tie the foot to the leg with a bandage.
And you must be careful or wary of the nerves which are above the posterior heel, so that the bandage is not too tight on them, and injures them.
Then leave it for two days, or three. If the bandage softens, tighten it; then loosen it on the third or fourth day; and forbid the weak person from walking for forty days. If, however, he attempts to walk before that space of time, there is no certainty that the dislocation will not be loosened on him, and be corrupted, and not receive healing afterwards.
If a hot abscess occurs to him, then it is necessary that you administer to its sedation what our narrative previously described elsewhere regarding treatment, and a fomentation, until it is removed.
Concerning the dislocation of the toes of the foot, and its treatment.
It is necessary that what is dislocated from them be brought back into place with a slight extension, without difficulty; indeed, it is easy.
If however the dislocation is in some of the dorsal knots of the foot, then it is necessary that the sick person place their foot on a level place on the ground, or on a board; and it should be standing as if walking.
Then you should stand and place your foot upon that which protrudes from those joints. Then press it with your foot with force, until it returns and you see it is now in its proper place, and no protrusion appears.
Then place a board under the sole of his foot, one that will encompass the entire foot, and have two ends. Then bind it with a suitable bandage for three days. Then loosen it and keep him from walking for many days, until it is strong and secure from relapse.
Concerning the types of dislocation with wound or fracture.
When something of that sort occurs, and you attempt its cure and restoration, death often results.  Because of this, it is not fitting for anyone to presume to treat such a case unless they are prudent in the art, long-practiced, merciful, pious, slow, not bold, nor presumptuous.  And they should use medicines at the outset that soothe only hot abscesses, and leave the sick person to divine judgment. Unless you see, or hope for, health from ruin with the gentleness of illness, and in which something of hope appears. Therefore, attempt its reduction immediately at the beginning of the matter, before a hot abscess occurs.
If, therefore, the limb returns as we desire, then administer a regimen that soothes the abscess, and treat the wound with whatever suitable drying ointments are appropriate.
If, however, the dislocation is with a fracture, and separate bone protrusions occur, then attempt their removal, and do in this what we have said in simpler illnesses from what has preceded concerning their locations.
And beware according to your ability, and guard yourself from entering the path of fear, according to my previous instruction to you.
For that is better for you before your Creator, and more enduring for your glory, and more beneficial to your body among those in your time.
